“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonparell
Illustrated Newspaper.
“Tlie Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians. —The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The direct election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency.
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “ nobility. ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude froni its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now ,all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land', wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushraan of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maonland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The'Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading ,matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to, if you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bool making is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

NOW IS THE ACCEPTED TIME.
(FOR THE PUBLIC.)
I have decided —
Set my mind to a purpose.
My heart trembles,
I strain and steady it,
I am not afraid.
My whole life I devote to the chosen project;
If I fail I shall die despised as a worthless man,
As one who wasted his wealth of energy and time.
Though my youth, maturity, nerve, and love shall
have been spent for Man,
Men will refer to me before children as an instance
of folly, even of vice.
’Twill be a bitter return for a life passed in hard
struggling
Against inward weakness and outward opposition.
My Love even now rejects me,
She will not credit me with a noble intention ;
She doubts, yet loves me, making consolation —
I will pass on to work,
Never forgetting my love, but never returning to
her.
My heart shrinks from the prospect of loneliness
and poverty in old age,
From the grief of being misunderstood, despised,
or pitied by those I would did love me,
My flesh shrinks from the prospect as a child from
fire,
But I am not afraid, and push forward.
I will do the life.
If sincere in wish and effort success will be
obtained ;
If insincere, no success can come.
Life is a discipline.
My life to train and test me, not you,
To improve me, not you.
What for ? I cannot tell—
Your life does the same for you.
I have determined and feel happy immediately,
Relieved from doubt and wavering between
wishes ;
With one wish choice is settled quickly
Without possibility of repentance.
Comrade ! come, fix your purpose,
If you would be a rogue, be a rogue,
Or a good man, be a good man,
And bold to the end.
N.S.W.
ANONYMOUS.
Pentridge-prison officials have lately been
going round the Vic. Government departments
taking from civil-servants orders for convict-made
goods. They now send a man with a bootmaker’s
measure sizing government feet for prison-made
boots.
Buffalo-hunters Cahill, Johnson and Flynn
tell wonderful stories of the deposits of the eggs
of wild in certain portions of the Northern
Territory. The swamps in the neighbourhood of
the alligators abound in the eggs of wild geese.
So numerous are they that they serve as the staple
food of both whites and blacks in the neighbour-
hood, and are actually cooked and fed to the
horses—the latter devouring their novel food with
relish.
Wills made in the couleur-de-rose boom-
days read strangely now. The big man of Dayles-
ford district counted his money in ’92, and dis-
tributed it generously all round, remembering his
poor sisters and his cousins and his aunts, and his
servants and the church. A farm and live-stock
were left to his only child. The whole foots up to
over ,£30,000. Then the daughter, who has been
given but little cash so far, is to step in for what-
ever’s left. But the estate is now only valued
at £22,871.
Her Grgcious has cordially invited the
young Khedive of Egypt to hang up his fez in the
hall at Windsor Castle when he visits Hingland.
V.R. grows diplomatic in her old age. Years
ago the other Khedive —the one who supported a
private ballet at the British bondholders’ expense
—came to London on the off-chance of being taken
in at Buckingham Palace, or some other royal
lodging-house. Nobody in particular met him at
the railway-station, so His Nibs drove off with his
luggage to Claridge’s hotel. At this crisis the
earless Earl of Dudley came to the rescue of the
Empire’s glory and took the potentate in.
At the North London Police-court recently
a damaged husband asked for a judicial separation
from his wife, on the ground that she habitually
jabbed him in the countenance with broken lamp
glasses and other sharp things. The poor wretch
was dotted with “ nasty cuts,” and “ much sym-
pathy ” was expressed for him by the Bench. The
Law, however, didn’t admit of granting a sum-
mary separation to a husband under these circum-
stances, the privilege being reserved for ladies only.
So the Bench committed the wife to prison for a
week, and she went off to do her sentence with
the openly avowed intention of jabbing her hus-
band some more.when she returned to his happy
home. |
“Jack” Stretch, the new Dean of Bal-
larat, deserves his promotion. One Sunday even-
ing a large Newfoundland dog found his way into
church while his reverence was preaching. Im-
mediately the attention of the assemblage was
riveted on that dog. The verger was called upon
to turn him out, with no effect. The dog made no
account of that verger beyond threatening to make
a mouthful of him. The muscular “Jack” turned
up his sleeves and descended .to the fray. Bruno
yielded and left. Then the victorious parson read
the congregation a lecture re people who couldn’t
keep their dogs at home. P.S. —That dog belonged
to J. Stretch.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable ),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 2, 1894.
The Collapse of “New Australia.”
For the first time since the beginning of
the ill-starred experiment the gruesome
tale of “New Australia” is now officially
laid before the public. Hitherto the infor-
mation received has come in the form of
scrappy and inconclusive letters from evicted
and prima-facie prejudiced settlers, and until
the other side of the story was heard there
was always a considerable amount of uncer-
tainty as to the real condition of affairs.
But now that Mrs. Lane, wife of the chair-
man, chief organiser and dictator of the
colony, has told the other side of the story,
there is no consolation in it. It differs from
the narratives that went before it only in the
one circumstance that it lays the entire
responsibility of the failure upon the settlers,
whereas the settlers laid the entire responsi-
bility upon Mr. Lane. As regards the
failure itself, the latest picture only differs
from the others inasmuch as it paints the
collapse in a rather more hopeless aspect
than anything which ivent before it. New
Australia is now plainly a commonplace,
squalid, ignominious disaster. It has not
succumbed to outside circumstances. So far as
can be ascertained, the emigrants found a
reasonably good climate, a splendid soil,
well-disposed and hospitable neighbours, and
a Government which was prepared to assist
them to the utmost of its ability. The great
scheme went to pieces chiefly upon the
dismally vulgar question —-who was to wash
up ? So far as the information goes, nobody
was willing to wash up, and out of that fact
the trouble arose.
w * *
The principles of “ true mateship” seem
to have gone to pieces even before the first
contingent started. The pioneers quarrelled
among themselves in the Balmain barracks,
and they quarrelled more and more on the
voyage. Their leader was deposed and set
up again ; his official proclamations were
torn down and jumped upon ; and his ukases
which seem to have been numerous and
foolish were treated with derision. A
horrible habit of holding meetings and
appointing boards and committees and sub-
committees spread among the argonauts,
and every one who wasn’t on a board or a
committee rose in revolt against every one
who was. Meanwhile, the figureheads of
the show continued to issue vast masses of
futile rules and regulations, and the ex-
pedition was governed altogether too much.
By the time it arrived in Paraguay,
it seems to have been practically re-
duced to chaos. The rear - guard is
accused of having plundered the baggage
of those who were on ahead, and of
having sold their boots and shirts for drink.
A spirit of general objection entered into
these wanderers, and when the advance-
party pitched the tents on a ridge the next
arrivals promptly rooted them up and
planted them in a swamp. The committee
decided to put up some temporary shelters
and then proceed with clearing and planting,
and the balance of the party, feeling it
necessary to sit on the committee, thereupon
decided to let the clearing and planting slide
till permanent dwellings were erected.
Rations were exchanged for native spirits,
and there were high old orgies in New
Australia. Most of the mothers withdrew
their children from school to mark their dis-
satisfaction when the jam and pickles gave
out ; and even before this happened a good
part of the educational staff had abandoned
its business and gone off on missions to the
local heathen. One brawny male invalid is
accused of having wilfully wrecked the
doctor’s thermometer, and a lady in an
interesting condition insisted upon being
confined in a saw-pit instead of in the house
provided for her. Also the man with the
magic lantern went round giving exhibitions
to the Indians, but flatly refused to let his
mates see the show on any account whatever.
Altogether there were great doings in Lane’s
unbuilt city of Anarchy, and the only differ-
ence between official and unofficial accounts
is that they apportion the blame in diverse
quarters —the rest of the story is much the
same.
The riots and frenzy which are raging in
New Australia are, however, matters which
mainly concern the settlers themselves.
The people of old Australia are interested in
a totally different aspect of the question.
According to the semi-official statement now
furnished, Lane early realised that the
painted glories of his ideal community were
not going to be realised—which is a delicate
way or stating that he discovered the original
settlers had "been deluded by gross and
wicked misrepresentations, and by pretences
so glaringly false as to be utterly inexcus-
able. Therefore, instructions were sent to
the agents in Australia to represent things
strictly as they were—to leave the garish
ideal and set down to hard, dismal reality,
so that no further immigrants would set out
without a definite knowledge of what lay
before them. If these instructions were
really given they were not followed to any
great extent. The men who remained
behind had not Lane’s eloquence, nor his
gift of painting gaudy impossibilities and
making them look like realities, but in their
own humble way at least some of them
seem to have kept on booming the exploded
bubble all they knew. The people of Aus-
tralia only guessed that it was exploded from
the letters of the evicted settlers, and the
efforts of the local officials seemed to be
mainly directed to explaining away these
ghastly revelations, and keeping up the old,
poetic story about the orange-groves, and
the guitars, and the moonshine upon the
river, and all the rest of the alleged gorgeous-
ness. If these local officials really under-
stood New Australia as it was —if they knew
the truth, or anything dimly approaching the
truth, about that place of chronic desolation
—then a cruel hoax was perpetrated by
somebody on the more recent emigrants.
Lane’s agents in Australia may not have
actually circulated any false or deceptive
statements, but, so far as is known, they did
not publicly withdraw a single one of their
leader’s gaudy assertions of a year or so ago,
which assertions were mainly responsible for
the wreck that has ensued.
Only a few days ago advices from Maori-
land announced that the local organiser was
so much impressed with the gorgeous
“ Temple of Truth at Christchurch that he
had obtained plans of the structure with a
view to erecting a similar building in New
Australia. This circumstance was, in itself,
a good advertisement for the settlement, but
if the place is as Mrs. Lane describes it,
there is no demand for temples there, and
never will be, and the proceeding was
mere garish romance, and nothing more.
Mrs. Lane herself is understood to have
returned to Australia to recruit a large
number of girls for the colony, which she
now describes as practically a wreck, and but
for the fact that Lane’s secession almost
compelled her to put forth a statement in
his defence, there is no visible reason to
believe that the old delusion would not have
been officially kept up to the bitter end. In
fact, so far as concerns Lane and his own
little party of secessionists, the same old
story is being told and the same old delusion
is starting afresh. Whoever else may go
wrong, this little knot of believers preserve
the “ true spirit of mateship,” and are solid
on the underlying influences of piety, and
have no yearning after strange drinks and
Guarani maidens, and will never quarrel or
burst up or secede any more. It is the same
tale which was told when the original expe-
dition started, and which was told a second
time when the first batch of malcontents was
expelled, and which will keep on being told
after each disruption until there is nothing
of New Australia left.
• • •
As matters now stand, one-third, or there-
abouts, of the expedition has been evicted,
and is wandering about penniless in a strange
and hungry land. Another third has gone
out into the wilderness to start afresh ; and
the balance remains on the original settle-
ment, where it is left without spiritual
guidance, or any high ideal, or any true
spirit of mateship, so that it will most likely
fall away in search of strange liquors and
strange women like its outcast brethren.
Strange liquors and Indian girls have, seem-
ingly, done more than anything else to
aggravate the “natural cussedness” of the ex-
pedition. In this respect it has proved itself
just like every other expedition, and the
result is only what might reasonably have
been expected. Man will drink, and gamble,
and prowl round to the back-doors of
the dusky daughters of the land till
the end of time, and every scheme
that fails to take these facts into con-
sideration is certain to come to grief. The
constitution of New Australia was based
on the assumption that it is possible to create
a community where every person is sober,
moral, religious, and full of a holy yearning
for self-sacrifice, and the collapse came about
because there is no such community outside
heaven, and never will be. If Lane was
aware of this at the beginning, he perpe-
trated on his followers about the meanest
“ have ” of the century ; if he was not aware
of it his ignorance of human nature was so
vast as to be past reasoning about.
Another Rumpus in the Fold.
Some ministers of religion read and think, the
consequence being that they get into serious
trouble with a number of their brethren who
do neither. The Moderator of the Presbyterian
General Assembly in New South Wales, Rev.
Mr. Maclnnes, sought to shake off some of his
theological swaddling-clothes, a few weeks
back, and created quite a panic in the minds of
those of his hearers who like to be swaddled.
The curtain was hastily rung down on the
scene, and the orchestra played an orthodox
tune as vigorously as possible to divert public
attention, but the incident had the effect of
augmenting the suspicions entertained by
cynical outsiders that a good many parsons are
beset with the same doubts which haunt those
who have never professed any standard of
faith, A similar comedy has just been per-
formed in Queensland. In a luckless moment,
the local Presbyterians elected as their
Moderator the Rev. G. D. Buchanan, a man
of brains and pluck, whose later years have
been passed in an arduous endeavour to find a
modus vivendi under the crushing weight of
the Westminster Confession. Mr. Buchanan
is a Scotch - American who was known,
years ago, as a powerful preacher in
a Baltimore church. Blessed or cursed
—as the reader may take it —with a rest-
less spirit, which would not permit of his
spending his energies peacefully as a fashion-
able pulpiteer, he forsook Baltimore at a call
from Victoria. After he had been heard a few
times and proved his mettle, the Cairns Memo-
rial Church, East Melbourne, was built as much
in his honour as in that of old Dr. Cairns,
whose name it bears, and, while he remained in
charge, the congregation which assembled
Sunday after Sunday was larger than collected
in most Melbourne churches. Great was the
consternation when he announced, one day,
that he was bent on forsaking this snug pen in
the ecclesiastical fold and wandering away to
pastures new in the vicinity of Brisbane. When
the clergy and elders of the Queensland Presby-
terian Assembly made this erratic genius
Moderator, they might have expected him to
prove less “ moderate ” than was consonant
with a due regard for conventional placidity.
But his inaugural address outpaced their wildest
imaginings, and set some of them wondering
whether, after all, Calvin had not lived in vain,
and the entire scheme of election, fore-ordina-
tion and damnation become a played-out farce.
« # •
Whether it was artfulness on Mr. Buchanan’s
part, or bland simplicity, he fired his bomb-
shell, labelled “The New Theology,” in as im-
perturbable a fashion as though he were merely
striking a safety - match. Alluding to the
Maclnnes turmoil, above mentioned, he re-
marked that “ the storm in a teacup, which had
occurred in New South Wales recently, was
rather amusing. The Moderator said nothing
which Robertson Smith did not say twenty or
thirty years ago, but it deemed that the New
South Wales people only just now discovered it.”
In all likelihood Mr. Buchanan suspected
that the Queensland people were equally be-
nighted, and, foreseeing an outbreak, adopted
this ingenious method of letting them know be-
forehand what he thought of them. Anyhow,
he gave them a shock. His “ new theology ”
surrenders the infallibility of the Bible, the
basis of dogma, and the authority of creeds. It
dismisses hell as cavalierly as Sir Richard
Bethel was said to have done in his famous
judgment, and puts evolution, or perpetual de-
velopment, in the place of Genesis. “Men had
discovered,” Mr. Buchanan affirmed, “ that
theology, as a science, must progress as other
sciences had done.” To do this it had to be
grounded in evolution, and the “ new theolo-
gians ” were “ above all things evolutionists.’
“They believed in spiritual certitude, but
not in an infallible Church or Bible.” The
Bible was studied in accordance with the
“historical method” which set you learn-
ing how the men who wrote it were
circumstanced and what they meant by it. This
was what Robertson Smith was ostracised for
doing in the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica,” but,
said Mr. Buchanan, “When I was in Edin-
burgh recently Professor Rainy remarked to
me, ‘ Robertson Smith to-day would not need
to leave his chair in that University.’” The
Edinburgh University has got abreast of
Robertson Smith, but there are good folks in
Bananaland who have not, and when they
heard the Moderator of the Presbyterian As-
sembly denouncing dogma, portraying the
darkened condition of the Fathers of the Church,
and pointing the finger of scorn at a material
hell, they plaintively asked themselves where
they stood, and marvelled if the Rock of Ages
had been partitioned off for suburban allot-
ments.
# • #
The daily press, which is always eager to
attack anyone who appears to be letting go the
“conventionalities,” solemnly affirmed in a
ponderous leader: “It is not within our know-
ledge that the official representatives of any of
the great Protestant churches in Australia have
listened to an exposition of religious beliefs so
startlingly different from The authoritative ami
orthodox statements of Christian doctrine.”
Mr. Buchanan was warned that he had
“slipped many or most of the old theological
anchors of the Presbyterian Church, or, for
the matter of that, of all the leading Protestant
churches.” Others took up the cry yet more
vehemently, and, worst of all, the Secularist
lecturer, Wallace Nelson, reviewed the deliver-
ance from his platform and contended that “in
all essential particulars the Moderator’s address
was a statement of the philosophical deism of
Thomas Paine.” That was too much. The
Assembly deemed it necessary to take some
action, and, accordingly, a reverend brother was
put up, at a later sitting, to ask the Moderator
whether he had anything further to say, so that
“no one could consider him an upholder of the
deism of Tom Paine.” Thus impelled, the
Moderator pointed out a number of striking
differences between his views and those of the
bugbear of the churches, and set down, in
categorical shape, the articles of his belief.
Touching Gehenna, he remarked: “It seemed
as though it had been a revelation to some
people in this community that there was no
such thing as a material hell of fire. He did
not think there was an intelligent minister
among them who had ever believed there was
such a thing.” There were members of the
Assembly who doubted if some of the views set
forth by the Moderator were “ in accordance
with the Scriptures or the standards of the
church,” and who yearned to discuss them.
Bub, as in the Sydney gathering on a similar
occasion, the wiser heads were shaken deprecat-
ingly, and there was a general chorus of “ Drop
it, oh ! please, drop it.” So they dropped it.
* # *
Not, however, before the Moderator—a little
alarmed, perhaps, at the storm in his own tea-
cup—had averred and re-asserted that there
was not one of the confessions of the Church
which, if put to him in a proper way, he was
not prepared to swallow. How he would
reconcile the doctrines of election, predestina
tion and eternal damnation with the law oi
evolution, inspired by Infinite Love, he was,
fortunately for himself, not called upon to
explain. It is not a pleasant spectacle to see a
capable modern mind vainly wriggling under
the pressure of ancient dogmas and forced to
own his belief in them in the same breath with
which he curses them. But this is the penalty
which subscription to creeds involves. It is
only when students of these oracular questions
discuss them in the pages of some philosophical
magazine that the world gains the benefit of
free utterance without a glance over the
shoulder as to the fate of a pittance or the loss
of a livelihood.
PLAIN ENGLISH.

Courage yet, ray brother or my sister !
ee p on— Liberty is to be subserved whatever
occurs ; „ , •
That is nothing that is quelled by one or two
failures, or any number of failures,
Q r py the indifference or ingratitude of the
people, of by any unfaithfulness,
Or the show of the tushes of power, soldiers,
cannon, penal statutes.
What we believe in waits latent forever through
all the continents,
Invites no one, promises nothing, sits in calm-
ness and light, is positive, and composed,
knows no discouragement,
Waiting patiently, waiting its time.
The battle rages with many a loud alarm and
frequent advance and retreat,
The infidel triumphs, or supposes he triumphs,
The prison, scaffold, garrote, handcuffs, iron
necklace, and lead-balls do their work,
The named and unnamed heroes pass to other
spheres,
The great speakers and writers are exiled, they
fie sick in distant lands,
The cause is asleep, the strongest throats [are
choked with their own blood,
The young men droop their eyelashes toward
the ground when they meet;
But for all this Liberty has not gone out of the
place, nor the infidel entered into full pos-
session.
When Liberty goes out of a place it is not the
first to go, nor the second or third to go ;
It waits for all the rest to go—it is the last.
When there are no more memories of heroes
and martyrs,
And when all life and all the souls of men and
women are discharged from any part of the
earth,
Then only shall Liberty or the idea of Liberty be
discharged from that part of the earth.
And the infidel come into full possession.
Then courage, European revolter, revoltress !
For till all ceases neither must you cease.
I do not know what you are for (I do not know
what I am for myself, nor what anything
is for),
But I will search carefully for it even in being
foiled,
In defeat, poverty, misconception, imprison-
ment—for they, too, are great.
Did we think victory great ?
So it is—but now it seems to me, when it can-
not be helped, that defeat is great,
And that death and dismay are great.
—Walt Whitman.
The Absentee’s Corpse.
The British Government, it is said, has resolved
to levy probate duty upon the colonial pro-
perty of all dead loyal subjects who, in their
lifetime, chose to get domiciled in the dear old
mother country. Samuel Wilson may die in
Australia, and be buried here to boot, but. since
he elected to domicile in' Beaconsfield’s
house and other British places, he will have to
enrich the British Government with a tithe of all
he possessed. Xu short, the Chancellor of
Britain’s Exchequer proposes to abolish the
absurd fiction that Samuel and his class are
Australian by deciding that they die wholly
and solely Hinglish. The Bulletin admires
the simple honesty of this legislation, and
trusts that ere Samuel pays his next visit
to his Victorian sheep - runs there will
he a British bill providing for Samuel’s
taxation as an absentee during the time he stays
away. Meanwhile, Australia, having now got
its lesson from “ ’Ome,” may possibly see fit to
take it to heart. In effect Harcourt puts his
hand to his mouth and whispers across the
briny deep—“ You see what we intend to do
with these people when they die. Make the
best of them Before the melancholy event
happens.” The duty of Australia is to grip
Time by the forelock and tax Samuel and Co.
before they pass hence. Great Britain is not
usurping any of our rights in this matter.
Australia has still the power to call the
absentee by the name of the land he
fattens upon, and charge him an annual
fee for living abroad. Britain will charge
him for domicile after his decease ; all
’things considered, Britain shows her modera-
tion in hot wanting to impose an extra
special rate. . The Bulletin has much printed
authority for stating that Queen Victoria loves
us dearly, therefore she must necessarily, be
very cross indeed with people of the Wilson
persuasion who give us constant cause for coni-
plaint. That by the way. The man who is
ueither British nor Australian has been set
’apart by the “’Ome”. Government as a fair
mark for taxation at both ends, and any . Bill
which will encourage him to have only one end,
that end being here, is a Bill that cannbt he
passed an hour too soon.
Timeo Danaos.
Premier Forrest, of Westralia, has “ declared
the Government- policy,” which is to bribe the
electorates with a new loan of £1,500,000, to
make railways from Southern Cross to Cool-
ganlie, from Mullevva to Cue, from Dounybrook
to the Budge town tinlields, and to the Collie
coalfields. The golden shower is also to extend
the Fremantle harbour-works, and to give all
the goldfields water-supply and telegraph-lines;
to establish a land-bank, a mint, cold-storage
works and markets at Perth, and to construct
“roads and bridges and other important
works.” It is also proposed to give every man
over 21 £3 a week for life, with free drinks,
two suits of clothes a year, a house to live
in, a black boy to wait on him, and
the run of his toothpick at a Government
restaurant. “The speech, was received with
the utmost enthusiasm.” No doubt; and the
enthusiasm will continue as long as the borrowed
money lasts, when the enthusiasts will pack up
their traps and quit, leaving the residents,
tethered to the soil, to pay the interest. Premier
Forrest’s proposal would be received with very
modified enthusiasm in the eastern province,
where people are beginning to realise the mean-
ing of taxation for absentees; but the
westerners are young and verdant, and will
probably be dissatisfied unless they, too, pay
through the nose for experience. The crafty
Mr. Forrest “doesn’t believe in land-
taxation”—not he. In fact, he “sees no
necessity for additional taxation ” of any
kind. Taxation means restitution of the
plunder with which the first families have
gorged themselves; to borrow means more
plunder—and those who are nearest the source
will carry most away. The Bulletin advises
the workers of Westralia to get rid of the
Forrest yoke at all costs, and return a radical
Parliament at the June elections. Then, if
they want to borrow, their own representatives
can borrow just as well as the Forrests, and
there will be greater certainty that the money
is devoted to avowed and legitimate objects.
Any man that now grabs for the golden bait is
just as big a donkey as the donkey who races
for carrots on a stick; and, like the donkey,
all he will get of the carrots is the sight and
smell.
The Queensland Labour-party.
Editor Bulletin, —The Labour - party in
Queensland, like its big brother of New South
Wales, is fast qualifying for the knacker’s yard.
It cannot stand corn, and its recent election
triumphs have turned its head. Nothing can
be more absurd than to represent Townsville
and Ipswich as exclusive victories for the A.L.F.
The success of Ogden and Wilkinson was
largely due to the dislike the small-fry business
man has to MTlwraithism, and to the very
natural repugnance of the R.C. to vote for a
man whose only saint is .King William, and
who thinks the Boyne the greatest battle of
all time. There are a thousand good proofs
that, except in the mining districts, the
Labour-party cannot stand alone. Yet at the
present moment it makes no distinction be-
tween its sympathisers and its enemies, and,
indeed, makes no secret of the fact that it
regards with more favour MTlwraith’s pro-
nounced antagonism than Lilley’s qualified
support. The recent deliverances of the
Browns and the Reids have practically des-
troyed that alliance between the Labour-party
and the “ Whigs,” which produced so much
good last session, and blew the gaff on so many
Ministerial jobs. In the coming session the
Labour-party, constituting 25 per cent, of the
House, is likely to be the only Opposition, for
its last year’s allies, finding themselves regarded
at best as merely unprofitable servants,
will take their despised support elsewhere,
and drift infallibly into MTlwraithism, or
indifferentism. In these circumstances, the
session promises to be the paradise of the
jobber and the syndicate!’, and though the
Labour-party may yell, their protests will be
closured in the House and stifled in the news-
papers. Had the alliance continued there was
a decent prospect that “One-man-one-vote”
would become law this year —and even the
most pig-headed unionist must see that this
reform is a necessary prelude to “Socialism in
our time ” —but under the altered conditions,
for which the Labour-party is alone responsible,
that reform is impossible. In its place we
are likely to have an Elective Upper House on
a property basis, which will make “ Labour in
politics” a'negligible quantity for years to
come.— Demos.
Dear Bulletin, —Will you allow a chappy t°
express his annual surprise and disgust at th«
free-born Australian custom of “ keeping-up 1
the Queen’s Birthday, an event which excites
no display of “ loyal feeling ” in that lady’s own
particular country. Your boasted Democracy
becomes a huge joke on May 24, and again on
November 9, when, for some dark, pitiable
reason, you publicly exult over the fact of
Albert Edward’s nativity. You scoff at the
Fat Man who deprecates anything that is “ un-
English.” May I swear, with all my heart and
soul, at the un-Englishness of Australia’s de-
votion to the royal persons of “ Wales” and
his mother ? In London, the alleged head-
quarters of British grovel, May 24 is neither a
holiday nor an event of the least public interest,
whilstNovernber 9 is merely remarkable as the
occasion for a Lord Mayor’s Show. Probably
not one in every three of the city’s residents, or
visitors up from the country, know that the
Coming K. was born on that date. In pro-
vincial towns it is the local mayor’s day, else-
where it passes quite unnoticed. Albert
Edward’s tailor and perfumer and bootmaker,
&c., burn a gopd deal of gas at night in compli-
ment to their royal customer, but there the
public rejoicing terminates, even in London.
The Queen’s Birthday, I believe, is also
honoured by a dozen shopkeepers, but not
with much enthusiasm; seeing that she buys’
verv few goods off them, These things
being thus, where is the ghost of an
excuse for Australia’s flunkey outbreaks.,?
Why does. an. entire, continent deliberately
create a tradition of loyalty out of its own
blasted inner consciousness ?, You may answer
that a number of early Hiuglish settlers, full of
stupid Hiuglish reverence for crumbling insti-
tutions, persuaded themselves that these pre-
cious birthdays were .great Hiuglish festivals,
and that young Australia is now glad to take
the holidays as they come without considering
Queen or A.E. in the matter. The explanation
is not satisfactory. Some of the birthday
entertainments are greasy with “ loyalty,” as
at the Melb. Exhibition, for instance, where
Miss Somebody recited a certain ill-constructed
letter written by the Queen some 40 years ago.
Young Australia seems to have a stomach for
such muck as this. Again the natives begin to
muster in force at birthday levees, to mark
their aggressive love for the monarch that is
and the monarch who is expected to come next.
These ghastly devices for forming class distinc-
tions would fizzle out but for the assistance
of the public holiday. Men are requested to
cease work and play at loyalty, and the game
of shaking hands with the Governor is set apart
for the “ well-dressed ” class of the community.
Have you ever considered the educational in-
fluence of Birthday levees, dear Bulletin ?
They breathe a spirit of aristocracy into the
nostrils of the retired publican, who is proud
to think that no seedily-dressed person would
be allowed the same privilege as himself. By
virtue of his dress-suit the ex-poison-vendor is
able to go through a form of making obeisance
to the Queen (the Governor acts for Queen),
whereas an equally “loyal”subject, attempting
to enter the vice-regal presence in a brown
tweed suit, would be shoved off the premises
by a policeman. Your Australian Birthday
grovels are, to my mind, a much more per-
nicious influence than the Court functions of
poor old Hingland, where “ loyalty ” is not
for ever being rubbed into the public by Her
Majesty’s Government. There, now ! —Yours,
English.

along Miss Sutherland’s charity work, on th e
ground that she never insisted that the infant®
beuefited should have a working knowledge o*
the Shorter Catechism and a saving faith in
John Knox, are beginning to realise the fury
of a woman scorned. Miss S. has made some
remarkably plain statements about fathers of
the kirk, and offers to point out the houses of
ill-fame of which they are landlords. Surely it
is easy to rebut this scandalous charge by
pointing out that, on the recent testimony of a
London city missionary, the slums from which
Wales draws his rents are the vilest and
filthiest in the latter-day Babylon. Or, if a
still more distinguished precedent be needed, it
may be pointed out that the Calvinistic deity
itself draws a substantial revenue of glory
from the existence of a hell full of the most
abandoned characters.
Professor Threlfall writes to S.M. Herald
bemoaning the retrenchment of Government
endowment to Sydney University, and swells
the number of students as far as possible while
reducing the apparent cost of their training.
The number of graduates, and not the number
of students, is the real test of University work,
since some who are entered on the books do not
attend the classes, and nearly all who do attend
stay for three years or more, being thus counted
thrice over in the figures which the alarmed
Professor marshals. Much more to the point
is Mr. Philip Moses, who states that to
produce the 120 graduates of last year it took
12 professors at £9OO a year each, I at £BOO, 1
at £750,2 at £5OO, 3at £400,5 at £350, lat £3OO,
12 at £250, and no less than sixty others at
smaller salaries. That makes 97 professors in
all, or very nearly one professor to every
graduate ! ! And these professors, in addition
to their salaries, divide £2455 per annum in
“ fees.” Mungo Macallum, the recent author
of a book as dead as its subject, draws £619 ;
somebody else gets £439, and so on. Theodore
Gurnev, professor of mathematics, appears
to get £l5O in salary, but his fees are £625
additional; another mathematical professor,
Mr. Newham, gets £4OO in salary, and £316
additional in fees. It would be interesting to
know exactly what labour is performed in
return for these stipends. All this proves to
the hilt The Bulletin’s contention that the
State which endows must also control.
Pending the unrestricted opening of the Uni-
versity, except by qualification, the wouldj-be
reformers of that gaudy institution should
agitate for an extension of the evening-lectures
principle. At present, so far as Sydney “shop”
is concerned, it only embraces the B.A. course,
while in Melbourne no evening lectures are
given at all. A year or two back a determined
demand for evening courses resulted in Melb.
University authorities undertaking to provide
lectures if £lOOO a year was granted by the
State or guaranteed by the students. Munro
refused, on behalf of the State, and properly,
too, seeing that the country mainly supports
the institution, while the intending students
couldn’t see why they should pay more than
the day undergrads.
Prof. Morris is on his way towards being a
saviour of his adopted country. Nothing is so
likely to check the flow of that British capital
which has been unsinewing us, as an appeal to
the English public for money for the relief of
Australian distress. Since Francis Adams
died and the Investors’ Review has sheathed
its bowie, there is no one to tell them the truth
in England directly ; Prof. Morris has done so
indirectly. No one is so dense as the common-
sense English capitalist. Facts will cannon
against his brain for a quarter of a century
without effect, while the spent ball of such a
rumour pop-gun will knock him so completely
that he will sheer off Australia for a decade
after, and shoot his millions into a South
American Republic. Such will be the effect, it
is probable and devoutly to be hoped, of
Morris’s Times’ letter. And such an effect
will be the postponement of our bond-slavery,
and of the day, which all true jingoes pray for,
when the affairs of Victoria will be administered
under the control of a receiver of the English
Chancery Division.
Mayor Manning, of Sydney, the knight with
the beautiful London Journal legs, was re-
quested, the other day, to take the chair at a
Town Hall meeting called to consider the case
of Montgomery and Williams, the condemned
burglars; and refused, on the ground that he
was not prepared to commit himself to an
expression of opinion on the subject. This dis-
ingenuous reply does a lot more credit to Mr.
Manning’s legs than to his soul. No one asked
or desired that he should express any opinion
—in fact, no one seriously suspected him of
having one. He was simply requested to be a
nice-looking figurehead on the platform, to
announce that Mr. Smith would now propose
the first resolution, and, later on, that
Mr. Jones would propose the second
resolution; and do other routine business.
Therefore, even if his dislike to expressing an
opinion was so deep-rooted as to amount to a
disease, his excuse had no more to do with the
matter on hand than it had with the transit of
Venus. Curiously enough, however, when the
person concerned is some influential ex-politi-
cian (who used to be, and, perhaps, still is, a
power in the constituency Mr. Manning aspires
to represent), and who is serving a very short
and well-deserved sentence for a very glaring,
sneaky crime, Mr. Manning’s dislike to ex-
pressing an opinion doesn’t trouble him in the
least. Only a few days ago he appeared as an
eloquent partisan—not as a mere passive figure-
head —in a deputation which demanded the
release of Francis Abigail. On behalf of that
hypocritical swindler, who sharpened his cold-
chisel on the Tables of the Law and made a
jemmy of the Word of God —who pranced with
a Bible in his hand at the head of sectarian
processions, and who waved the red rag of
religious bigotry on all possible occasions, and
lived on his assumption of cast-iron integrity,
and was utterly merciless in Parliament and .in
the courts towards all others whose honesty was
even remotely suspected —Mayor Manning was
enthusiastic, and his regret at the “cruelty”
of Abigail’s very brief sentence was almost
pathetic. Tho difference between his views
on these two occasions creates a suspicion that
Mr. Manning’s beautiful legs have led him
astray. Montgomery and W illiams, at their
worst, were smaller and far less dangerous
criminals than Abigail. In their case, Mr.
Manning wasn’t required to commit himself—
it was even an open question whether the meet-
ing would commit itself, for it was simply
convened to consider the matter, and it was
quite as open to it to decide that the two
burglars should be hanged as to come to the
opposite conclusion. Mr. Manning, after three
or four years as Mayor, apparently chooses to
ignore the duties of his office rather than em-
barrass in any way the callous set of people to
whose coat-tails he is politically attached.
English rule in India, according to—
Sydney Telegraph.
But much has happened
since the mutiny which jus-
tifies us in regarding such
occurrences with com-
posure. . . The history
of our rule in India is a
record of continuous re-
form. Under the proclama-
tion which transferred its
government to the Crown
described as the “Magna
Charta of the Indian people”
—a policy of political jus-
tice was promulgated which
has in the main been loyally
carried out. . . Thus, on
every hand is visible abun-
dant evidence of the bene-
ficent work done in India by
the Britisli Government dur-
ing the last three decades,
and it is in contemplating
this great work, in which all
classes have had impartial
share, that we may well
question if the mysterious
markings on the mango
trees we read of to-day are
of the slightest significance.
Brisbane Courier.
India is, in fact, governed
by an alien and despotic
power. . . It may be said
that Britain has only re-
placed a bad despotism by
a better. But it is one
thing to submit to the
despotism of a native ruler,
and quite another to submit
to the despotism of an alien
power. And, bad though
the native ruler may be, in-
dolent, corrupt, careless of
human life, he yet thinks as
the people think, and swims
with the stream of their
customs and superstitions.
English rule seeks the wel-
fare of the Indian people as
we throw a bone to a dbg.
It is not too much to say
that nothing but force, ever
present and reputedly in-
vincible, can prevent mutiny
and rebellion among a
people ruled as we have
elected to rule India. , .
But peace and safety will
never be assured until the
rights of man are recognised
in the Government of India
as in all free countries.

After New South Wales Government and
Assembly have done much, and waived more,
to remedy the flaw in the Elections Act which,
purely by inadvertence, disfranchises electors
who remove from one constituency to another,
they find their action nullified by the wilful
malice of the Legislative Council, w'- ch threw
out the main clause of the Amending Bi by 18
votes to 9. Not a shred of justiff ion was
alleged for this extraordinary procedure,
which can only be prompted by a wish on the
part of the representatives of Property to debar
as many poor voters as possible from recording
their votes. If the Government insists, the
Council must give way. But the incident is
another proof of the urgent necessity for mak-
ing the Upper House representative of the jus-
tice and intelligence, not the prejudice and
stupidity, of the people.
Seven swagmen were arrested at Maryborough,
Vic., the other day for being out of work.
They were Melbourne men, they said, travelling
in search of billets. A local Solon fixed them
severely with his eye, and said they were a
formidable gang, “ and with a little burgling
knowledge would be dangerous to the com-
munity.” Six months. There was nothing
whatever to lead Dogberry to believe that the
men had any burgling knowledge, or were likely
to acquire any such knowledge, but he sent
them to gaol to protect their innocence. A
local movement is on foot to get this legal
genius ten years’ solitary confinement, because
it is widely believed that with a little homi-
cidal mania he would be dangerous to the com-
munity. He has no homicidal traits, but it ia
a fact all the same that if he had he would be
dangerous to the community, and we hope he
will get his ten years. For the same good
reason The Bulletin believes that everybody
else ought to get at least six months.
The little batch of Queen’s Birthday “honours”
which has reached Australia is another pitiful
evidence that times are bad in this country. So
many awful “revelations” have been made of
late that it is difficult now to create a knight
with any certainty that he won’t presently take
his title into the Insolvency-court, or the .dock,
or some place equally disreputable. Therefore,
a little has been done in the way of knighting a
few old or pottering mediocrities, who appear
too commonplace ever to do anything of
a surprising character, whether it be good
or bad, and having done this the Cro.wn
seems to have “ put the peg in and sworn off.”.

Mr. Thomas Smith, a shareholder in the Mer-
cantile Bank, desires silence for the future
concerning the balance-sheets of that precious
institution. “ His counsel is to let the dead
past bury its dead,” which has been the advice
of Fat Man from the outset. Gentlemen of the
T. Smith species seem incapable of understand-
ing that the Davies scandal is as much alive to-
day as it was two years ago, and will continue
to pop up in unexpected places for an indefinite
period. Neither do bank-shareholders duly
recognise their proper relations to the manage-
ment. They are the people who drew big
dividends from that same said-to-be-dead
past, and on the whole they didn’t do badly
out of the scandals. All they have now
to do is—meet their calls for the benefit
of depositors, the depositors whose money
was paid away in unearned dividends.
Bub, as showing the blind assertiveness of
shareholder Smith, he has the “cheek” to
propose a grant of £lOO to somebody out of the
Mercantile Bank assets. He ignores the patent
fact that his interest in the assets is nil, since they
will not be equal to the full payment of de-
positors. Smith is no uncommon case. In-
deed, the Law is a good deal like Smith when
it permits the shareholders in a hopelessly
insolvent concern to appoint the liquidators,
and otherwise behave as though they were all-
important personages in the winding-up pro-
cess, instead of mere background characters.
Directly a bank stops payment the shareholders
arfe sleeping partners in a bankruptcy, and
should be treated accordingly, whilst the
depositors appointed a receiver with strict in-
structions to get every available penny out of
the “ firm ” — i.e., the said shareholders. When
busted banks are dealt with in the same manner as
busted butchers or bakers, the shareholders will
begin to give a sharp eye to their own business,
and the day of Davies tentacles and Munro
clans will come to an end. Reconstruction
was primarily the work of “ enthusiastic
shareholders.”
By a late English mail come particulars of a
case strongly resembling that of Montgomery
and Williams, now under sentence of death in
Sydney. Two constables discovered three
young colliers robbing a chicken-house. A
scuffle ensued, and one constable was beaten
and kicked, and otherwise ill-treated. He was
able to go home, but some seven hours later he
was found dead in bed, and the medical
witnesses attributed his death to shock
caused by the violence to which he had
been subjected. The colliers were sentenced to
death, with a strong recommendation to mercy ;
but the sentence was almost immediately com-
muted by the Home Secretary to one of penal
servitude. Here we have a case in which the
constable actually died from his wounds ; none
of the constables assaulted by the Sydney
criminals has died or is likely to die. If Eng-
land reprieves when life is actually taken, shall
Australia hang when there is no guilt of blood-
shed ?
New South Wales Railway Commissioners grant
firms doing business to the extent of £/0,000
per annum in railway freights, three yearly
tickets with a discount of 75 per cent, on
season-ticket rates, and three free passes, also
an additional pass for each £20,000 freights in
excess of £70,000. It is easy to recognise the
principle on which a private firm acts when it
makes concessions to large purchasers or cus-
tomers doing heavy business ; but that prin-
ciple does not apply when the State acts
as a trader. To the State as a trader, the
man who does a thousand pounds’ worth of
business appeals for concessions, proportion-
ally at all events, with as much reason
as the man doing a business amounting to
a hundred times as much. As the Commis-
sioners’ regulation runs, seventy men may do
£70,000 in railway freights without any con-
cession whatever being granted them, but if one
man comes along and does all their business, that
customer will receive the concessions and privi-
leges enumerated. And - ’ yet it is admitted, as
a political axiom, that seventy men, each doing
a thousand pounds’ business, are more an object
for State encouragement than is the solitary
individual who does a seventy-thousand pounds’
business. Is the regulation, in addition to being
impolitic, an illustration of the common ten-
dency to grease the fat pig ?
Mr. Isaac Jacobs writes to Melbourne Age on
behalf of the Chosen People’s character, and
many of I.J.’s remarks are fair and to the
point. But when he asks whether “ the pawn-
broking class of Jewish money-lenders are any
worse than their Christian confreres ?” the good
man has evidently a bad case. He might as
well suggest that Chinamen are no more un-
desirable than the larrikins who herd with
them. It is even doubtful whether Mr. Jacobs
could put his finger upon one pawnshop keeper
in Melbourne who is nob a Jew by blood, and
though the business may be, as he says, fairly
conducted, from the pawnbroking point of view,
it is still a business that brings upon a Christian
the contempt of his own people. A John Jones
loses caste when he hangs three brazen balls
over his doorway, whereas an Isaac Jacobs
doesn’t yet—though the time is coming. Not all
the grand Jewish of the world can alter
that fact.

Statistics as a basis of argument figure again
on the water-drinkers’ platform. During last
year in New South Wales, 4650 persons were
convicted of drunkenness between the hours of
8 a.m. Saturday and 8 a.m. Sunday, while only
1529 were convicted for that offence between
8 a.m. Sunday and the same hour on Monday.
Therefore, argue the Templars, the Sunday
closing of hotels reduces the drunk-list from 4650
to 1529, giving us an accretion of 3121 sober
people to the community. But, first, many drink
in their own houses on Sunday, and remain in-
doors, who drink in pubs, on other days and are
caught on their way home. Second, those who
drink in pubs, on Sundays are taken better care
of by the publican, as his business safety may
be compromised by his customers getting into
the hands of the police. He accordingly either
keeps them in his house till they sober up, or
sends them home with an escort. Thirdly, of
drunkards who fall into the hands of the
police, the great majority spend all they have
on Saturday, and are thus compulsorily sober
on Sunday; and, fourthly, when the police
have locked up 4650 people for Saturday’s orgy
they haven’t left at large in New South Wales
more than two or three thousand of that class
of drinkers to operate upon on Sunday.
THAT “BACKBOUE."
Mr. Marshall Lyle, writing to S.M. Herald
re the condemned burglars Montgomery and
Williams :
The results of the abolition of| capital punishment in
other countries are worth considering. They show that
the supporters of capital punishment have not one word
they can say in excuse for themselves, unless it be that
the English race is more ferocious and brutal than the
other races of Europe. 1 will mention a few instances of
the effect following abolition in other countries.
Holland.—Capital punishment abolished Sept. 1870
(there was no execution since 1860). The statistics of
murder were as follows; —1861-9,16 murders ; 1871-9,17
murders; and this notwithstanding an average increase
In population.
Finland.—There has been no execution since 1827. The
Judge of the Court of Appeal states: “The security of
Eerson and property has not been in the least diminished
y the suspension of capital punishment. Murders are
extremely rare."
Switzerland.—ln 1874 capital punishment was abolished
by the Federal Council. In 1879 Cantons were allowed to
choose for themselves, and two or three elected to re-
instate the death penalty, but the majority decided
against its introduction.
Belgium.—No execution since 1863. In the 10 years
before 1863, 921 murders. In the 10 years after 1863, 703
murders.
Prussia.—ln decade 1869-78 484 persons sentenced to
death ; only one execution.
Portugal.—Capital punishment abolished.
Roumania.—Capital punishment abolished.
Tusoany.—No execution for mofe than 50 years.
Russia.—Capital punishment only retained for treason
and military insubordination. _
America.—Michigan, capital punishment abolished in
1847 ; Rhode Island, 1852 ; Wisconsin, 1853 ; lowa, 1872;
Maine abolished it in 1876, but restored it in 1883,
but after studying the effect of bringing the death
penalty back, in the interests of morality and to
stop crime, abolished it again in 1887. In Michigan the
statistics show that since 1847 murders have de-
creased relatively to the population 57 per cent. As to
Wisconsin, Governor Washburnb writes in 1873“ It is
20 years since the abolition of capital punishment. No
State can show greater freedom from homicidal crime.
With a population representing almost every nationality,
statistics show that crime, instead of increasing with the
growth of the State, has actually diminished.” Of lowa,
Senator Jessup writes in 1876:—“Murder in the first
degree has not increased, but has for four years decreased.
Previous to the repeal of the old law, there was one mur-
der for every 800,000 people. For the four years since
abolition there has been one in every 1,200,000, There is
more Lynch law where the gallows is retained.”
Editor Bulletin, “L.M.’s” letter about
Mildura does not go to the bottom
of the difficulty. The Mildura settler cries
about broken promises and plants dying
for want of water, and he will have to
cry. No irrigation scheme that involves
pumping, on anything like a large scale, can
possibly pay. Every acre of land needs at least
53,000 cubic feet of water a year, and if it has
to be lifted only 40 feet you have an expendi-
ture of 60,000 foot tons of power per
acre annually. Calculate what that comes
to for hundreds of thousands of acres,
and you have a lively notion of the cost for
coal, &c., to say nothing of first cost, interest,
and maintenance of works. A squatter can
use his washpool engine and pump to irrigate a
few acres of cultivation. He has the pumping
tackle, anyhow ; firewood costs nothing but
carting, and that can be done any time. He
must find work for his engine-driver between
shearings. It is the case of the Egyptian fellah
who waters his little garden with a Persian
wheel when he has nothing else to do. The
irrigation problem in Australia is yet unsolved,
and will remain so till an engineer with an
original mind takes it in hand without regard
to precedent.—Y.R.

“ Lady Windermere’s Fan” only lastedi
one week at Sydney Lyceum. Three days of
“Mrs. Tanquefay” was announced to follow, “by
special requestand “The Cabinet Minister,” by
Pinero, is advertised for next Saturday. This
piece, unlike the three last Lyceum productions,,
doesn’t profess to teach any good lesson, and is
simply howling mirth, naked and undisguised.
Which, perhaps, is a good thing, for the dress circle
has been so much impressed by “Mrs. Tanqueray”
and other recent productions that it is seriously
thinking of going forth in its low-neoked dress
upon a mission to the heathen.

That ancient horse-drama “The Flying:
Scud” will be staged at Sydney Her Majesty’s
next Saturday, and the preparations have been
undertaken in a most conscientious spirit. All the
magnificence which the old play will contain is to
be put into it, and it is confidently expected that
by Saturday it will look almost new again.
*****
A respectable little company, consisting
largely of Frank Oates, took possession of Sydney
Royal last week, and ran a brief season, the'
principal item on the programme being that fear-
some literary effort, “As Large as Life.” There
are many features about this alleged comedy that
are too horrible to be seriously discussed, and
where it isn’t horrible it is generally incoherent,
and where it isn’t incoherent it is mildly and
gently idiotic, and the rest of the time it is
maudlin. Therefore, The Bulletin hereby drops
it softly into the tomb and trusts that it will
never again be heard of. Its one distinction is
that it is really the worst and most aggravating
comedy this paper remembers.
** * *
Sydney Alhambra is making a plain,
but honest crust out of the variety show industry,,
under the management of Mr. Geo. Gibbons.
The recently reorganised Federal Minstrels also'
survive at the School of Arts, and are doing a
steadily improving business up to time of writing.
Other side-shows consist mostly of a lecture in the'
Y.M.O.A. Hall upon “An Artist’s Wanderings
in Samoa,” in which the eternal Robert Louis
Stevenson will be dragged in by his hair to pro-
voke more execration from a weary community
in a much-exhausted land.
** * *
“The Shamrock and the Rose,” having:
flourished in Sydney, may repeat that miracle at
Melbourne Royal. Cheap prices cover much'
dramatic sin, especially where Ireland is con-
cerned, but if a reduced tariff can cloak the male-
factions of Charles Holloway & 00. in this
instance, poor old Melbourne must have pawned
its brain-pan. Some years ago a rampageous
stranger, named O’Grady, came from Britain to
produce his own Irish narratives upon these same
boards, and public opinion cast him forth. He
cursed the local critics, shook a Celtic fist at the
gallery, and went growling back to his lair. Yet
the works of O’Grady were intellectual bean-
feasts compared with “The Shamrock and the
Rose,” in which the one tolerable character— a
comic process-server with a grimy countenance —i B
borrowed from O’Grady aforesaid. The villain of
this piece seems intended to deter mankind from
crime. He is wicked under difficulties. As soon
as he comes to the front with a base design, he
receives a blow between the eyes, but in the
next act everybody ignores the ill-fated creature’s
discomfiture, and he goes on plotting as though
he were a complete success. Whatever happens
is a flat contradiction of some previous event. An
aged father secretes .£15,000 in a black bag, and
the villain topples him over a precipice, where-
upon the bag turns out to be empty, in order that
the villain may suffer further discomfiture. Also,
after the virtuous farmer has been charged for a
long time with the murder, it transpires that the
old gentleman was never killed at all, and the
corpse had no foundation in fact. Furthermore
the farmer’s virtuous girl swears that he was
in her bedroom when the murder wasn’t com-
mitted, and the farmer retaliates by mentioning
that he is married to the lady, but neither of
these idiotic affirmations makes the least difference.
Charles Holloway aggravates the impossibility 0 f
“The Shamrock and the Rose ” by playing the
chief Irishman. The festivity of Charles is even
sadder than his brogue, and his brogue i 8 a
grievous matter anyway. Miss Alice Deorwyn
caricatures simplicity in the part of a deserted
colleen, whilst Miss Williamson now appears as
the Saxon spinster, with pleasing results. The
comic process-server falls to the lot of Sweeney,
and the fine old fruity voice of Harry Daniels is
humorously cast for the character of a desperado
armed with a gun and a tin medal stamped with
the words, “No Mercy.” Many other people are
more or less satisfactory, including the villain,
whose strong point is a certain masterful mannei
of falling down when assaulted.
At Melb. Princess’s the army under Bland
Holt continues to carry Burmah by storm. On
second thoughts B.H. is not the actual leader of
this Christian mission, although his gallantry in
jumping his horse! over a chasm (by deputy) and
waving his wounded arm for reinforcements is the
main feature of a glorious campaign. Nature did
not design its Holt for a dandy captain who is a
demon when roused, but as a delineator of hia
own identity. That outspoken comedian never
fails to amuse. The all-round immensity of “ A
Life of Pleasure” was chronicled last week,
and meanwhile this huge production has waxed
bigger if possible. Apart from dying Dacoits
and Gatling guns it embraces riots in Ire-
land, a realistic house-boat party on the
Thames, dissipation at the Empire music-hall,
and a large amount of domestic tragedy.
Above all things it is superbly giddy. When
Miss Watson flings a glass of zoedone at
Sass’s shirt-front, and retracts her determination
to enter the demimonde, the moral atmosphere
grows sultry. Likewise there is a good deal of
summer weather about when Sass, having sneaked
into the apartments of his lawful bride, attempts
to carry out the marriage contract. Perhaps it
is as well that Sass doesn’t act these risky scenes.
He merely shoots his shirt-cuffs and looks like a
gentlemanly iceberg, leaving the ladies to play up
to themselves, as it were. This is bad for true
dramatic art, but good for propriety.
** * *
The antithesis of Sass is represented by
Diver, at the Bijou. The midnight episode in
“ Friends” runs parallel with the wife-chase in
“ A Life of Pleasure,’’ the only difference lying hi
the fact that Diver goes gurgling after somebody
•else’s wife, whereas Sass is concerned with his
■own hymeneal property. Diver reels with the
intoxication of the moment; Sass maintains a cold
■equilibrium and says “Haw!” Society must
judge between the genuineness of these rival per-
formances, and if the taller actor gets the vote
marriage may reckon itself a dead failure without
further ado. Two more nights of “ Friends,” an
admirable but unappreciated show, will bring
■comedy to a halt at this house.
On Saturday the Montague-Turner Opera
00. opens here under Garnerian management. They
start upon the oft-told tale of “ Maritana,” and
hope to excite the populace with rapid changes of
programme. Also, charges for admission have
been re-constructed to suit the times, and dress-
circle tickets to see Don Caesar brandish his top-
note at a soldier’s doom are now obtainable for
three shillings.
** * *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus is getting along cheer-
fully at the Alexandra, where matinees are
thrown in twice a week, and the funny clowns
ought to be thrown out for ever. Thus far the
animals have had no chance to bite their keeper
in public, but John Burns is beginning to shake
the dewdrops from an incipient mane, and his
genetal style holds out the promise of a gory
tussle anon. John Burns is a religious influence,
nevertheless, for the tamer has learned to sing
“ O ! To be a Daniel 1” with much feeling. Martyn
Hagan is a recent addition to the Alhambra Co.,
and the Coghills have a pretty firm hold of affairs
at the Gaiety.

From an Argus criticism of Madame Urso
in one of her purely show pieces :—“A combina-
tion of harmonies and double stopping, perhaps,
appeals more to the initiated than to the casual
listener, but we may ask our readers to believe
that it is a thing which few of the greatest living
violinists would attempt and in which none could
conceivably achieve a more complete success than
did our gifted visitor.” The humour of music
reporters is wholly unconscious. Take the Akgus
authority into ah assemblage of Welshmen who
enjoy the recitation of a poem in their native
tongue. Assure him that it is a fine poem, splen-
didly spoken, and then ask him seriously to feel as
though he had appreciated the performance.
The good man will laugh. Yet he asks people to
admire the work of a skilful mechanic, although
they can neither realise the difficulties of tb®
handicraft nor find beauty in its result. Music is
the one universal language of civilised humanity,
and the fiddle that merely talks Welsh to Welsf
men is not making music at all.

_ 7 .« V oioe; there seems a vague idea that there should
, „ decided difference in method and production between
be „m.tralto and a soprano. But the fact is, that many of
fhe°famous contraltos persistently fly in the face of the
1 Hpmic rules of voice-produotion; and are, therefore,
as examples for students. Madame Sterling and
“°.*T Gomez are phenomenal in their power, hut they
A w use two registers of their voices. Their method is to
?"the chest register up to Q or A; then produce a
register up to G or A ; the head voice being
dispensed with altogether. The majority of good con-
voices, such as Madame Yanderveer Green s or Ada
rwlaslev’s would not last long if they indulged in this
° »thod Madame Belle Cole’s method, on the other
n,nd is to make the registers overlap, and she has
developed the head voice, just as a soprano won ddo ; the
result being a long range of even quality,, a blending of
ful three registers. The absence of strain or forcing
institutes the chief charm of singing, and the reserve
0 f tone, which a good contralto voice can coram md
P ,?th this method, produces more striking and satisfactory
ffpnta after all than the mere loudness which is the chief
iTpopWistic of many contraltos. Of course this has a
Sng effect while the voice is fresh, but the upper
notes are the first to give way ; later on the chest register
fella and the ruin of the magnificent organ is deplorable.

Arthur Swanborough, an old theatrical manager, tells a
storv of his first reception of Albert Edward at the St.
Tames Theatre. In an economical spirit of grovel he
ordered the vicinity of the royal box to be illuminated
wRh Pink candles. The house was draughty, and the
candles were continually getting blown out by the wind,
whereupon they diffused a smell of smouldering wick
until a hot, excited usher could relight them. Presently
» Wales” turned up, and Swanborough flunkeyed him
along to his box, wondering whether his Royal Highness
would be pleased to address a kingly remark to his loyal
•übieot The Heir Apparent, being now fairly within
nose-shot of the triumphal candles, snorted; I say,
Swanborough, what a d d stink ” Then he frowned
slightly and blew his august nose with imperial disdain.

and her companion high-kickers. No, I must candidly
confess, after some considerable experience of them, that
Adelaideans are pious frauds.”

It was young Dick Kenna, of Bathurst, N.S.W., who
owned the fighting kangaroo of London Aquarium.
Johnny Sheridan and Jimmy Macmahon were managing
partners. Dick did the Chicago World 8 l air with six
more kangaroos, caught for him by Joe Yeomans, of
Bathurst. All the kangaroos dying, Dick bought a cal-
culating horse and came to London, where he struck oil.
He is purchaser of the Australian rights of Morocco
Bound.” and is financing the co. which is coming to
Australia to plav it, and which includes Johnny Sheridan.
Dick’s brother, Pat, a Sydney Liedertafel man with a
beautiful baritone voice, is now in India singing in ooraio
opera.

M. LuCre, the husband of Camilla Urso, had one thril-
ling experience in his life, anyhow. He was travelling by
express, with his wife and a concert-party, to give a per-
formance at Philadelphia, when he found to his horror
that the violins had been left behind at Washington. It
was necessary to go back for them, and Lu6re implored
the conductor to stop, so that he might catch a return
train and bring the lost luggage along in time for the
show. But the conductor didn’t see it; his train wasn’t
timed to stop, and nothing—not even a handful of dollars
—would make him depart from his schedule. The most
he would do, after much entreaty, was to slow down as
he passed a certain wayside station, and Lufere might
taKe his life in both hands and jump if he liked. The
Frenchman jumped accordingly, went head-first into a
sandheap, caught the return train, and the violins and
a badly-scratched and abraded husband were at Phil-
adelphia by seven o’clock that evening.

Times are bad with everybody, but the third-class actor
has come down with even a heavier thud than the average.

A curious affair stopped theatrical performances at
Windsor Castle for nearly 30 years, the gap between C.
Kean and Irving. The entrepreneur had the ill-luck to
engage one Jimmy Rogers, comedian, who was disgusted
to receive 13s. 4d. as his Royal wages for the night—nowa-
days he would be a £2O a week man. The morning after
the show he turned up at one of the London police-courts
with “ A donation to the poor-box, your Worship, 13s 4d.,
the joint contiibution of Her Most Gracious Majesty
Queen Victoria and Mr. James Rogers, comedian.” For
this his snobbish manager sacked him.

One of the New Australians, Mr. Edward
Ohanning, writes from Paraguay :
The country is like the Darling Downs in Queensland,
only fresher. The people here are half-castes—l mean
the masses—and very nice they seem to be, although
their characters are not of the best. In the town of
Adjos, about nine miles from here, there are between 300
and 400 women and only 14 men—including the priest.
Parson Fitchett told an Adelaide audience
recently that the gallows was an evidence of civic
life christianised. The Bulletin long ago pointed
out that Christianity reached its consummation
in “Nosey Bob,’’ and that the gurgled “0
Christ!” of his victim as the drop fell is the
typical utterance of latter-day religion.
W. C. Shipway, another member of the
Soudan goat-hunting contingent and an enthusi-
astic Single-taxer, proposes to stand for Padding-
ton (N.S W.). Being a Single-taxer, W. C. Ship-
way naturally wants absolute Freetrade; he wants,
further, to abolish payment of members and fix
up an elective Upper House on a property basis,
so that his Henry Georgeism is curiously, mixed
with the soundest Fat Man principles. Also, he
points to the fact that he has lived for 14 years in
the electorate as a proof that he has its interests
sincerely at heart. Which proof, by the way,
isn’t so convincing as it might be. Bertrand, for
example, has been a lot more than 14 years m
gaol, but that doesn’t show that he has the in-
terests of the gaol at heart.
The Australasian publishes, side by side,
two photographs of Port Melbourne railway-pier
—one, dated 1884, showing it lined with vessels
and thronged with busy people ; the other, dated
1894, showing it utterly silent and deserted save
for a! few loafers and unemployed. The difference
is supposed to represent the horrors of 10 years
Protection—what it really represents is the
horrors of 10 years hard borrowing. The curse of
some 70 or 80 millions of “foreign capital” has
laid Melbourne waste in the interval, and the
vessels which used to dump down vast loads of
unpaid-for material, for the building of boom rail-
ways, are gone, never to return. Also, since the
new wharves were built and the river dredged the
shipping goes up the Yarra now.
A curious deadlock has come about at
Doncaster (Vic.). The Shire Council consists of
six members, four being a quorum. Two of the
six are away at Coolgardie. A third one persists
in moving all manner of wild and horrible resolu-
tions, and when the others dissent he walks out,
and the meeting lapses for want of a quorum.
Minister Webb, being appealed to, explained that
the only legal remedy was to hold four successive
meetings, and, if the offender didn’t attend, to
declare his seat vacant, but the remaining coun-
cillors pointed out that they couldn’t form a meet-
ing without him, and he wouldn’t attend unless
he was allowed to propose all the motions, and
they were carried unanimously, and if he did
attend they couldn’t expel him for being absent.
So the deadlock continues.
There are many troubles in the lot of a
Minister of the Crown in these provinces. On®
is the female deputation which comes in and tell 8
you that you were drunk, and another is the male
person who comes in and shakes you, and knocks
your head against the wall. In Maoriland recently,
a youth, who had been consigned to the Industrial
School, was accidentally drowned. His father at
once called at the Education Department for the
purpose of shaking the Minister, but that gentle-
man being out he shook the Inspector-General
instead till his spectacles and his hat and most of
his clothes flew off. The shake, in fact, was the
most cordial thing of its kind ever seen in Welling-
ton. Then he engaged in single combat with the
messengers, clerks, police, door-keeper, office-boy
and charwoman, and when at last he was secured
it seemed as if a Zulu army had devastated the
office.
