Among the active supporters of the Uni-
versity evening lectures movement down South
in 1892 were the then Premier Shiels, Barristers
Madden and Higgins, Chancellor Brownless,
Murray Smith and the Age and Argus. The
concession could have been granted by re-arrange-
ment of the time-table, but the Tories of the
University preferred to block it and did so effectu-
ally by demanding <£2 10s. per course extra from
would-be-students. Therefore, many under-grads,
continue to pay for lectures which they never get,
their only substantial return for heavy fees being
the pen, ink, paper and attention received at
examinations.

fear, has had the contrary effect, as the natives
say they are prepared to try conclusions with her
in the event of a crisis.’’ Which means that there
is going to be another scrimmage in the Uonny-
brook of the Pacific, and there is a chance that the
natives, who are spoiling for a fight with the Ger-
mans, will get all they want and a bad licking into
the bargain—especially if the Germans are allowed
to play a lone hand by England and America.
Vailele, which is seven miles from Apia, is a place
dear to Samoans, for here it was, a few years ago,
that they beat off the German attack andpxultingly
challenged their foes to “ try it on again.” And
the German naval men want to try it on again
badly, but in such a manner that they will come
out on top. Vailele is a place that can be de-
fended even against Maxims, but a party could
easily take it from the rear by making a circuit
from Apia inland—provided that the Tua Massaga
“ push,” who hold the Apia district, would act in
conjunction with an invading force, and clear the
way. But even if Malietoa’s troops do ally with
a German landing-party, the temptation to
slaughter them once they get them in the_ bush
would be irresistible —and, from a native point of
view, patriotic and honourable.
Professor Morris’s charity appeal on behalf of the
Melbourne distressed has appeared in the Times. . . .
Mr. Gillies commenting: “This is a blow with a venge-
ance. ... It looks as if the country’s resources were
exhausted, and the colony could not meet any temporary
distress. 1 felt you ought to know this at once, so as to
take any steps you thought fit. . . Victorian colonists
now in England are highly indignant. . . Government
indignant. . . .” J.B.P.Use every means to con-
tradict statements.” —Age.
Dear Bulletin, —In your sub-leader of
21sfc, on the burst-up of the New Australians in
Paraguay, you say
The next trouble will probably be a civil war between
the moral tone party and the crowd with no moral tone,
and Tire BuliLktin backs the immoral lot for all it is
worth. People of high moral tone are seldom any good in
a fight.
A shocking sentiment —and yet how true! Nine-
teen out of twenty soi-disant possessors of a “ high
moral tone” have a great aversion to fight, and
hence the ungodly and immoral man does mostly
come out on top. I don’t mean to say that there
have not been great and glorious exceptions. If
history is not lying, the Huguenots were pure-
living and moral toned, but yet full of fight ; so
were Cromwell’s brigade and the old Puritan
fathers. I daresay you have heard of the develop-
ment of the great German house of Godeffroy in
the Pacific. The success of their gigantic estab-
lishment, whose sphere extended from Valparaiso
to the Orient, was due to one man, Theodore
Weber. He always put four questions to those
who sought employment from him as traders,
agents, or clerks Can you learn native languages?
Can you live among the natives without forcing
quarrels ? Can you keep your mouth shut about
the firm’s business when you meet white men?
Are you a moral man ? If the applicant answered
the last query in the affirmative he was never sent
away to an outside post, but kept employed as a
clerk. “ A man who says he’s moral,” said
Weber, “is hardly ever any good for a post of
danger. He’ll rarely fight when wanted, and his
morality only brings him into contempt with the
natives, who think his morality is caused by a
diseased mind. There is only one place for a moral
man in the Pacific—that is behind a counter.”—
Yours, SOHWAETZKOPF.
What! ask for alms ? the very thought
A blush of shame to Duncan brought.
It clashes with a lordly screw—
Such craven talk will never do.
Better let hungry children wail,
And, parents, you can go to gaol—
Or patience; your stomachs yet may bo
Filled by the kindly J.B.P.
Stay ! there’s the Yarra’s filthy chasm—
But shun J. Madden’s fierce sarcasm.
With resolution stern and deep
Take the last dread, fatal leap.
Starve, rot, die, O mob uncouth !
Do anything bub—tell the truth.
GUMSUCKRR.
How Gilbert Died.
[FOR THE BULLETIN]
There’s never a stone at the sleeper’s head,
There’s never a fence beside,
And the wandering stock on the grave may
tread
Unnoticed and undenied—
But the smallest child on the Watershed
Can tell you how Gilbert died,
For he rode at dusk with his comrade, Dunn,
To the hut at the Stockman’s Ford ;
In the waning light of the sinking sun
They peered with a fierce accord—
They were outlaws both, and on each man’s
head
Was a thousand pounds reward,
They had taken toll of the country round
And the troopers came behind
With a black that tracked like a human hound —
In the scrub and the ranges blind
He could run the trail where a white man’s eye
No sign of a track could find,
He had hunted them out of the One Tree Hill,
And over the Old Man Plain,
But they wheeled their tracks with a wild
beast’s skill,
And they made for the hills again ;
Then away to the hut at the Stockman’s Ford
They rode with a loosened rein.
And their grandsire gave them a welcome bold '
“ Come in and rest in peace—
No safer place does the country hold—
With the night pursuit must cease j
And we’ll drink success to the roving boys,
And to Hell with the black police.”
But they went to death when they entered
there,
In the hut at the Stockman’s Ford,
For their grandsire’s words were as false as fair,
They were doomed to the hangman’s cord—
He had sold them both to the black police
For the sake of the big reward.
In the depth of night there are forms that glide
As stealthy serpents creep,
And around the hut where the outlaws hide
They plant in the shadows deep,
And they wait till the first faint flush of morn
Shall waken their prey from sleep.
But Gilbert woke while the night was dark—
A restless sleeper aye,
He had heard the sound of a sheep-dog’s bark
And his horse’s answering neigh—
And he said to his mate: “ There are hawky
abroad
And it’s time that we went away.”
Their rifles stood at the stretcher-head,
Their bridles lay to hand,
They wakened the old man out of his bed
When they heard the sharp command—
“ln the name of the Queen, lay down your
arms,”
Now, Dunn and Gilbert, stand 1 w
Then Gilbert lifted the rifle true
That close at his side he kept,
He pointed it straight at the voice and drew,
But never a flash outleapt—
For the water ran from the rifle-breech,
It was drenched while the outlaws slept,
Then he threw it down with a bitter oath
And he turned to his comrade, Dunn :
are sold,” he said, “We are dead men
both,
Yet there may be a chance for one—
I’ll stand and I’ll fight with the pistol here,
You take to your heels and run.”
So, Dunn crept out on his hands and knees,
In the dim, half-dawning light,
He made his way to a patch of trees
And vanished ere fled the night—
And the trackers sought for his tracks all day,
But they never could trace his flight.
But, Gilbert stepped from the open door,
In a confident style and rash,
He heard at his side the rifles roar,
And he heard the bullets crash,
But he laughed as he lifted his pistol-hand,
And he fired at the rifle-flash.
Then out of the shadows the troopers aimed *
At his voice and the pistol-sound,
With the rifle-flashes the darkness flamed ;
He staggered and spun around.
And they riddled his body with rifle-balls
As it lay on the blood-stained ground.
/
There's never a stone at the sleeper’s head;
There’s never a fence beside ;
And the wandering stock on the grave may
tread
Unnoticed and undenied ;
But the smallest child on the Watershed
Can tell vou how Gilbert died.

Personal Items.
Annie Besant leaves London for Australia
in July.
Isaac Pitman has been knighted in his Slsfc
year. This is intended to improve his chances in
Heaven.
London Times, by a delightful jumble of
names, has produced a new Australian poet named
“Gordon Kendall.”
Cardinal Moran is in very bad health, and
the hard work of the diocese is done by Dr.
Higgins, his assistant.
Professor Morris’s memoir of his father-
in-law, George Higinbothara, will not be published
till November. It is being printed in London.
The proposal to erect a statue to the late
J. D. Young, M.L.0., of N.S.W., is just about as
reasonable as—here all phrases become impotent.
An admirer said the other day :—“ There
are not three men in Australia who could live up
to Parkes’ hair. Just put his hair on some of his
detractors.”
On Tuesday, 29th May, Gov. Duff passed
into the second year of his exile in N.S. Wales.
Some recent English papers look for his early
resignation.
It would astonish people—or perhaps it
wouldn’t—to know the name of a visiting Eastern
magnate who was recently pilled at the Weld
Club, Perth.
“Montgomery still callous I” The small
marauder can’t even get credit for bull-dog
courage. A big one (in a uniform) would have
been “ still unshaken !”
Parkes says that N.S.W. ex-Judge Peter
Faucett, when offered a knighthood, declined it
“ with that gentleness which is a part of all true
dignity.” Parkes accepted a knighthood.
A N.S, Wales pension of £lB2O per annum
falls in through the death of the much-respected
ex-Judge Faucett. There are still three ex-
Supreme Court Judges on the pension-list.
The Age’s 24th of May leader this year is
greasily loyal. Last year it had quite a strong
republican flavour. David Syme grows older
every year. He will soon be old enough for the
Upper House.
The families of two Australian Judges
have given names to two thoroughfares in Dublin.
“ Molesworth,” an aristocratic place 60 years
ago, got its name from the family of “Old Moley,”
the big-brained Equity Judge of Victoria for 30
years, “Faucett” Lane, a poverty-stricken back
highway near Glasnevin Cemetery, was called
after the family of Peter Faucett, ex-Justice of
N.S.W. Supreme Court, who has just died.

JimmyChappes, the original of “ Cheesey,
in Marcus Clarke’s novel “For the term of his
Natural Life,” has just died at Hobart, aged 73.
By-the-way, many unpublished manuscripts of
Clarke’s are in the hands of Mr. Woolcott, of Mel-
bourne, who was his solicitor. Woolcott is a
grandson of the famous Peter Pindar (John Wol-
cott), who invented the legend about King George
111. and the apple-dumpling.
Parson Jefferis has come back to his old
North Adelaide church, which he left for Sydney
Pitt-streeb Congregational. His English boom
gradually petered out, and his peculiar oratory
was coldly listened to.
With the exception of Owen and Manning,
who are neighbours at Hunter’s Hill (where all
the Sydney Equity lawyers live), none of the
N.S.W. Supreme Court judges are ever seen
together off the Bench.
The following N.S.W, M.’sP. were origi-
nally printers by profession : O’Sullivan, M‘Court>
Stevenson, J. D. Fitzgerald, Haynes, Campbell-
Gillies, Morgan, Miller, Sam. Lees, Houghton
and Sharpe. Verily, ink’s thicker than water.
Alfred Allen, M.P., the great N.S.W.
teetotal statesman, has another book of poems
well forward. A friend, enjoying the privileges
of intimacy, says that it will include a rhymed
version of the Wood and Coal Bill of a couple of
years ago.
An up-country Victorian paper lately said
in a leading article : “ There need be no longer
any demand for Jules Verne’s and other black-
guards’works of imagination.” Next issue came
the correction : For “other blackguards” please
read “ Rider Haggard’s.”
Rowland Rees, formerly the leading
“eccentric” member of S.A. Assembly, told the
E. Torrens electors that he would lead the Oppo-
sition on his election ; in six months would be
Premier ; in twelve, Sir Rowland Rees, K.C.M.G.
Sir Rowland polled just 31 votes.
Newnes, the Tit-Bits pirate, is vaguely
alleged to have been made a baronet. He will
probably take for his arms the death’s head and
cross-bones rampant. The probable genesis of
this cheeky person’s ennobblement is his recent
greasy article on Queen Victoria’s dolls.
Now that there is a vacancy in the N.S.W.
pension-list, through the death of ex-Supreme
Court Judge Faucett, we may soon expect to hear
of the retirement of Mr. Justice Foster, and the
elevation to the Bench of Mr. Toby Barton, Q.O.
—who ought to have been there years ago.
Ex-Judge Peter Faucett, who died last
week, appeared in public for the last time six
weeks ago. He entered N.S.W. Legislative
Council one evening (after a long absence) when
the House met, shook hands all round, and left
by another door, after the President had taken the
chair.
One trait in the character of the late ex-
Judge Peter Faucett deserves to be widely known,
now that it cannot cause him any inconvenience.
Although, of course, frequently imposed on, he
was never known to refuse alms to a mendicant in
the street or elsewhere. There is at least one
N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge who, when asked
for alms, invariably looks out for a policeman.

our youngest son vot run avay from home!”
CIRCUS MANAGER: “We have a boy of that name, a very troublesome

Here is an item of secret history. Judge
Owen, of N.S.W. Equity Court, would now be
N.S.W. Chief Justice but for the fact that a
cablegram sent to Europe, where he was then
travelling, offering him the post, failed to reach
him. So Judge Darley got the Chief Justiceship.

citor*'wears his pigtail down his back under his
clothes. Ypu can see it disappearing down
between his shirt-collar and his skin. Ling is a
good cricketer" and| an excellent French scholar,
as Scotch College records prove, and he is a very
excellent fellow, to boot.
George Upward, the noted Victorian eight-
oar coach and ex-stroke, is a fine example of manly
self-reliance, pluck—and luck. Originally appren-
ticed to a saddler, he learned shorthand and got
into the Vie. Government Printing Office. Then
he became associate to Judge Williams; then
secretary to Premier Gillies ; and when the posi-
tion of Sergeant-at-Arms fell vacant, he got that.
His next stop will probably be the Bar. After
that, possibly a Judgeship will terminate a career
always onward and mostly upward.
THOSE SYRIANS!
FARMER WOODPECK: “Holy blazes, ’as yer place bin struck b’lightnin’?”
FARMER WAYBACK : “No; wife was afraid of Syrian hawkers, so I got her a gun. After I
had a bullet dug out of my leg, and buried half the stock, I thought it time to
lose that gun. It’s safer for my wife to shoot with an axe.”
N.S.W. Minister Slattery declines to
accept “until after the general election” a banquet
and testimonial tendered him. He is right
to be cautious. A Sydney man on trial for arson
once invited his friends to dine with him “ after
bis acquittal.” They duly turned up—and found
he had just got seven years.
In its obituary notice of Edmund Yates>
S.M. Herald says that “ after ten years of sue"
cess, Mr. Yates actually underwent a term of two
months’ imprisonment in defence of the rights of
free criticism.” That was hardly it. Mr. Yates
got into gaol for having made a thinly-veiled
accusation of adultery against a lady.
M‘Gregor, the Labour-member just re-
turned to S.A. Council, was employed on relief
works six months ago, and just before his election
he was navvying on deep drainage excavations at
Norwood, the chief town of his constituency. He
is a shrewd, well-read Scotchman, though now,
because of an accident, he is almost blind.
The late Edmund Yates was fond of tell-
ing a story about a joke made by John Leech, the
Punch caricaturist. Yates told him that Robert
Brough (author of “Songs of the Governing
Classes”) was going to Australia. “Ah,” said
Leech, “is he going bo Brough it in the bush?”
It was the only joke Yates ever heard Leech
make.
Une of the sisters of solicitor and barrister
Ah Mouy, of Melbourne, is in the variety-pro-
fession and known as Eunice Fernandez, pianists
and mandolinist. In the former instrumental
capacity she was for long attached to Harry
Rickards’ show. The casual observer would more
readily judge her to be of Andalusian origin than
of Celestial.

from the list of loyal citizens who attended

The affable Judge Kettle, of Maoriland,
doesn’t believe much in medical certificates. Once,
he says, a juror appeared before him and handed
up a document stating that he was confined to
bed with a serious illness, and couldn’t possibly
appear. “I think,” said Kettle, with his most
heavenly smile, “that you made a mistake in
delivering this to me personally.”
N.S.W. Justice Windeyer Jast week fell
into a smileful error while sitting in Vice-Ad-
miralty. He was deep in his notes, when a comely
lady was called. When she had got into the box,
the Judge looked up, and blandly said : “Thereis
no divorce on to-day, madam.” The lady, who
happened to be a spinster, indignantly informed
his Honor that she didn’t want divorce, and hoped
she never would. His Honor humbly apologised.
All people named E. T. Smith seem some-
how connected with alcohol. Knight E. T.
Smith, of Adelaide, lives on the product of alcohol,
being an ex-brewer; B. T. Smith, the Gough-
phonograph, lives on alcohol, because if it didn’t
exist he could not make a living by railing against
it; the late Drury-lane manager, E. T. Smith, is,
through his son, L. L. Smith, responsible for
Creme de Bouzy champagne, and a good deal of
Victorian wine and brandy business.

name, it shows us Christie Murray and som«

Brisbane Newspaper Co., in whose interest
0, H. Buzaeott has destroyed his health, and at
whose instance he has become insolvent, held a
meeting to sympathise with him; and, as a crown-
ing evidence of their solicitude, asked him by
special resolution to accept “the Yost type-writer
at present in his custody, as a small token of the
co.’s hearty desire for his welfare!” Which re-
minds The Bulletin of the railway contractor
who, when a navvy was killed by a tunnel-blast,
graciously presented the widow and orphans with
—his old shovel!
Kowalski’s “Marche Hongroise,” which
every budding pianist thumps for octave study,
has reached a circulation of 700,000,-an almost
incredible sale. The composer sold the copyright
for .£2O. Recently an English music-publishing
firm printed a “simplified edition,” being a
horrible mutilation of the original., Kowalski
wrote indignantly from Sydney, complaining of
the vandalism. The firm wrote a most polite
reply, assuring him that they thought the com-
poser was dead long ago. On receipt of this, the
genial composer exclaimed —“But, good gracious,
I am not dead ; absolutely.”
A parable for the talkative Chief-Justice
Madden, and the facts;are,true as taxes. There
was a Maoriland Judge who delighted to lecture a
condemned prisoner for an hour after the verdict,
but one day, just as he started his oration, a voice
from the dock said, “ Look here, Judge, none of
your (vermilliori) jaw. The worst (pink) penalty
the law provides is bangin’. I’m booked for that,
and you’re got no (red ochre) right to clap one of
your (crimson) sermons bn top of it. It’s
(numerous adjectives) cruelty to animals, that’s
what it is (bless) you !” That Judge never orated
again. Also, there was another Maoriland Judge
famed for terseness. His method was this:
“Prisoner, you’re found guilty of murder. You’ll
be hanged by the neck till you’re dead. Next
case, please!’’
LASSITUDE,
HEADACHE,
BACKACHE,
AND INDIGESTION
ARE SYMPTOMS OF A
DISORDERED LIVER.
CLEMENTS TONIC
IS THE ONLY
RELIABLE AND RADICAL CURE.
HAVE NO OTHER.
Mr. Edward T. Costin, Homeville, West Maitland,
N.S.W., who writes on March 23rd, 1893.—I beg
to inform you that Mrs. Costin suffered for years
with very bad nervous headaches, and about two
years since they got so bad that her hair was
turning grey, and a door slamming, or anybody
entering a room suddenly, would make her start
with fear. Seeing so many testimonials- from
people who I knew would not let their names be
published unless your Clements Tonic had had the
effect they stated, I got Mrs. Costin to try a bottle.
After taking it there was a decided improvement,
and after taking a few more bottles all headache
left, and the hair resumed its original colour.
I am happy to state that, with an occasional bottle
of your Clements Tonic, Mrs. Costin is now
enjoying very good health.—I remain, dear Sir,
yours faithfully, Edward Thos. Costin, Homeville,
West Maitland, N.S.W.

At Lithgow (N.S.W.) last week a public
meeting gave a vote on the payment of members
question : 220 were in favour of paying legislators,
and 91 were in favour of—letting the legislators
pay themselves.

The Federation proposals of the various
Australian provinces recall the words of Johnson,
to an Irishman, when the Union was mooted :
“ Don’t unite with us, it would be the union of
the shark with his prey.” Each province appears
to favour Federation for what she probably could
make out of the others.
Parkes’s revelation “ for the first time” of
the late Judge Peter Faucett’s refusal of a title is
no revelation at all. A church dignitary, with
others, used what influence he possessed to induce
the old man to assent at the time, but he was
obstinate, and, unfortunately, without ever giving
full or satisfactory reasons.
Dan O’Connor’s chairman at a recen
election meeting introduced the spluttering orator
as “a miniature Plato—not a wind-bag.” This
has sent everybody to the encyclopaedia to find
out who in antiquity was Plato, that he should
deserve this ignominy, “Plato’s opinions were
very contradictory, and his philosophy exhibited
many variations.” H’rn !
All Sydney University Chancellor Man-
ning’s talk about the bursaries, &c., distributed
amongst poor students amounts simply to the fact
that patronising charity is one part of the pluto-
cratic University’s dispensation. Poverty thus
converts into a favour what is properly due as a
right. The University of a Democracy should be
free and encourage night-classes.
While Parkes, at St. Leonards, one night
lately, _bitterly reproached Dibbs and Co. with
importing personal friends into salaried positions
at the country’s expense, he happened to look in
the direction of Mr. Oscar Meyer, who enjoyed a
seat on the platform, and a maliciously-disposed
observer states that he distinctly saw two winks—
an Aryan and a Semitic—embracing in mid-air.

income of the Queensland civil servant who is not

school teacher —the lowest-paid official of all. In
face of this attack on the system of primary edu-
cation, certain capitalistic M.’sL.A. propose to
introduce a bill into Parliament to establish a set
of superior schools in which their own bantlings
will be pampered on superior pap at the expense
of the State.
The treasury of the Sultan of Morocco, at
Mequinez, is said to contain £12,000,000 sterling
in coin, jewels and bullion. No wonder there is a
yearning among the European powers to annex the
Moorish kingdom. What a looting there will be
when the time comes ! And what a stupendous,
international drunk O’Donnell’s hungry troopers
lost in 1860 when they let Morocco off with an
idemnity of £4,000,000, not knowing that the
funds for the greatest burst in history were to be
had only a few miles ahead-! :

According to Whitaker’s Almanac, during
the last three years the religious denomina-
tions of English-speaking races have increased
as follows; Unitarians, 100 per cent. ; Free-
thinkers, 43 per cent.; Lutherans, 43 per cent. ;
Episcopalians, 25 per cent. ; minor religious sects,
25 per cent.; “no particular religion,” 18'5 per
cent.; Congregationalists, 10'9 per cent.; Method-
ists, 9 per cent. ; Presbyterians, B'l per cent. ;
Baptists, 7 per cent. ; Roman Catholics, o'6 per
cent. The total increase of population was 15'8
per cent.
Premier Patterson (Vic.) thinks that
“ there is little difference in the moral aspect
between the bulk of registry-office marriages and
the process of jumping a broomstick.” Also, he
is inclined to think that the State, by permitting
civil marriages, is lending encouragement to im-
morality. Patterson has never been suspected of
having any definite opinions except the opinions
that are best fitted to keep him in office, and from
the above it is evident that James B. expects the
morose Sabbatarian party to keep on top for some
time longer.
Lord Salisbury says that the Rosebery
Government, instead of jerrymandering with the
Registration Bill, “ ought to do something to
remedy the sufferings of the poor.” It is one of
the most touching features of party politics that
the party which is out always thinks that the
party which is in should do something for the
poor. Also, both parties are solid on the fact that
the poor want to have something done for them,
which is charity, instead of getting into a position
to do something for themselves, which is demo-
cracy.
The opposition of Single-taxer Cotton to
the N.S.W, Betterment Bill roused up Traill,
M.L.A., to denounce “ the wooden demand for all
or nothing.” The kind of democrat who wants all
or nothing is capitalism’s best friend. No great
reform ever comes in one lump, therefore the all
or nothing man always gets nothing. he
mostly knows in advance that he will get nothing,
and generally it is exactly what he is aiming at.
The practical reformer always takes any instal-
ment of reform he can get, be it large or small,
and then reaches out for more.
Sydney Telegraph remarks, without com-
ment, that “Some of the jurymen in the case
against Montgomery and Williams think that,
though the men were technically guilty of intent
to murder, they did not in reality intend to kill.”
It is one of the most horrible features of Botany,
Bay law that it is so fixed that men can be found
“technically” guilty of a crime, which even the
jury recognises they didn’t commit, and be
technically hanged on a purely technical gallows
for it, the law being aware all the time that it was
only a technical crime after all.
Prof. Goldwin Smith
I am a Second Chamber man. I believe In a Second
Chamber —it it be a good Second Chamber. What I
complain of in the House of Lords is that during the
tenure of one Government it is a Second Chamber of an
inexorable kind, but that while another Government is
in, it is not a Second Chamber at all. The effect cl the
Lords, as it at present stands, is this, that in one case it
acts as a Court of Appeal, and a packed Court of Appeal,
against the Liberal party, while in the other case—the
case of a Conservative Government—it acts not as a
Second Chamber at all. We have two Chambers under a
Liberal Government, and in the Conservative ease W 8
have a single Chamber.
And so it is and ever will be with any Chamber
chosen or elected on Property qualification.
The Single-tax is responsible for the defeat
of Cornelius Proud, at East Torrens (S.A.). The
Labour-party were under obligations to Birks and
Proud, and to show their gratitude they Quixotic-
ally sacrificed two seats. In future, just as the
“ spot ” is barred in big billiard-matches, so will
the Single-tax be barred in the case of Labour
nominees in South Australia. Packham, the Ass
nominee who has won ex-Treasurer Playford’s seat
at East Torrens, is a political chameleon. Four
years ago be opposed Edwin Smith in the same
district as a Labour nominee, and last year he
tried again as an Independent.
“ Wingeu ” Abbott, tho bellicose N.S.W.
squatter-advocate, wrote two letters to the press
within the last week, maintaining in one that if a
prisoner who held a high position in public affairs
were entitled to a release from gaol before the
termination of his sentence, so also thieves and
swindlers from the humble ranks of life should
be released. In the other letter he argued that
the attitude of N.S.W. Legislative Council to the
Mining on Private Lands Bill was preposterous
and unjust, as the people are the rightful owners
of the minerals beneath the surface. N.B.—The
general election approaches.
Mr. E. BERTRAM MACKENNAL.
E. Bertram Mackennal, whose statue of
Circe achieved honourable mention at the Paris
Salon, and may be expected to do likewise
at,the current Royal Academy show in London,
is the 30-year-old son of Mackennal, the clever
Melbourne modeller. A dozen years ago, Mac.
fils went to England to study at the R.A., then
to Paris and Rome. Anon the young artist
returned to Melbourne for the immediate purpose
of carving two panels for the front of Parliament
House. Some other samples of his skill were
wasted upon the local authorities, who have an
awkward habit of abandoning their statue schemes
owing to want of funds, and Bertram Mackennal
had begun to feel aggrieved by his native land,
when Sarah Bernhardt came along, recognised his
genius with characteristic suddenness and incited
him to re-cross the sea as soon as possible. In
Melbourne and Sydney he worked upon a bust of
Sarah which has made its mark in Paris. The
feet of E. B. Mackennal, who, by the way, is a
poet in verse as well as in marble, are now steadily
climbing the ladder of fame, and Australia points
to his birthplace with pardonable pride. An
exceptionally clever fellow, he is also a particularly
good fellow, whom popularity will find it difficult
to spoil. Just now he pays the rates, bub other-
wise lives rent free at the toney London residence
of Mrs. Brown-Potter, that amiable goddess
having invited him to fill the premises during her
absence on tour. For a genius who thus gets into
“ the swim ” there should be a brilliant time
coming, and The Bulletin has secret misgivings
that the subject of this notice may one day lack
the strength of mind necessary to repudiate a
knighthood.
Lee and Cummins, charged with the
Barraba murder, are bo be tried at Tamworth—
nob at Sydney as formerly proposed. Dibbs is
going to stand for Tamworth, and if he deprived
that town of its sensational murder-trial, with a
possible hanging to follow, his chance would be
just about nil. A good, large, cordial murder-
case in the Premier’s electorate just before polling-
day is worth a lob of votes. Instead of shifting
the Tamworth suspects to Sydney, what the
Dibbs Govt, should do is to collect all the sus-
pects of the province and send them to be tried at
Tamworth. Then the enthusiasm would be simply
boundless.
Dear Bulletin, —Bruce Smith has not
drawn his N.S.W. Parliamentary “screw,” cer-
tainly ; neither has he handed it back to the
Treasury. If lam not mistaken, it is paid to his
credit, and may accumulate for 20 years, and be
drawn by him or his heirs in a lump sura at the
end of that period. When he -formally pays it
into the Treasury it will be time for him to get
credit for self-sacrifice. G. H. Reid actually did
this with that proportion of his Parliamentary
allowance to which he deemed himself disentitled ;
hence he may be regarded as the only M.P.
(opposed to Payment of-Members) who is possessed
of a grain of consistency.—Yours, &e., J.0.F.,
Windsor.
An angry person writes to Melb. Age pro-
testing that rich Victorian merchants benefit by
the “low” (twopenny minimum) postal rates,
whilst the poor working man is taxed to make up
the loss. The public mind is decidedly hazy as
regards post-office business. In the first place,
there isn’t a loss to make up, but a profit, so the
Eoor man is not unduly taxed in this instance,
econdly, and lastly, the people who write only
six letters a year would find the cost of corres-
pondence enormously increased were it not for the
other people who post 1 sixty, six hundred, or six
thousand times as much. 'The stamp department
is practically a co-operative business, in which the
large purchasers enable Small purchasers to get
their goods cheap.
Cable informs us that Anarchist Henry
“ maintained his bravado to the last, twice shout-
ing out on the scaffold ‘Vive L’Anarohie!’”
That was bravado, mark ! When the mediaeval
Protestants or Catholics stuck to their opinions at
the burning stake, they maintained their faith.
Like the foolish Frenchman they were always
striving'to-regenerate society by murder, and the
question as to which sect should burn tire other
was determined by circumstances. In either case
the roasted pietists became known to posterity as
martyrs. Henry is only a striking exam pi nr of
bravado. The distinction between church religion
and political religion is kindly defined for us—
Lawanorder.
The Australian Star (Sydney)' started
life as a democratic paper. Now it celebrates
Victoria’s birthday with a - long article on the
blessings of monarchy, and when it has got all the
old, stale, exploded platitudes off its chest it com-
placently observes : “To our opponents there only
now remains the stock objection—that the heredi-
tary principle may place a fool on the Throne.”
Which reminds The Bulletin of a small, stunted,
aggressive atheist of five-feet nothing—a narrow-
chested person with watery eyes and goggles—
whom it once met with in a suburban hall, This
person—he was about 19—held forth in a shrill
voice for ten minutes or so, and then remarked :
“ Having thus conclusively abolished the Deity,
we will now consider various other aspects of the
case.” It is always the smallest-sized man who
abolishes the Deity in ten minutes.

Society.
Dear Bulletin,— Your correspondent,
“ T.M.,” says: “It is humorously pathetic to
see in tradesmen’s windows in Mildura notices to
the effect that ‘ orders will be taken on” several
firms.” So it is. The Mildura man “ gives an
order ” for everything he wants —clothes, groceries,
grog, furniture, rent, horse-feed, board and
lodging. Lately there were some weddings here.
One bridegroom gave the local jeweller an “order”
for the hymeneal ring; another, at the conclusion
of the ceremony, handed the parson an “ order ”
on one of the firms. There is now no other
original way left to locally utilise paper currency
unless we put an “ order ” for sixpence in the
plate o’ Sundays.— Nat.
* * *
Exuberant loyalty got a nasty jar in Bris-
bane, the other day, at a religious garden-party.
Lady Norman, who was to open the show, did
not turn up to time, but the wife of a well-known
wine-and-spirit-man did, with the carriage-clatter
that beseems vice-royalty, whereupon the string
band struck up “Gorsave,” to the horror of some
and the delight of others. The.“ cream ”of Aus-
tralian society don’t seem to recognise that
“ Gorsave” is merely played to male Excellencies,

It was a long, long time ago in the far, far
North. He was an M.L.A. out shooting in the
close season. Approaches a policeman new to the
district, who knows not the legislative Caesar.
“ I’ll trouble yez to hand over the gun, sorr ! ”
commenced the guardian of the laws. “ How
long have you been in the district? ” queries the
shooter. “Two months!” was the answer.
“ You’ve been here too long—you want a change,
my friend,” said the legislator blandly. And he
got it.
# * *
A woman swore in Sydney Divorce Court
the other day that she went to a local clergyman
to take the pledge, but what she saw there—a
bottle of brandy on the table, a bottle of wine on
the piano, and cakes lying about—so impressed
her with the mockery of the thing that she did not
repeat the words of the pledge after the minister.

little boom in illicit-still

poison when it is distilled.
If there were no market for
this home-made whisky, the

The Chinese Mission Hall is now in full
swing in Little Bourke-street, Melb. On week
evenings good Christian young ladies go down to
teach John to read and write English, and on
Sundays they give him liberal doses of Scripture.
He sneezes at the Scripture, but finds the young
ladies “ welly ni.” Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat,
at the dedication of this new gospel-shop, said that
“China was the only country in which slavery
had never existed, and the proportion of criminals
to the population there was only one-tenth of the
proportion in our country.” Which explains why
a thin missionary, with spectacles, farewells to
China every month with a great display of enthu-
siasm, while the poor Caucasian languishes un-
cared-for with nobody to tell him about Jonah.
# # «
The Sydney lost-dog question is being
dished up again in all its richly-flavoured aspects.
The lost dog administration goes on this plan. The
Corporation dog catchers arrest canine waifs and
strays on the simple principle that any dog which
can be overtaken and captured is a stray, and any
dog which can’t be overtaken isn’t. The animals
are confined together in a narrow pit where some
are suffocated, or chewed to death, or generally
mashed underfoot, and the remainder acquire all
the aggregate contagious diseases of the crowd.
After 24 hours the least valuable of the dogs are
killed by a simple and chaste, not to say beauti-
fully humane and Botany Bay-like, process of
pounding on the head with an iron bar, and the
others are held over for further consideration.
When the man with the bar is an expert he brains
each dog with one sickening thud; when
he isn’t an expert-ugh! The deceased are
then collected and carted to a boiling-
down establishment, where their fat is made
into soap. Therefore, the Sydney man washes
himself with old diseased terrier or mashed
Towzer. The lean of the boiled-down remains
goes for duck-feed, and the duck lays many eggs
on this dog-nourishment, and man eats the eggs
in a peacefully contented fashion, unaware that
the lank and leprous pup is the real foundation
thereof. Whether any of the dog-fat is turned
into oleomargarine or lard no one knows—possibly
it is. Anyhow, The Bulletin staff has abjured
eggs, and it scrubs itself now with a brick.
* # #
The last words of one well-known Mel-
bournian re another land-boom magnate were brief
and to the point. “Blank is a scoundrel,” said
the dying man, and straightway he departed for
the place where such remarks have to be justified.
“Tarbrush” Woods, M.L.A., on his death-bed
merely blamed himself for acting as another
man’s dummy, but even that small incident
may have struck a chill to many souls now insol-
vent. It is not well to be cursed or complained of
by one whose brow grows clammy, and whose
glazed eyes are looking their last at a sinful world.
A considerable number of Melbourne’s bruised
idols can take it for granted, however, that their
names will be mentioned from time to time by
faint, accusing voices from the bed-clothes. If
perchance one of these shrivelled fat men casually
shivers it is because a moribund dupe murmurs,
“ Blank is a scoundrel ! ”

women. An actress never falls enthusiastically
upon the chest of a man who is distasteful to her.

Dear Bulletin, —In the discussion on the
“morals ” of Paula Tanqueray, this view has not
yet been stated : Abstractly , the sin of unchastity
is no greater for the woman than for the man;
but, relatively, her guilt is increased twofold by
the fact that in committing this particular sin, the
woman betrays the trust which the State places in
her in making every woman the guardian of her
own virtue. In the East, the woman is nob held
responsible for her actions, and is locked up. The
absolute fidelity—forced, if Eastern ; voluntary, if
Western—of the woman is part of the bed-rock on
which civilised society is built up. Man must
protect himself against confusion of offspring,
and let the newly-emancipated sex remem-
ber this that a gradual lowering of the
standard of “morality” may lead us back-
words to the Yashmak and the Harem.
No man can palm off on a woman a child that is
not her own, whereas “it has been many an
honest man’s hap,” says Fielding “to pass as the
father of children which are none of his.” Of
course, we may at the present time, for anything
we know to the contrary, be pressing forward to a
glorious Socialistic Millennium of Phalansteries
and Free Love, in which the very wisest child will
not be able—nor willing—to recognise his own
father ; but until that change in the nature of man
takes place that he shall cease to care about pre-
serving the homogeneousness of his offspring, the
relative standard of chastity fof tliO two sexes will
be pretty much what It Id at present.—Yours
OiESAR’s Wife. ’

Sydney E. News, obituarising Edmund
Yates, says that—
The society journal at its best is no great things. At
its worst, in its niter shallowness, its incessant person-
alities, and its eternal chatter about little things, it has
really been one of the most offensive, as well as melan-
choly, developments of our time.
To appreciate this solemn truth it is only neces-
sary to read the “society ” report printed in the
Town and Country Journal and “lifted” into
the JSvening News—a chronicle wherein are re-
counted the glories of the bailiff’s tea-party, and
how Miss Smith “was much sought after,” and
“ looked nice as usual.”

A Brittlebank, who is almost as enthu-
siastic a correspondent as that other ex-argonaut,
Pope, writes re Rue Australia :
The buildings on the settlement are not fit for beasts to
herd in, and the standard of decency is so low as to even
shock those of the densest sensibilities. The Australian
press should bestir itself in denouncing the scheme, and
do all in its power to prevent young girls coming here.
This is no place for respectable women.
Which probably means that some of the settlers
are beginning to keep harems and practise a modi-
fied form of polygamy. Man is polygamous by
nature, and when he gets away from civilisation
his very first desire is to keep a harem. The
reason why, in most cases, he only does it when he
gets away from civilisation is that the world can’t
struggle out of the old groove; therefore, if he
starts his harem openly in his old haunts he will
be blasted by the criticism of other people, who
mostly feel the same way that he does, but don’t
like to admit the fact. But if he and his critics
could all get away from public observation
together they would mostly go the same road.
The desire to keep a harem is practically incur
able.

Dear Amelia M'Gaunt, —I am a young
Civil-servant, provided by Govt, with a lucrative
salary of £2 10s. per week, which has to provide
me with faultless clothes, spotless shirt, tan boots
and button-hole (in order that the Block may re-
ceive me with open arms on Saturday mornings);
also with my keep and that of three cubs—St.
Bernards. >lf I marry you I shall have to keep
you, and perhaps more cubs, and the Govt, is
talking about retrenchment. —Yours, &c., Cholly.
B.M.

quote “ Tay Pay ” on Paula
Tanqueray’s suicide, which

and murderous inequality
of your sexual code.” Now
in the name of brutal frank-
ness what was the matter
with Paula? She hints her-
self that she would have
been all right could she
have had a child. If she
had been all right (for want
of a better word) she would
have met Mrs. Cortelyon’s
overtures and the “ mur-
derous inequality,” &c., &c.,
would have ceased to weigh
—society was forgiving
enough. But her whole
nature was perverted, soured
and cursed by a benign,
smug- faced Providence wh ich
had arranged that her early
debaucheries should inca-
pacitate her for maternity—
“I know I can never have
a child,” wails the poor
wretch. “A God of Mercy.”
Pah ! how the phrase must
have stunk in her nostrils.

crites—slap a poor, much-
abused “ society ” in the
face, instead of the real
“ cruel and murderous ”
hounder-down ?—W.

ON THE GEELONG BOAT.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was a portly passenger, he had a double chin;
A ruddy man with wrinkles that implied a
comic bent.
He called a crier to him, and he bought a Bul-
letin,
And then picked his seat, and squatted with an
air of sweet content.
He filled his pipe and lit it, and he took a pull of
“ pap”
As the steamer churned the smells up (Yah 1 the
odour made me sneeze),
Then he doffed his glossy “topper,” hid his bald-
ness in a cap,
Donned a wrap, and reared his heels up, blew a
cloud, and sat at ease.
From the prow I gazed upon him. “Here’s a good
old sort,” I said,
As he beamed with calm enjoyment on an
illustrated page;
But presently he started, and the bland content-
ment fled
From his ruddy face, and left it blank, perplexed,
then black with rage.
He looked once more and snorted, and then down
upon the deck
He hurled the paper, cursing in a way that gave
me pain.
For an hour at least his mutterings of wrath he
could not check,
And I watched him through the journey, and he
never smiled again.’
“ Alas ! ” me thought, “ what bigots are these
men of means and weight,
How intolerant, how selfish, and how narrow in
their view.”
I secured the tattered journal, and my eye fell on
the date,
It was set forth on the cover—“ Sydney, April,
’92.”
SILAS SNELL.


DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY ia primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy ia not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be.
cot for sevenpence • but if the ruling price for the best is eightpence, and somebody comes- along and offers it for
sevenpence then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

Dear Bulletin, —The colour of the cuticle
is nothing. The other day I took the wife and
boys for a picnic. We bad to cross a swamp,
lined with vegetable-gardens. We ventured to
intrude on one. “There’s no thoroughfare here,”
growled a menacing white face when we got half
through. We pled hard ; two miles round by the
road. “Now, you go back.” We went sorrow-
fully ; tried a Chinaman next door. He led us
pH through his garden, smiling so sweetly that we
bought two melons we didn’t want. Why doesn’t
the white hawker succeed ? Because he isn’t white
inside. —D.G.

Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent,
“Banshee,” is out in his facts about women
medical students in Melbourne. Melb. Hospital
did not establish a boycott against women medicals.
Its committee did not refuse them ; it only pointed
out that it might nob make for modesty for men
and women students to mix together in hospital
work during their studentship. Anyone who has
spent a morning in the out-patient department of
a big hospital will easily understand why. The
committee suggested that perhaps the Alfred
Hospital might take the women, while Melb.
Hospital took the men. Everybody considered
the idea good so it was pub into effect. Nor was
there even any undertaking that the women should
confine themselves to obstetrics or diseases of
women and children. For without full work and
full certificates they could nob legally receive
diplomas. As to the Melb. Hospital committee’s
refusal to have women residents, the cause is nob
prejudice, but the fact that the accommodation
for the resident medical officers is far too limited.
• » *
A pronounced Yic. parson is fulminating
against civil marriage which he Stigginsmatises as
“ legitimised immorality.” The Registry-office
charges a few shillings for the “fixing” process,
the Church extorts as many pounds. Which,
then, offers the greatest to mar-
riage, and, per centra, which the greatest incentive
to immorality ? For it is only those unions which
need encouragement that become immoral if they
remain informal. The fact is, the depression is
driving people to the registry-offices where they
cun be “spliced” cheaply, independently and
effectually. True, the Church will reduce the
charge if required, but in making a reduction it
always wears its blackest scowl.

Dear Bulletin, — Be the Mormon colony
at Forster, Gape Hawke, N.S.W. I spent some
of the happiest) wage-days there. More money,
better living ; more picnics and shorter hours
working for a selector who was one of the leading
lights. Their pastor worked during the week
and preached most eloquently on Sundays on any
important question of the day. They call them-
selves Latter-Day Saints, and repudiate the sect
who strive for polygamy, which is denounced in
their Bible, the Book of Mormon.—O. A. J.L.

A GOOD STOVE.
A WORD OF CAUTION.
Everyone knows what a good stove is—at least,
the women do; and every woman knows that, of
all the treasures of the household, a really first-
rate, reliable cooking-stove is the greatest desider-
atum in a house, A great many stoves are
advertised every year, many of which are like
jerry-built houses—thrown together to sell A
word of caution is therefore necessary to intending
buyers, because there are few things so annoying
and vexatious as to buy a stove and then find that
it does not possess all the qualities that are neces-
sary to give complete and-lasting satisfaction.
Now, the points in choosing a good stove are:
1. Strong cast-iron frame.
2. Good draught,
3. Facilities to burn either wood, coal, or
even turf.
4. Simplicity of parts, and thus, easy to clean.
5. Duplicate parts kept in stock, so that
breakages can be replaced at small cost
and without delay.
And if, in addition to these five points, you can
get a stove that, is also an .ornament, to the kitchen,
all the better. ■
Price, of course, is a point, but that is a matter
which need scarcely be mentioned, since, if the
above points are combined in any stove, it would
be cheap at any price within reason.
It only remains for us to say that, with con-
fidence, we can honestly recommend The
Housewife,” sold by Holdswortb, Macpherson
and Co., 250 George-streeb, Sydney, from =63 10s.
upwards, as one that fully and entirely fills the
bill.” . . . ..
We should suggest our readers writing to this
firm for an illustrated catalogue, with further
particulars.
A PUFF OF POWDER.-A METRICAL VERSION.
[For Tub Bulletin.]
In the sweet
Suggestive hiss
Of a fleet
Midsummer- kiss,
Or the fair
Seductive charms
Of a pair
Of naked arms
There is danger.
There is death
In woman’s pratlls
Or the breath
Of guns in battle ;
And the louder
Angry din
When the powder
Roars therein.
More destructive still than those
Is the powder on thy nose.
W.J.

No notice will he taken of communications insuffi-
ciently stamped. Name and address must
appear on MS. No liability is undertaken re
contributions voluntarily submitted , and in no
case will MS. be returned unless stamps,are
sent.

In the days of Cromwell it was “killing—
no murder.” In Botany Bay it is “murder no
killing.”
The Sydney Telegraph’s sensation about
how the one industry which Protection has created
in N.S.W.— i.e., the sugar-growing business—only
pays the white employes 16s. a week, has got
another knock. First of all, it was announced that
the wages were 16s. and rations; now it further
transpires that the minimum wage paid by the
largest and most prosperous sugar co. is really 205.,
with board and lodging. Therefore, the Tele-
graph now rises to demonstrate that men could be
got at a cheaper rate than this ; consequently, the
wage above-mentioned is really, to a great extent,
an act of charity ; and, as a further consequence,
Protection is a pauperising institution and half-
brother or so to the Benevolent Asylum. Which
practically amounts to the statement that if Pro-
tection pays 16s. a week, it is a curse because it
puts down wages when it shouldn’t do so; and
when it pays what is equivalent to about double
that amount, it is a curse because it puts up wages
when it needn’t do so.

My Dear Moorabinda—
The Queen’s Birthday parade of N.S.W. troops
took place in the Centennial Park under the
glassy eye of Gov. Duff atop of a bright bay gee
and sporting a silk hat and phenomenally-short
stirrup leathers —to make his balance true, I sup-
pose. The dazzling appearance of “Uncle” in
“ purple and gold ” (to speak with poetic license)
certainly justified the current whisper that in re-
forming the millinery of the military he has only
taken the trimming off the staff and added it to
his own king-parrotish glories; however, looking
at it with the petrified philosophy of an unaffected
person, it seems only right the leading man should
have the pick of the wardrobe if but to encourage
the others. It is so fortifying to the raw recruit
to think that four thick rows of tinsel round his
coat tails and a hat like a three-cornered tart are
possibilities that every Australian soldier carries
in his knapsack.
I think the Review is going off as a society
spectacle. A mere handful of carriages turned
out on Thursday, but whether the financial pres-
sure of the times has set people on their feet
again, or whether the Highland Regiment has
lost its fatal fascination for Sydney belledora, I
am at a loss to determine. But the absence of a
crush in the vice-regal enclosure was balanced by
the absence of a crush in the field, where the com-
ponent parts of the display were city and suburban
soldiers ; the Military Estimates not running to
the wholesale importation of the bush-whacking
aspects of local valour.
Some of the horses might have been fresh
caught from Jamberoo by their appearance. One
chalk-coloured charger had its shoulder skinned,
and another grey horse was strawberry-coloured
witli dried mud. When “Uncle ” was starring in
the provinces he spoke up sharply about the door-
mat appearance of most of the country troop-
horses. He objected to bare-ribbed frameworks,
with hairy hoofs and chronic depression of spirits,
supporting the equestrian glory of our “Horse”
on their unkempt backs, and I certainly looked
for shaven fetlocks at least all along the line at
the Review. Here and there one saw evidences
of careful strapping, and sometimes the gee was
better-looking than the rider. One concave-
chested warrior had a very broad-chested mount;
and, talking about hollow chests, one officer’s
“chuck” had slipped to his belt, and it played
the very Falstaff with his silhouette.
Madame Lucien Henry’s monthly reunion at
her class-room, 289 Pitt-street, the other Tuesday,
took the improving shape of a compact and
clearly-enunciated lecturette by Madame Henry
on “ Millet,” rendered light and airy by music and
tea and good engravings of the painter’s best
works, which were handed round during the
lecture. By the way, I don’t think Art moves in
a very mysterious way for local society, which loves
the pink and white maiden hugging a pug dog in
its secret heart, and sees no rugged beauty in King
Billy, For example, Madame Henry showed one
engraving of a peasant’s head—an “ugly” study,
but full of force and genius and a savage ideality.
Yet more than half the audience looked at it and
grinned.
The Barracks broke out into a military tattoo on
Queen’s Birthday eve. I hope the military will
make a point of tattooing from time to time, as
Sydney is a place absolutely gasping for more band-
music under the silent stars, especially in the
spring and summer. But the evening breeze of
May isn’t just the sort of temperature one feels
envious of sitting out in, and I am sure that a
large section of the muffled-up women in front of
the officers’ quarters must have yearned to warm
their feet and hands at the flaming flambeaux.
The members of Sydney University Dramatic
Society were more stereotyped than usual in the
pieces they put up at Sydney Royal Standard
Theatre the other evening. “Time will Tell ”
isn’t such a literary fossil as the compact U.D.S.
usually faces you with, and Gilbert’s “Creatures
of Impulse” is strictly modern. It is highly pro-
bable the stock of nice, decent old comedies, suit-
able for young amateurs, has given out and left
the society to the flabby mercies of modern insi-
pidity, but I hope there are one or two dramatic
bald-heads left to act as buffers between this clever
society and plays like “ Withered Leaves.”
In the fairy comedy Miss B. Roper Elliott
talked like May Pollard and acted in her usual
competent fashion. Mr. A. B. Davies, as a bold
warrior, was somewhat oppressed by a misfit bald-
head, but he divided the histrionic honours with
Miss Elliot all the same, and the whole comedy,
smartly dressed and with “ real pigeons ” on a
roof, went very gaily to the crisp strains of the
Post-office band.
The main dish was the comedy “Time will
Tell,” in which the greenest laurels were achieved
by Mr. Gregan M'Mahon, as the wicked, but
airy, French count. From the top-look of his
frizzy black wig to his crimson socks, Mr.
M'Mahon was cleverly made up, and his acting
most convincing throughout. His badly-broken
French accent was unerring, too, which is a point
quite a number of “ dialect ” professionals fre-
quently fail to make. Pretty Miss Caro, as two
separate varieties of damsel, was lady-like and
effective throughout, and Miss Ethel Paige made a
housewifely maiden of the heiress, and achieved
the rare theatrical phenomenon of a real blush
behind the footlights.
Of the LL.B.’s and other savants engaged in
the mimic show, Mr. Pickburn as a lord, Mr.
D. W, MTntyre as a breezy manufacturer, and
Mr. Garnsey as a martyr, played like old stock
actors who scent a cue a mile off, and the only
people who rather got on one’s nerves were the
“Duke,” who roared like a sucking lion, and the
Attorney, who didn’t. Mr. Charles Burford
coached the players, and he deserves the best
quality of all-wool praise for his part of the
business.
No strictly truthful person could assert that the
Birthday meeting of Sydney Turf Club at Rand-
wick was a smart and successful function —on the
second day, at least. For one thing, autumn
frocks have lost their edge in merry May, and the
world doesn’t trot out its winter tackle until the
A.J.C. midwinter meeting crops up. Obviously
Lady Duff hadn’t donned her new winter turn-
out. She wore a plain dark-blue skirt and a
summer mantle of black lace, the whole topped by
the inevitable toque, a size too large, of black
velvet, trimmed in a rakish way with black
feathers out of curl and a sort of plaster of
autumn leaves in front. It was the kind of top-
ping-off one associates with (let me be candid,
for once) a check shawl and a jug of beer, though
mow the style is under Y.R. patronage I suppose
■it holds a higher place in coeval millinery.
A feature on the lawn was the A.D.C.’s fiancee
i(in pale-grey and silver and hints of heliotrope
velvet), nestling under her captive’s wing. The
Major-Generaless wore a gown the colour of
peat-smoke; Mayoress Manning, a cosy-looking
dress of broken checks, draped over seal-brown
velvet and bordered with brown fur, small green
bonnet. Green —bright caterpillar green—is
■coming in, so a golden-haired society girl wore it
cap d pie. In the distance she reminded me that
there is a green hill far away. Another lady wore
green brilliantly lit up with the now fashionable
scarlet geranium. Mrs. (Major) Rennie’s strong
point was her three-quarter cloak of velvet and
cloth bordered with sable. Mrs. Brough wore
quaker-grey and a small black bonnet, and sat on
the grand-stand near a brother-professional who
is the proud possessor of about the most artful
wig I ever saw.
I sat behind him at a theatre once and
thoroughly entered into the ingenuity of his
artful top-knot. You will have seen that when a
man takes his hat off the hair is pressed down
where the inner rim goes ? Well, that’s where bis
wig commenced. There were no clumsy finishinga-
off round the nape of the neck, and in the event
of a red Indian scalping him the process would be
very elementary. The possible savage would just
lift that convincing top-knot off like the lid of a
saucepan.
Miss Ada Bell’s “Flowerland’ Festival and
Dramatic Entertainment takes place in Sydney
Town Hall early in June —admittance one shilling
or by “Flowerland ” art union ticket which gives
the holder a chance of getting one of “ Flower-
land’s” charming pictures. lam not at liberty to
anticipate the programme, but the Festival will
be a complete novelty, and the art union tickets
are selling quickly.
Other June fixtures are the ball in aid of that
worthy society, the Fresh Air League, whose
work is explained in the title. The ball is dated
for next Wednesday, the 6th inst., in Sydney
Town Hall, and, judging by the ticket sale, the
whole lawn intends to be present. The Univer-
sity ball on 21st, and the ball in aid of Tempo
refuge on 26th, both of which occur in the Town
Hall, are all June fixtures. To-night (Thursday)
the dance in aid of the Servants’ Home in Char-
lotte Place eventuates at Paddington Town Hall
in the presence of Lady Duff, and deserves to be
largely attended. Mrs. Bowden-Smith is Presi-
dent, and the tickets are only ss. each.
I had the pleasure, the other day, of hearing Miss
Fitz-Stubbs play her smooth new waltz, “The
Orlando,” after which I don’t wonder at its
having reached its second edition already. There
is a wonderful sequence in the melodies —each
movement seems to be the complement of the
other. By the way, somebody tried to jump the
title of “The Orlando,” but Mrs. Woods, by
rushing thro’ a printer’s edition and selling a few
copies, “got ahead.” “In future,” she says, “I
will make a missing-word of my title till the day
of issue,”
Day was made hideous as usual on 25th May
by the Birthday levee at Sydney Government
House, when the air was darkened with velvet-
lined, fur-trimmed officials and the “ general
public,” which latter went up in their willie-
wagtails, at 3 p.m., and tried to look as if,it
wasn’t a strain on their loyalty. Judging by the
published lists the crowd was indeed a representa-
tive one, ranging from the Chief Justice to the
sparkling corner-man at a variety-show—who, I
am told, was the only suggestion of corks in the
tedious and thirsty function.
The levee is another proof of what a rosy time
women have. When vice-regality gives a mixed
day-reception provender is carried about all the
afternoon, but when a he-function blocks the
social way there isn’t a biscuit from Dan to Beer-
sheba. I think this famine system of reception
must have a weakening effect on the loyalty of the
male bird, which requires much more bolstering
up than does the deeply-rooted monarch-worship
of women, who give away their frantic adoration
of the Throne and Person with or without a cup
of tea. And yet man’s tongue cleaves to the roof
of his mouth in his loyal moments, while woman
is bailed up by waiters with sponge-cakes.
What is the secret of the effervescence which is
always in the air at French functions ? The over-
crowded ball in aid of the Bienfaisance Frangais
at Paddington Town Hall, the other Wednesday,
was no exception, although the countless dancers
trod on one another’s toes, and Lady Duff looked
wearily amiable in a gown we have all admired (o r
aa the case may be) dozens of times. But there
was no getting away from the sparklesomeness of
the thing, and though Society (which was largely
represented) had come without its diamonds (you
pile on the jewellery according to the price you
pay for your ticket), there were many fetching
garments in evidence. Madame Tauveron (hon.
sec.) wore an indescribable gown that impressed
you with its Parisian elegance, and so did her
coadjutor, Madame de Bissel. At the same time
I couldn’t tell you what the colours were. It is
always difficult to reduce a really well-dressed
woman to black and white. You are only conscious
of effect.
Madame Kellermann’s scarlet silk toilet, with a
coquettish tuft of tri-colour feathers in her hair,
was a bright spot in the crowd, and in Such a
crowd it was wisdom to wear primal colours.
Women in pinky-whitey hues are absolutely
unseen in a crowded room, and I just caught sight
of Brenda Gibson in white satin and a white
gardenia in her hair dancing the lancers with Mr.
1 ranklyne, who appeared to have just stepped out
of “ Lady Windermere’s Fan ” —by-play and all.
Miss Dudlay also aired her massiveness in the
same set, and wore a pinky frock with ribbons, A
lady in green, with her hair powdered, must have
known she would look like a leek, and somebody
with a figure like a coast-lizard unsagely wore a
tight fit. In an age like this, of Grecian draperies,
even a female Punch needn’t reveal the hump,
unless she chooses. By the way, so elated are the
committee at the success of their dance that next
year the Sydney Town Hall, with double-priced
tickets, will be the order of march.
If the vice-regal people turn up much later than
they are in the habit of doing at public balls, the
committee will do well to put the opening V.R.
set of lancers four numbers before supper instead
of at the top of the card. At the French ball, the
company was panting after several dances before
the Guvmentouse people turned up and “ opened
the ball,” as the shibboleth goes. As a matter of
vulgar fact, that ball had opened itself very cheer-
fully an hour or two before. The chronic un-
punctualities of their Excellencies the Duffs
almost looks as if they didn’t dress for dinner.
At the Melbourne Birthday Review, says a
correspondent, although there was a great attend-
ance the manoeuvres did not elicit a single cheer.
Had the soldiers all been building-society secre-
taries and land-company directors they could not
have been regarded with greater apathy by the
crowd, for whom they provided a very decent
show.
I hear from Melbourne that the small attend-
ance at Lord Hopetoun’s levee, on Queen’s Birth-
day, was an acknowledgment of His Excellency
and Lady Hopetoun’s prolonged absence from the
metropolis. For the evening party a thousand
were invited, and a third of these didn’t turn up.
The Hobart levee was the largest and the Mel-
bourne one the smallest in those respective cities
for several years.
An unusual circumstance was responsible for
many people enjoying the privilege of private
entree at Gov. Hopetoun’s shivoo last week. At
the ball-room entrance a butler and orderly found
they had a dispute that would not permit of ad-
journment, the result being that the former “ got
home with one in the eye” of the latter, who
rolled down the steps with a bang. A posse of
policemen were soon on the scene, and to keep
matters quiet they directed all the approaching
carriages to proceed to the private entrance, while
the white-card guests wondered why so unexpected
an honour had been done them.
I hear that a certain accomplished and pushing
professional gentleman of somewhat South-Sea-
idol-ish appearance has just become engaged to a
very pretty Northern blonde, nearly related to a
British political magnate.
Yours apprehensively.
SAPPHO SMITH.
Memo for statesmen afflicted with “ back-
bone”—A man’s backbone is a passage to his
brain. It is very well to have a backbone—if you
have a brain.
If the Irishmen of Gipps Division of West
Sydney “vote en bloc for Dan O’Connor against
Black,” as threatened, they will succeed in prov-
ing that they are not democratic, but only Irish.
As the rival sections of N.S.W. Labour
party seem utterly unable to establish a reasonable
modus vivendi, why don’t they toss up re the
nomination of a candidate in every constituency
where dog is trying to eat dog? Take Clark and
Holman, for instance. Neither can possibly get
in while Hawthorne, with united Property at his
back and a good personal following, is in the way.
The means employed to convict the
burglars Montgomery and Williams on the
capital charge of wounding with intent to murder
were almost as dubious as those employed to
convict and hang the Mt. Rennie prisoners. The
action of N.S.W. Crown Law Office, which, in
defiance of every vital principle of public policy,
filed only one count, a capital indictment, against
the desperadoes, instead of arraigning them under
the section specially provided to meet such
cases, would, in any court of arbitration, be
set down as the sharpest of sharp practice, and
utterly unworthy of any sphere save, perhaps, the
pawnbroking or time-payment-furniture business.
It was the Crown’s duty to render the burglars’
conviction certain if they were guilty. By filing
the capital indictment, instead of one involving
life - imprisonment, and allowing the jury no
medium course, it challenged them to acquit
the men altogether. They did not acquit—but
the end of that prosecution in no way justified
the means.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Editor Bulletin,—-My attention has been drawn
to a statement in this week’s issue of Tim Bulletin (p. 20),
under the head of “Sporting Notions, '* which seriously
reflects upon my character; “ Victor Cohen, manager of
the ’93 Australian Eleven, lent him (a well-known sporting
journalist) £B, but the schedule does not state whether
It came oat of Vic.’s pocket or the general funds of the
Eleven,” Now, the loan in question was made by me in
the year 1887 or 1888, I think the former, long before the
cricket-team, of which I was manager in 1893, was
thought of or organised. The implied charge as to my
having misappropriated the amount mentioned from
moneys, of which I was trustee, is, therefore, unfounded,
and I must ask you to publish in your'next issue an
explanation of and an apology for having made
the statement in question.—Yours, Victor Cohen.
Malua, Billyard Avenue, Elizabeth Bay, 23rd May, 1894.
[ln reference to the foregoing, The Bulletin can only
express its regret that the paragraph in question should
have been for one instant thought by Mr. Cohen suscep-
tible of any sinister interpretation. No suspicion of any
unworthy imputation against him was felt when the para-
graph was passed—the lines were read to convey that it
was not known whether Mr. Cohen had lent the money in
his private, personal capacity, or as manager for (and on
behalf of) the Eleven. We have to apologise if, in the
opinion of Mr. Cohen or his friends, any other meaning
can pessibly attach to a paragraph printed without the
remotest desire to reflect upon his honour.—Ed. B.]
Dear Bulletin, — You’ve published some good
yarns fc-om time to time concerning old Mossy Isaacs,
but, so far, the best remains in the bag. Here it is. and
it's a true bill. In Martini’s year an innocent-looking
“mug"asked Mossy for the double—Martini and First
Demon for the Derby and Cup. Mossy wrote the wager
in his book, the “mug” looking over his shoulder.
Mossy was then asked for a ticket, and whilst writing it
he must have had a rush of “ pickled cucumber ” to
the brain, for he wrote “ Martini and Martini, £100.” The
mug took the ticket and “never said a word.” When
the backer got a “ leg in ” with Martini, Mossy met him
and asked if he wanted to lay off First Demon. The
backer “ Never said a word, s’elp me Moses !” When the
Cup winner weighed in, Mossy was politely but firmly
asked for £lOO. Mossy replied—! I Showed his book.
Mug showed his ticket. Mossy swore like the army in
Flanders. Wouldn’t pay—it was a swindle.
Mug complained to Byron Moore. Mossy appeared ami
showed his book wherein he had entered the wager—Mar-
tini and First Demon. B.M. said : “Is that your writing
on the ticket, ‘Martini and Martini,’ Mr. Isaacs?” "Yes,”
cried Mossy, “ but it’s a mistake. I”—!—! PoorMns-y
raved and explained, but was only met with, “ Is tin.l
yourticket?” “Yes.” “Then pay!” Mossy paid. When
the mug had pocketed the century he soothed M>s<y
with—“ You know, Mossy, it First Demon had wo i
the Cup I should have claimed the wager in your book mil
lost the ticket.” The writer, as one of Mossy’s unfbi in-
nate clients, took an unholy delight in introducing people
to him with the sole object of leading him to relate bow
he was got at for a hundred. To see Mossy push back his
ancient pith helmet and relate this story with big teais in
his sad blue eyes was joj r to a backer. He invariably
finished with—“ The artfulness of the ! When 1
asked him to lay off First Demon w’ith me he never said a
word 1 ” Once there was a backer who struck Mossy for
a £2O double that Mossy picked for him—but that s
another story.—Yours, A. W.F.

Dear Bulletin, —Most rowing men admit that
the success of Victorian eight-oar crews depends much
upon the coach—George Upward. George to day literally
permeates the rowing-world of Victoria, and bestrides it
like a Colossus. He is responsible for the healthy spirit
which accepts every man as an “amateur” who rows for
the love of the sport. The manual-labourer has no im-
pediment placed in his way, and takes his chance of
selection with the bank-clerk and the civil-servant ; know-
ing that his rowing form will be the test of success, and
not the cut of his clothes, the society he moves in, or his
father’s bank-account. Were it so in N.S. Wales, the
results of these contests might be different. Upward
is also somewhat responsible for the improvements
in boats which have made Victorian boat-buildeis
invincible. I saw the other day in an English paper
that a distinguished naval-desigrter had suggested
that the seats of the Oxford and Cambridge eight-
oar boat should all be placed exactly in the centre,
and not one and one as now. This brilliant idea got into
the distinguished designer’s nut a little too late, for the
Melb. builders adopted a precisely similar suggestion
from Upward, some considerable time ago, and have long
been building their best boats on that principle. Upward
has also encouraged country clubs, and country rowers
are often included in intercolonial crews. The stroke of
the last Victorian crew was from the provinces—from
Ballarat, I think. Upward also believes that we have the
best boat-builders in the world, declares Peter Sullivan,
of Sydney, able to build wager-boats to row for a king-
dom ; asseverates that Australian timber is the best
material for best boats ; and only admits with reluctance
that Nowegian timber is indispensable for oars.
As a coach he is facile pnneeps, his great
strength being in raising the moral tone of the
whole crew, never admonishing an erring rower before
his brethren, as he is of opinion that it weakens tfie men
by superinducing a belief amongst seven that the eighth
is a passenger. When other sports are degenerating—
racing undoubtedly; cricket partially (remember the
business of the theatre-pass trophy); and football emphati-
cally—it is refreshing to find that the torch of real sport
for its own sake has been kept alight in rowing. Much
credit for this is due to the benevolent despotism of
George Upward, one of the straightest of Australian
sportsmen, whose natural modesty and repugnance to
self-advertisement will receive a rude shock when he reads
this.—Yours, &c., Cornstalk.
N.S. Wales understands eight-oar rowing.

The last N.S.W. intercolonial eight-oar crew
was steered by a one-armed lad, Charley Hellings, who
has a patent apparatus which enables him to steer with
one hand. After all, the brilliant coxswain steers mostly
with his head.
* * * «
Bogardus and Carver’s shooting has always been
looked upon as phenomenal, but during the trial of Herr
Dowe’s bullet-proof coat, in the Winter-garten at Berlin,
two experts gave some terrifying examples of their skill.
One of them screwed a rifle fast, and pointed it at an egg
suspended about 40 paces off. He then went and placed
his head under the egg and made the rifle go off by firing
at the trigger with another rifle which he held in his hand.
The same man was then placed in a wooden frame which
fitted close to his body, the edges being hung with plaster
eggs. His companion then fired with a magazine rifle at
the eggs which hung close to hiseyes, cheeks, &c. Finally,
he shot an egg off the other’s head at a distance of 100
paces, and then two eggs at the same time with two
revolvers!
• ♦ * «
Dry-goods Butler, of Melb., winner of the Q.
Deposit Bank buildings, gave his tenants a dinner the
other day in Brisbane. He said that the Y.M.O.A. had
asked him straight out for his big win.
* ♦ • *
The S.A. T.C.’s commission (7i per cent.) on
the totalisator receipts for the Adelaide Cup meeting
exceeded £3OOO. With the machine legalised at Fleming-
ton the Y.R.C. would speedily be able to restore the faded
glories of their Melb. Cup and increase stakes all round,
but inasmuch as the innovation would abolish the horse-
milking trade and encourage sport instead of chicanery,
the alleged sportsmen of Victoria are arrayed against it.
Some of the owners who were loudest in their outcry
against the Club’s necessary reduction of stakes are also
the determined supporters of “ books ’’ v. totalisator. It
would be interesting to get these gentlemen in a corner
and hear them attempt to reconcile their two arguments.

allowed two guesses for each 1/- sent by Postal or P.O.
Order, 1/1 if in stamps. No coupons necessary. Add ex-
change to Postal notes other colonies. Total amount (less
10 per cent.) divided amongst successful guessers.

with Mr. T. F. Hughes, the well-known Albury Bookseller,
and opened in his presence.

LiThe matter of the application of Lucy Ive, mortgagee under memorandum of mortgage from Alexis-
Greig Mackenzie, No. 214,303, dated 24th July, 1893, over the land comprised in Certificate ot
WHEREAS 1 the’abovenamed mortgagee has made application for an order of foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised in the said mortgage as hereunder particularised, is offered and will rema n
open for sale until the 29th June, 1894, which date has been appointed as the time upon or after which
I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicant an order for foreclosure, which will exclude all
claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any other person claiming through or under him, unless
in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy the principal
and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and interest up to
the time of such sale.

LEASES FOR OYSTER CULTURE,
It is hereby notified for general information that the undermentioned person has applied to lease
for Ovster Culture the portion of land set opposite his name in the annexed schedule.
Tracings showing the positions of the portions enumerated may be inspected at this Department
daily, excepting Saturdays, between 11 and 3 o’clock, and on Saturdays between 11 and 12 o clock.
Any person may, by memorial to the Commissioners of Fisheries, within thirty days from the
date of this notice, and on grounds to be stated in such memorial, pray that leases of the portions may
not be granted. LINDSAY THOMPSON,
