An offer to back Steve Farrell, the Yankee ped.,
Who has won several Sheffield handicaps, against any
sprinter in the world, should find ready response in Aus-
tralia, if our peds are as good as credited, whioh is
doubtful.
* • • ♦
A sculling revival looms. Gaudaur now chal-
lenges any man in the world ; but, defeated by Bill Beach
eight years ago, how could he Mve beside a man of Stan-
bury’s class?
• • • *
One Sydney club intends to enforce a law by
which bookmakers would deposit a guarantee before
bCTng allowed to bet. If introduced, it would become
universal and squelch the welsher.
• • • ♦
In Australasia, the amateur peds. are not half
Bo “amateurish" as the timekeepers who credit them
with big records. On paper, Hempton, the Maorilander,
was faster than Wally Williams, the Sydney pro., yet,
when they trained together at Botany, it was evident
that, each at his best, the pro. could give the amateur
syds. in 120.
* • • *
The starting-machine is, taken all round, a
success. For one thing, it will not fine jockey-boys for
“ langwidge,” and then upbraid them in bullookdriverese.
* * * *
Corbett now tries to evade Jackson on the
ground of business theatrical engagements. Well, Cor-
bett’s business is prize-fighting, not acting. If it isn t,
then let him retire and enter the lists against Irving and
Jefferson—as an actor.
* * * ♦
Parson Davis aphorises: “ Sport recognises
neither colour nor country. Patriotism is all right in its
way, but it cannot be dragged into prize-fighting. Wrap-
ping himself in the Stars and Stripes is all pappyoock.
If he is licked, not a star will drop out of the American
flag. Corbett cannot shield himself with this patriotic
bunkum.”
» • ♦ ♦
W. Thorn, Sydney, is now nearly Australia’s
champion ped. He lately won a handicap, running
94jyds. in 9 3-ssec. He was getting a yard and a-quarter
from Larry Marsh, but won by more than his start.
* * * *
A movement is on foot to establish an amateur
athletes’ club-house in Sydney.
* * * »
Clever little Abe Willis was knocked out in
five rounds by Hegarty, in Melbourne, last week. For
four rounds Willis had all the best of a tapping-match,
but when it came to solid slogging in the fifth, Willis’
much-abused constitution played him false, and he was
unable to rally from a punch on the jaw. It was ever
thus. Athletes knock themselves about for years, and
expect to come to the scratch sound after three or four
weeks “ training.’’
♦ • * •
At Melb. 24 hours’ cycling contest all the
sprinters of one, two, and even five miles, were left far in
the rear ; Arthur Turner, however, apparently went for
the hundred miles record, whioh he took, and did not
trouble about finishing. Beeson, who came second, was
not admitted by the management as a competitor in the
original list, not being considered good enough, and only
got in by some others scratching themselves. Chrisp, the
winner, put uo 401 4-5 miles under unfavourable condi-
tions, as against the English record of 426 J with every-
thing suitable.
* • » •
When the “Samuel Hordern,” the boat rowed
by the N.S. Welshmen in Victoria, was landed on
the Sydney wharf, a horse danced a fandango on it,
reducing it to matchwood. Bowing men think there was
a deal of horse sense about that animal.

GIRLS OF THE RIGHT SORT.
“ My daughter, unless you can work the ship off the
coast, she will soon strike the rocks, and we shall all
be lost.”
So said the captain of a fine merchant vessel to
his daughter. He was right; it was their only
chance. The bark Anhui, 701) tons, was bound
from Cuxhaven to Rio w ith a general cargo. She
had scarcely left port when the captain was
disabled by a broken leg. A mutiny followed.
Under threat of bad weather the Anina anchored
in a bight of a bay on the dangerous coast of Corn-
wall. Here the officers and all of the crew deserted.
A furious cyclonic south-west gale arose, The
anchors dragged, and the, girl burned a flare on
deck. The lifeboat responded, but was staved
against the ship’s side by a sea. _ All the boat’s
■crew were lost except the coxswain, who gained
•the deck. He was not a sailor, yet, with him
alone under her orders, this girl, who was a sailor,
■cut both cables, set some headsail, and got out
■into the open. It was touch and go, but true grit
won. Three weeks longer the girl commanded
before help came. Yet it did come finally, and
■sodidthe wedding of the handsome youngcoxswain
and the captain’s beautiful and heroic daughter.
And yet there are some fools left who say we
must look to men chiefly for courage and intelli-
gence. Stuff and nonsense! Any woman will
scream when she sees a mouse (that’s mere nerves),
and ten minutes later she will meet disaster or
■death with a quiet smile. Then, too, women
have a genius for throwing in a suggestion exactly
when it is wanted.
A man writes this way:—“l came home de-
jected,” so he goes on, “ and didn’t know what to
■do ; but my daughter said ”
But wait a minute. Before we hear what his
■daughter said, let’s have his story from the start,
■shipshape and Bristol fashion. He says : —“ In
December, 1890, I was suddenly taken one day
with an excruciating pain in the pit of the stomach
and in the right side. For over twelve hours I
could neither sit nor lie down. The medical man
who examined me gave me some medicine, but on
the second day jaundice set in, and from that time
1 suffered from a similar attack about once every
three weeks. Every remedy was tried without
avail; nothing did the slightest good. Thekidney
secretion was something frightful, being a mass of
matter, blood, and bile.
“ This continued five months, and I grew weaker
and thinner every day. My friends thought
nothing could save me. Many urged me to have
further advice, as at this time the secretions were
much worse, and the motions resembled white
clay. Another attack came on, and as I was
daily getting worse, I said, ‘ I will see the doctor
first, and if he can do me no good, I will seek
further medical help.’
“ Accordingly I went to see him, but he was
from home, and would not return until late at
night. I came home dejected and did not know
what to do, but my daughter said, 4 Why don’t you
try Mother SeigeVs Curative Syrup? We hear it
has cured so many. If it does you no good it will
do no harm.’ ‘ Well,’ I said to her, 4 1 will try a
bottle.’
44 1 then began to take it, and oh ! how thankful
I afterwards was, for on the third day I could see
such a change. The secretion, instead of being
nothing but corruption, became clear, and the
motions a healthy colour. From that time I daily
gained health and strength, and in a short time I
was as well as ever in my life, and have had no
return of the disease.
44 1 can, therefore, speak of this medicine in the
highest terms, for, under God’s blessing, it cured
me when nothing else had the slightest effect.”
The above communication is from a business
man of high character in the county of Brecon.
Eor especial reasons he desires his name to be
■withheld for the present, but we freely pledge our
■own reputation for the truth of his statement.
The date is February 12,1892.
The attacks which would probably have soon
ended his life were of severe kidney and liver
congestion, growing out of profound indigestion
and dyspepsia. His system was flooded with bile
acid poisons, and he may thank Heaven for having
a daughter who made the right suggestion at a
critical moment. In courage and good sense she
is like the other noble girl who saved her father’s
ship from wreck while he lay helpless in his cabin.
Success attend them in their own life voyages,
say we.

The London Tory newspapers which print
such paragraphs must be run by madmen :
The appointment of the Earl of Cork to he Master of
the Horse came as a surprise to most people, as it was
generally supposed that the Marquis of Breadalbane
wanted the post. It is one of the best of the ornamental
Household appointments, as the salary of £2500 a year by
no means represents the total emoluments. A royal
carriage and pair and a couple of royal servants are
placed at the Master’s disposal, and whatever riding
horses he may require. There are several other desirable
perquisites, and the duties of the office, are practically
nil, as Sir George Maude, the Crown Equerry, does all the
work. The Master of the Horse has only to attend the
Queen at the Drawing Rooms, and give a state banquet
on her Majesty’s birthday. Lord Cork, who is known as
the best-hatted man in London—nobody has ever been
able to discover the secret of the peculiar gloss which his
tall hats always possess -is a very popular personage in
society, and will be heartily congratulated by a host of
friends.
Which in its effect is surely as treasonable as the
stuffing of Queen Victoria’s mattress with picric-
acid bombs would be.
Chief Secretary Tozer openly declares that
Q. police-magistrates are, with one or two excep-
tions, a wooden-headed lot, who are rapidly
making his hair grey. They were livelier in the
old days when each was practically despotic
sovereign of his territory. One Western stipen-
diary was hastily called once upon a time, to settle
a fight between Kanakas and Chinese. He armed
himself with a heavy hunting-crop, stuffed the
Riot Act into his saddle-pouch, and, after half-an-
hour’s gallop, went into the scrimmage promptly.
When he had “laid out” three Chinamen some-
one suggested to him to “read the Act.” The
P.M. stuttered always, and merely remarked, as
he floored two more Celestials, “ I r-r read it kuk-
kuk-coming al-along the r-r-road.” The trouble
didn’t last long after that; but one of the China-
men had the bad manners to die, and next day the
plantation overseer was run in for killing him.
The P.M. was on the bench, supported by local
J.’sP. After all the witnesses against the overseer
had finished their little stories, “ His Worship ”
descended from the bench and went into the box,
gave his evidence dead in the overseer’s favour,
returned to the bench, refused to commit, and
dismissed defendant without a stain on his
character, blandly observing that be wished to
“ kuk-kuk-complimerit the 1-I-last w-w-witness on
the s-s-straight-fuf-fuf-forward manner in which
he g g-gave h-his tut-tut-tut-testimony.”
“ Mr. Duncan Gillies, the
for Victoria, -visited Govent Garden Market, to-
day, for the purpose of examining various
methods adopted for the sale of fruit.” This
cablegram, although even more extravagantly
ridiculous than it seems, is not as innocent as it
looks. The idea that Gillies stalked round to the
market about mid-day, with a grave air and a
note-book in his pocket, for the purpose of
encouraging the fruit industry in Victoria, is
not correct. Market-morning was the time
selected for Duncan’s visit, which must have been
about as unofficial as they make ’em. Duncan
had been dining, also supping. He was “keeping
it up ” jolly late, was the Agent-General, and
very likely he was singing something he had just
heard at the ’alls, when somebody proposed—one
of the chappies with him, you know—“S pose we
go round to Covent Garden and see them unload-
ing cabbages, by Jove ! ” Gillies thereupon stuck
ids crush hat further back, and bared his crumpled
dress-shirt front to the early breath of the sweet
spring morning, saying, “My oath 1 yes, ’vanning
interests of the colony, good idea.” So the six of
them linked arms and made objects of themselves,
as many thousands of won’t-go’me-till-morning
visitors have done before them, and the market-
gardeners threw radishes at them, and low, vulgar
hawkers butted them with baskets on purpose,
and altogether Gillies and Co. finished up the
night in a sound, traditional manner. Some of the
methods of selling fruit are girls, occasionally
wholesome-looking wenches, and they were the
particular feature of the trade that Duncan
examined most.
The successful removal of Piles of the
most stubborn description is being effected by
Sharp’s new “ Cannalin ” treatment, large quan-
tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.
After half-a-dozen contradictions Hoolan’s
resignation for Burke (N.Q.) electorate has
actually been handed to the Speaker, and the
election comes off on June 16. Glassey didn’t
want to stand, and the Labour-party would much
prefer Hoolan to hang on, but he thinks he has
done enough for the party and must pay attention
to private affairs, which don’t prosper when one is
1000 miles away from the scene of action making
speeches on .£3 a week. Glassey will probably
have to face Lissner, the MTlwraithian Minister-
in-posse who was defeated at the Towers; Dr.
Routh, a N.Q. medico; and James Campbell, an
old Etheridge identity ; with the evergreen Billy
Little for an off-chance. By the way, a letter
from John Plumper Hoolan reached this office the
other day, which defied the united efforts of the
staff to read it until it was turned upside-down,
when it proved to be a new version of the Lord’s
Prayer mixed with curses on politics. Hoolan
was by long odds the worst writer in
Queensland Parliament, and when he sup-
plied mining items to a now-defunct paper
the comps, had to shut their eyes and follow copy
by scent, nosing it out like a dog on a bad trail.
J.P.H. is a decent fellow, but uncommonly hairy,
which gave point to Billy Smyth’s jibe (in the days
before regeneration and “ progressive euchre”)
that he made his first rise by being carried round
in a cage as the “ Wild Man from Borneo,” Any-
how, Hoolan is a loss to the Q. Labour-party ;
and, in spite of his extravagances, The Bulletin
hopes to see him back in Parliament some day.

THE GOLD DIGGER'S WATCH.
IT STANDS SOME VERY ROUGH
USAGE.
AN ALARMING CAMEL ACCIDENT AT
COOLGARDIE.
Mr. William Dunn writes as follows to the
Waterbury Watch Company, Margaret-street,
Sydney, from Crystal Brook, Guyra, New South
Wales : —“ I wish to give you information in refer-
ence to the quick-winding Waterbury Watch which
I purchased at your establishment in 1892. I have
been to the centre of West Australia, prospect-
ing in new country for fifteen months. The watch
was all the time subjected to very rough usage,
but still it kept very accurate time. I was wear-
ing it when A CAMEL BOLTED WITH ME
THROUGH THE MAIN STREET OE COOL-
GARDIE. It was a young camel, and had never
been ridden before. It ran away with me, and
suddenly lay down, the shock being somewhat
similar to a railway collision ; but it never inter-
fered with the watch. I have been over there
* dry blowing’ for alluvial gold. It is one of the
most dirty kinds of work that a watch can be put
to, but it did not interfere in any way with my
Waterbury, and I must say it is A BEAUTIFUL
TIMEKEEPER—indeed, I would never think of
carrying any other. lam well known over there,
being one of the prospectors of the ‘Brilliant Gold
Reef,’2s miles north of Coolgardie. lam also a
well-known New England prospector.”

HERR RASSMUSSEN, PH.D.
A Few Sketches of His Interesting Life and
Work.
Herr Rassmussen, who is known throughout
the world as “The Celebrated Danish Her-
balist,” was born in the little kingdom of Den-
mark, shortly after its last disastrous war with
Germany. His parents being wealthy, and
belonging to the most influential class, he
received a first-class education, and displayed
exceptional talent in the science of botany and
botanical chemistry, and very soon surpassed
everyone of his fellow scholars in those sub-
jects, and eventually made the science of botany
his life-study.
Finding his native country too small, and its
flora too limited to extend his botanical re-
searches, he left for Australia, and landed here
in the beginning of 1879. His first object was
to learn the English language and become
familiar with the customs and habits of his
adopted country, as well as with its botanical
history and medicinal flora. Herr Rassmussen
had quite his share of the troubles and difficul-
ties which usually befall a newcomer in Aus-
tralia ; but he soon overcame every difficulty,
and after five years’ travelling through all the
colonies, investigating every known Australian
medicinal plant, and discovering many new
ones, he eventually settled down permanently
in Sydney in 1883, and then commenced a
most noble mission—that of turning his most
wonderful botanical discoveries to the benefit
and relief of suffering humanity. Herr Rass-
mussen succeeded in this gigantic undertaking
far beyond all expectations, and his name very
soon became a household word, not only in
every Australian camp, village and city, but
also throughout Europe, Africa and America.
Space will not permit to mention the
numerous cures effected by his now world-
famed Alfaline Remedies, suffice it to say
that thousands of original testimonials may be
inspected by anyone at his Head Depot, 547
George Street, Sydney, many of which may
also be seen in his celebrated work, “ The
Natural Doctor.”
There is no doubt whatever that Herr Rass-
mussen deserves all the success and praise which
he has met with, because he is a most scientific,
conscientious and genuine herbalist—his last
ten years record being so wonderful that his
remedies must have cured more human suffering
in that period than is usually effected by a
hundred average doctors in a similar number of
years. He does not hold any consultation
similar to a doctor; Ins whole business is
purely a Proprietary Herbal Remedy Business.
He simply prepares a genuine herbal remedy
specially for the nerves,and another for the blood,
and so on, which can always be relied upon.
He prints on the box containing the remedy its
price and all advice as to doses and diet and
other particulars, and advertises same, which
is the fairest way possible to conduct a business
of this kind. Any information or particulars
desired are given or sent absolutely free of
charge. Pamphlets are sent post free to any
person desiring one, containing all particulars
and many useful and valuable hints on health.
Herr Rassmussen has very large branches
of his extensive business in Melbourne,
Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Wellington (N.Z.),
and throughout the world. He is an
honorary member of several scientific socie-
ties, a gold medalist, diplomee and Hon.
Corr. Mem', of the Academic Parisienne des
Inveuteurs, France ; last year he received the
Honorary University degree of Doctor of
Philosophy ; in 1887, he was presented with a
magnificent gold medal by several of his cured
patrons in Sydney, and also awarded a silver
medal in Denmark, 1888, a bronze medal,
Melbourne, 1893; and in 1891 he issued his
“ Natural Doctor,” a herbal medical work of a
high standard for which he received great
praise from the press, and which is now trans-
lated into many languages.

Mean ?
The real root of the perennial difficulty
between mistress and maid, is the feminine
character. Women are not born to be placed in
authority over, or in subjection to, each other.
1 here is no spirit of fairness in them in their
mutual relations. Men can exercise authority
over their kind—they are just to each other in all
matters, save, perhaps, where a women isconcerned;
but of tew women can as much be said, however
staunch they may be in their love affairs. If
anything can steady them it is une grande passion,
but tnen that itself is as rare as the qualities
which 1 am vaunting. Yes, women are in truth
very different creatures from men, and when they
are so misguided as to attempt to ape them,
instead of remaining on the plane where they have
been placed by Nature as the co-equals, and
neither as the inferiors nor the superiors of the
opposite sex—when they misconceive their posi-
tion to_ that extent the result is usually unfor-
tunate. ” — London Referee.
Dear Bulletin,-— As a loyal man I rejoice
exceedingly to see The Bulletin, last week, pub-
lish verses in honour of the gracious lady who
rules over our destinies. lam a poor man myself,
with a large family, but yet in my humble way
contrived to show my loyalty by reading to my
assembled family the prayers for the preservation
of the Royal Eamily, and as my dear children
were disappointed at ray inability to provide them
with fireworks, I took them to the Rev. Obadiah
Yowl man’s magic- lan tern exhibition, where, after
witnessing interesting pictures of the members of
our ever gracious Queen’s family, we were shown
quite a large picture of the flames of Hell, with
a catherine-wheel accompaniment—much more
pleasing and instructive, you will say, than fire-
works. I don’t know why I write and tell you
this, but I feel so glad after seeing your loyal
poem, last week, that I want everyone to know
how to be happy next 24th May.—Yours, A
Gotha Guelph-Jones.
Dear Bulletin, —When “H. J. M‘Oooey”
talks of “no death-adder attaining, or ever
being likely to attain, a greater length
than 32in.” he displays some of that ignor-
ance he is so ready to attribute to bushmen.
Hanging up in my office is the skin of a death-
adder, shot by me on the Wecan Creek, 20 miles
north from Inverell, which measured 35|in.
before being skinned. To convince Mr. M'Cooay
that every bushman is not “an ignorant blather-
skite,” I will forward the skin to The Bulletin
office, on the following conditions : —lf the skin is
not that of a death-adder, and does not now,
without the head and about 2in. of the tail (the
latter has dropped off) measure 3ft., I will make a
present of the skin to the Museum—it will ba of
some value. If, however, competent authorities
decide in my favour, Mr. M‘Cooey shall pay me
£lO for the skin, and keep it, and withdraw all
the venomous things he has written about bush-
men. Probably there appears a great discrepancy
between an adder 2ft. llfin. and his skin, minus
head and 2in. of tail, measuring 3ft., but Mr.
M‘Gooey’s science will tell him that in pegging to
dry the skin would stretch. I think he will
admit, though, the impossibility of stretching a
death-adder’s skin from 32in. (his limit of growth)
to about 39in. —Yours, J. MTlveen.
Port Louis Merchants’ Gazette, of 12th
March, gives the following account of a hurricane
in the Mauritius, which blew a train over a bridge
and killed a score of people. One of the passengers
jumped out just in time. He writes :
I then looked about and beheld a scene I shall never
forget. One of the middle carriages went over the bridge,
taking down with it the other carriages, save the two
front ones and the half of another which had been broken
in two halves. Those carriages went slowly down, one
after the other. The outcry which then rose was some-
thing terrific. I was in a complete state of amazement
when I saw the carriage in which 1 was a minute before
going with the others down the abyss. I knelt and
thanked God.
This reminds one of the unctuous utterance of a
similar saint on a similar occasion; “My dear
friend Smith nearly upset the boat, but I had the
presence of mind to rap him on the knuckles, and
he sank; so I got home in time for dinner.”
Selfishness is the essence of all religions which
base morality on a system of supernatural rewards
and punishments. The believer who is going to
Heaven cares nothing for the millions of souls to
be tortured in the abyss. “ O Lord, let me get my
little soul in !”
Col. Maude’s new book, “ Memories of
the Mutiny,” is the sort of book to give a hungry
man the feeling that he wasn’t hungry. For
instance:—
The natives were shot down or strung up with as little
compunction as if they were dogs. At Cawnpore, he saw
“fourteen men who had just been hanged upon a single
branch of a large neetn tree. Several of us were looking
up at the victims, and noticing the signs of life that were
still visible in some of them. Suddenly, without any
warning, the neemwood being exceedingly soft and
brittle, the branch gave way and crashed down among
us with its ghastly burden. Sometimes the condemned
men were tied up to a stick fastened across the shafts of
a cart or a gig set on end.” Remonstrating with the
Provost-marshal on this cruel method of strangulation,
the Colonel received the following for reply:—“Well, I
don’t know, sir ; I ain’t heard any complaints !”
The oft-made and contradicted assertion about
blowing mutineers from guns brings out this
breezy little tale :
The saintly Havelock asked him if he knew how to blow
a man from a gun, and receiving a reply in the negative,
rejoined, “ Very well, 1 will send you a man this evening.
. . . The first man led out was a fine-looking Sepoy,
with good features, and a bold, resolute expression. He
begged that he might not be bound, but this could not be
allowed, and I had his wrists tied tightly, each to the
upper part of a wdieel of the gun. Then I depressed the
muzzle until it pointed to the pit of his stomach.” On the
cannon being fired and the smoke clearing away nothing-
was visible but two legs lying in front of the gun ; and
after the lapse of seven or eight seconds down fell his
head which had shot up into the air to the height of pro-
bably 200 feet. Presently the same results occurred in
the case of the second man blown from a gun at Have-
lock’s orders. “These,” writes Colonel Maude, “ w'ere
the only two occasions in which this truly fearful punish-
ment was inflicted by Havelock, although at Meerut,
Delhi, and other places there were many more.”

The circular to teachers which has been
carried by the religious party on the London
School Board, after a great struggle, enjoins the
use of the Bible as a text-book, and suggests the
elucidation of the principles of the Christian reli-
gion, “including a belief in God theEather as
our Creator, in God the Son as our Redeemer, and
in God the Holy Ghost as our Sanctifier.” As
this is pure dogmatism, and the teachers as a body
believe in it as little as any other educated men,
they oppose the circular strongly, but the clerical
party was too well represented on the Board.
Practically, however, it will be a dead letter,
since it is impossible to enforce it, and the debate
will probably lead to a change in the Board’s con-
stitution.

M'Burney, Wallis & Co.’s Report.
M'Burney, Wallis and Co. report:—This being the non-
auction week, we have only private sales as a guide to the
state of the market, and from these we gather that prices
are on a par with auctions held last week. From our
London agents we learn by cable that July series of
London sales opens on the 3rd, September series on the
18th, and November on the 22nd.
We do not look forward to any marked increase in
prices for the July or September series, but have better
hopes for November, if the extensive demand which will
then be taking place in Sydney does not check a London
increase. Last year the Sydney market was about even
with London, and the coining season, we venture to pre-
dict, will see our market well ahead.
On Thursday, the 31st inst., we will bring forward a
very representative catalogue and expect competition
quite up to late rates, or possibly a little better. No
appreciable increase in value should be looked for before
the arrival of the new clip.

* • • «
“ A Sydney Worker ” —who doubtless toils in a
silk hat and expensive imported boots—writes to London
Spectator stating that strikes have caused 99 per cent,
of the present distress in Australia. Neither the writer
nor the stupid paper that publishes his lie has wit to
remember that Australia is not a manufacturing country
like Hingland with a dozen European countries ever
waiting for an opportunity to sneak a bit of its business.
A strike here means a temporary stoppage of production
followed by an extra flow of production which nearly
covers the lost time, and the after-effect of the trouble is
very slight, nor can there possibly be a long after-effect.
The Australian strikes (coal-strikes excepted) of recent
years have injured nobody, as it happened. People
didn’t really lose money thereby. A few tens of
thousands of pounds were prevented from getting into
the banks, where they would have been disposed of in
extra overdraft to men who would assuredly have
swallowed it in the form of foreign champagne. Uncon-
victed crime is what’s the matter with Australia just
now, and the disease threatens to become chronic.
* * * *
Liquidator Ducket read aloud last week a list
of companies whose shares are “ held as security ” by the
Mercantile Bank for advances to the amount of nearly
£300,000. “No one in the room,” said Ducker, “would
now give £5 for the lot.” 48,000 shares in the Victorian
Deposit Bank were pawned with the obliging Mercantile
management; Intercolonial Land Company proprietors
disposed of 31,000 shares in the same way, and so forth.
10,000 chances in the delectable Balfour, Elliott and Co.
arrangements were “ swopped ” for Mercantile cash.
These facts are growing old now, and The Bulletin
desires to mention them with the respect due to age.

It will please Premier Dibbs and certain other
people to know that Dowell, M.P., claims the authorship
of the N.S.W. Bank Notes Act. As a matter of fact, the
real authors of it were the bosses of the Land Co. of Austra-
lasia, who, when they found themselves in a tight place,
whispered the idea into the ear of Colonial Treasurer
M’Millan.

The other day the big man of a Southern bank
departed on a quiet trip abroad. No flourish of news-
paper trumpets proclaimed the event, and only one
friend, it is said, came to see the gentlemen off from the
reconstructed city. At present there are a considerable
number of Southern bank officials travelling on the
leave-of-absence ticket, and perhaps, when the second
crisis cometh, and strange things have to be accounted
for, we shall discover that all the nominally responsible
parties knew nothing about anything because they were
Jar away at the time.

Dear Bulletin,—Forged Sydney tram-tickets!
Um ! the game has been going on for long. The genuine
tickets were so crudely printed that the department itself
could hardly tell the shams from the real. Lots of
outsiders knew all about it. I know one white printer
who had a golden offer years ago to work the thing.
He refused bravely, but he was only one printer after all,
and where one defeats the devil of temptation how many
are only too anxious to succumb ? And, now, it was not
an official or a detective but a mere shopkeeper who
“ narked ” the golden game.—N.P. N.B.—The “ white ”
printer is now in gaol—mainly, for other people’s
swindling. As Ned Kelly said, “ Such are life,”
• • * •
We are gravely informed that the N.S.W.
tramway tickets have a watermark, while those alleged to
he forged have not. The N.S.W. Government watermark
is by no means an absolute precaution, since it is put on
by the private paper firms which succeed in getting con-
tracts. In England, while there is no Government print-
ing-office, the work being done by a few private firms, all
Government paper, from the Bank of England notes to
the Revenue Officers’ rough survey-book, is manufac-
tured in Government mills, under lock and key and
Excise supervision.

Up South recently a “colonial-experience" man
went out to take stock delivery on a bank-encumbered
station. The cattle panned out splendidly, and every day
a fine mob of sleek cattle was yarded, inspected, and
passed. Later on it leaked out that the same mob of
picked cattle had been yarded daily for upwards of a fort-
night.

Dear Bulletin,—“Tirai Piripi” contrasts the
swindling systems of the crooked Jew and the crooked
Scot. The difference between the two is that the former
prefers to do you with your eyes wide open, for the glory
of his outeness is half the pleasure. A Scot, on the other
hand, puts you to sleep with a homily, and then runs the
rule over you. I would rather be done by a Jew than a
Scot, for the former will often enough deal liberally with
you once the transaction is ended, but the latter—not
much,—Wuret M.
One aspect of the Abigail case has been over-
looked. A thousand Investors’ Reviews and howling
Wilsons could not have done one-tenth the injury to Aus-
tralian credit which was done by. the Abigails of Austra-
lian society. When the glorified little cobbler appeared
at functions and spoke on platforms from London to
Belfast, people wondered why so mean and illiterate a
creature could have attained the rank of Cabinet Minister.
The anomaly was put down to honesty, industry, and the
deference due to a self-made man in a new country. But
when the cable burst the honesty bubble, the English
public only remembered the cunning, ignorant schemer
who used his political position to inveigle them into his
cronk pawnshop.
• • • •
The City of Melbourne Building Society’s scheme
for selling its otherwise unsaleable properties to its own
depositors is at last beginning to work. The deposit
receipts are taken at their face value, and as they will
only sell in the open market for 7s. 3d. in the £, the
depositor feels that he has done well; the society trades
off its desirable suburban villas at their face value, and
as it couldn’t get 7s. 3d. in the £ for them in the open
market, it is also satisfied. Also, the depositor saves rent
and gets a watertight roof over his head, and the society
saves interest on deposits, taxes, repairs, worry, com-
mission and other charges. It is a pity the process
can’t be extended indefinitely. If James Beef Patterson
wants to show his backbone for the very last time,
he might do worse than pass an Act whereby every busted
financial institution would be authorised to pay its
depositors, as far as it could, in property, said property
being valued by an independent authority at something
like present prices, and make up the deficiency after-
wards, as much as possible, in calls. It might be rough on
the average depositor to be paid in brick villas and the
like, hut he would get more that way than by the present
process, where half the villas go in the slow and costly
agony of liquidation.
• • • •
People who know the present ruling Victorian
crowd intimately don’t feel especially sanguine about the
proposed State Bank. Even if it does get started, there
are many awkward possibilities about it. It may be used
to provide billets for a lot of fallen bankers who want
billets badly just now, and who would gladly give the
State the benefit of their experience for an adequate con-
sideration. It may come in handy to save sundry recon-
structed banks when the deposits fall due-a perfectly
legitimate proceeding if it makes advances to them on
adequate security, but a ghastly business if it makes ad-
vances on the good old Mercantile basis. If the old boora-
orowd gets hold of it, the State Bank will probably make
advances to any rotten bank without any security at all,
rather than run the risk of more “revelations.” Also,
there is a dismal possibility that the State Bank may
acquire a connection to start with by the simple process
of taking over a few of the cronk banks bodily. Nothing
is absolutely impossible in the present state of Melbourne
commercial morals.
• • • •
Now that the totals are made up, 1893 has been
indeed a gay year with the “private enterprise ” railways
of the U.S.—the country of private enterprise par excel-
lence. During the 12 months 71 railroad companies
burst, representing in all 22,534 miles of line ; and over a
hundred million pounds of capital has gone hopelessly
bung, while liabilities, consisting mainly of £150,000,000
worth of bonds, will realise various sums in the dollar —
how much the average will be is extremely uncertain.
Other obligations, apart from bonds, represent probably
about £30,000,000 more, and will mostly realise very little
in the dollar. During the last ten years 306 U.S. railway
companies have gone bankrupt, with nearly £700,000,000
of liabilities and 67,400 miles of track for assets. This
result has been arrived at—despite enormous State sub-
sidies and all manner of Government assistance —mainly
through frantic competition and wholesale muddlement
and fraud. Altogether “ private enterprise" in railways
looks uglier and more unprofitable every time it is heard
from, and all the eloquence of W. E. Abbott can’t improve
its bony and forbidding aspect.
* * • ♦
The English and Australian Mortgage Bank
(in liquidation, as a matter of course) presents one of
those_ hair-raising balance-sheets to which Melbourne is
growing so much accustomed. In a general way, its
liabilities to depositors, &c., are £850,000, and its assets,
including the calls which may come in, are about the
same. But to get at this result the assets, as the liquida-
tors candidly acknowledge, have been taken at their book
valuation—which is generally about four times the present
realisable value; so when the shareholders have been
squeezed to the uttermost, the prospects look very like
6s. or 6s. in the £.

knows where the money is, and some queer rumours have
from time to time been current re nasty knocks sustained

• * * •
An important point was decided by Q. Supreme
Court, last week, in the mining-case, Plant v. Rolleston.
Judge Harding held that a freehold grant from the Crown
does not carry gold and silver, and that anybody could
go and mine where these metals are. Griffith and
Real have overruled this, and decide that although
the Grown retains the right to metals, the freeholder can
nevertheless keep intruders off his land, whether he mines
himself or no—the same unsatisfactory state of things as
in N.S.W. The case may go to Privy Council.
Editor Bulletin.—Sir, — A paragraph in “The
Wild Cat Column ’’ of your issue dated 26th inst. reflects
seriously on the financial position of the New Zealand
Insurance Co., but the statements, apparently founded on
figures recently circulated by some disappointed and
spiteful person with a desire to injure the company, will
possibly recoil upon his own head. No insurance co. from
its inception could have been more careful than the
N.Z.I. Co. has been in the interests of its shareholders.
When in the early days of its prosperity it could
have paid 50 per cent, dividends out of profits, its
directors wisely limited its dividends to 10 per cent,
and carried forward the balance of profit each year
to enable them to build up a substantial reserve. The
policy of the 00. has always been to move cautiously
and safely and dividends have been paid from
year to year, at all times, out of the year’s profits.
1 enclose a copy of the balance-sheet for ’93, from which
you will see that the profits last year were £53,804135. 6d.
to which was added the amount of balance, £17,898 16s.
lid. carried forward last year, making a total sum of
£71,703 10s. 5d., out of which a div. at the rate of 10 per
cent, with a bonus of 2J per cent, was paid, £lO,OOO
credited to investment fluctuation account, £lO,OOO in re-
duction of company’s freehold premises, £5OOO to city
leasehold sinking fund, and £19,203 10s. sd. was carried
forward to next year’s account. 1 think you will admit
that this is not a bad record for the co., especially when
you consider the times through which New Zealand and
the other provinces have passed. There ennnot be better
evidence of the stability of the co. than the fact that its
shares, which are paid up to £2, are saleable in
New Zealand at 58s.—a premium of nearly 50 per
cent. The New Zealand Insurance 00. is admitted
to be one of the most carefully-managed and stable
companies in Australasia. It is not of mushroom growth,
and its annual profits are larger than the capital and
the whole of the assets of many companies now doing
business, and its promptitude in paying claims is well-
known. The following are its figures, supplied to the
N.Z. Government last year under the Companies Act:—
Assets, £520,843 ss. 7d.; liabilities, £49,010 Is; being
£271,832 17s. 7d. in excess of the paid-up capital of
£200,000. The Co.’s investments are assessed at bed-rock
value, and were never in a more sound position than they
are now. It must, however, expect, as a matter of fact,
that on account of its success many hungry and disap-
pointed offices will adopt any means to gratify their spite
because of their inability to share therein, and will imi-
tate cowards and assassins, who crouch behind stone walls
so that, unseen, they may be enabled to carry out their
purpose. Knowing your readiness at all times to act
justly, I forward this letter for insertion, to remove any
unfavourable impression that may have been made
through the publication of the article referred to.—Yours
faithfully, S. Hague Smith, Manager New Zealand Insur-
ance Company for New South Wales. [The Bulletin
didn’t get its information from any rival office. Also,
The Bulletin’s only said that the oo.’s reserve was
reported to be invested in shares carrying a vast con-
tingent liability in calls.—Ed. B.]
• • • •
The Mount Huxley boom is, no doubt, most
exciting to the people interested, but to outsiders it is
only amusing. Nearly everybody concerned belongs to
the ancient race of Israel, and while the average Hebrew
knows too much to dabble in gold-mining after the
manner of the ordinary Gentile, yet, when he does let
himself go, he is a most enthusiastic and even reckless
speculator. In view of recent market developments there
can be no doubt that the Mount Huxley promoters
solemnly believe every word of the poetical literature
which has been appearing in the disinterested press.
Some of the papers have actually devoted leader-type to
ravishing descriptions of this Yandemonian Golconda.
As for the average mining investor, he shrugs
his shoulders and waits. There were millions in Taran-
ganba, there were also millions in Last Call. There were
also millions in a historical N.S.W. mine, the outward
and visible sign of which, so far as Sydney is oonoerned,
was a pickle-bottle-full of golden stuff—on the strength
whereof the Chosen went simply wild. They were wilder
still when they left off.
Mount Huxley may be good, bad, or indifferent,
but it has been absurdly over-boomed. Tas. Govt.
Geologist has inspected Mount Huxley, and expresses
himself cautiously to the effect that if there is gold it will
pay, and if there isn’t any gold it won’t. Promoter
Samuels’ eloquent statement that “Mount Morgan is
only a backyard to it ” is pardonable as coming from a
gentleman whose line hitherto has been musical instru-
ments, not mines, and who is evidently still very strong
on “ the flute.” Nor does anybody attach any importance
whatsoever to the “expert” opinions of more or less
amateur artists, and so on. The name of Dr. Helms, the
well-known chemist and assayer, has been freely men-
tioned in connection with the mine, in which he is under-
stood to be a big shareholder; but, then, the accomplished
and honest doctor is not a miner, and doesn’t profess to
be; moreover, he has so far said nothing publicly about
the matter.
The Bulletin recommends the Mount Huxley
people, if they want to put their fate to the test, to ask
the Queensland Govt, to let Mr. Jack inspect their mine.
If Mr. J. says Mount Huxley probably contains £140,000,000
worth of gold—an addition, say, of one-ninth to the world’s
present stock of gold, coined and uncoined, the mining
public will put its shirt on the gamble.
Mr. Beardsley, manager of Kennedy and Son’s
smelting works, who examined the property in the inter-
ests of a Melbourne capitalist, says that Mount Huxley is
a good speculation, a “big, poor thing,” not of fabulous
richness; but which, if worked on good, economical lines,
would pay. There must be no waste, and it would not
pay to give £lO,OOO away in purchase-money. He con-
sidered that there would be abundance of water ten
months out of the year, and the lay of the country made
it possible that the King River might be diverted. He
thought the country very similar to Treadwell Island,
Alaska, where the stuff is taken out of an open-face
quarry, at a value of 12s. per ton, keeping 380 stampers
going. Mr. Samuels talks of thousands of stampers, and
is bent on getting out £lOO,OOO worth of gold before
Xmas. He is reported as afraid lest the mine’s colossal
output should depreciate the price of gold. The Bul-
letin sincerely hopes it will.
• * * *
The following are a few hunks from Mr.
Samuel’s speech at Hobart—as reported by the Mercury :
“When the mine was in full swing that statement would
be verified, for he firmly believed that the output of gold
from it would be so tremendous as probably to lower the
standard of the value of the mineral. ... At the foot
of the mountain there was a mass of a sedimentary
pyrites formation, which had been roughly computed to
measure six chains either way, with a depth of not less
than 30ft., and all of which was gold-bearing, although
with the mass of stuff in the mountain itself, it would not
require to be worked for generations to come. (Laughter.)
The assay of the pyrites formation gave the enormous re-
turns of 148 oz. gold per ton of concentrated pyrites, 83oz.
free and 115 oz. chlorinated gold. From measurements
furnished by a mining surveyor there were 9,000,000 cubic
yards of stuff in 60 acres of the mountain, and allowing
that each cubic yard equalled 2 tons weight of stone with
an average yield of sdwt., the result would be £20,000,000.
(Laughter.) Personally, he (Mr. Samuel) was convinced
that the five blocks contained £150,000,000 worth of gold,
and that the discovery on Mount Huxley would, in the
course of five to ten years, depreciate the value of gold.
(Laughter.) He brought down two dishes of rubble
from the mine on his saddle, and took them over
to Sydney to get a fair assay. In the first sample
they got 7dwt. 3gr. of free gold out of what was washed,
and the average was loz. 15dwt. per ton for the dish.
The other dish went loz. 17dwt. per ton. This was from
one side, and the other side was richer. Dividends would
be paid before they could turn round. (Laughter). As
to the prospects of the mine, when he placed it before his
friends they rushed him. The difficulty was not to get
money, but to keep it away and keep fellows out.
(Renewed laughter). The shares on the other side were
£250 each. There were but 10 for Tasmania, but they
would be divided into 100 at £25 each, and he could
assure them, as a sane man, that in 1896, when they had
got to work fully and strongly, every £25 share in this
mine would be worth from £7OO to £BOO a year to the
holder. (Laughter.) He gave them the pledge of a man
of reputation that they would have £lOO,OOO of gold out
of the mine before Christmas. He was returning to
Sydney, and 60 head of stampers would be on the mine as
soon as they could be got there. He would not stop till
they had 1000 or 2000 head of stampers on Mount Huxley.
And that would not make a hole in the mountain. . . .
Talk about Mount Morgan for a million, why it was only a
backyard to this place.” (Loud Laughter.)

Mount Lyell half-yearly meeting in Melb. to-
day (31st). For six months ended March 31, the shoot of
silver-bearing copper ore first met in the south drive of
No. 4 tunnel yielded 284,3280 z. silver and nearly 56 tons
copper, averaging 13030 z. silver and 25.62 per cent, copper
to the ton of stuff. This has raised the balance to credit
of profit and loss to £36,314. It is proposed to issue
£150,000 debentures to build a railway to the mine, and
the Tas. Parliament is expected to guarantee them.

Samuel James, of London, sends us his weekly circular on
Australian mines, dated April 19. In addition to the
circular from the same source we receive a very useful
guide to the gold-fields of Charters Towers. This guide
has a map of the fields, full particulars of each mine,
showing latest dividend paid, and much other useful
information.

In a village north of Townsville lived two bank
managers, a Customs officer, and C.C. (the Chinkie
Chiseller). 0.0.’s balance at bank No. 2 was £400; his
overdraft at bank No. 1, £lOO, with bond warrants on a
large number of opium packages lodged as security. On
several occasions C.C. took cases of opium from bond, but
each time brought two or three back, saying either that
the opium wanted age or that the hieroglyphics betokened
that something was the matter with it. Presently the
bank’s account with C.C. showed an overdraft of £3OO
against 18 bond warrants. Then C.C. withdrew his
balance from bank No. 2 and vamoosed the ranche. The
18 oases turned out to be filled with stones, though the
Customs seals were intact. Anybody who wants to die
has only to whisper “ Opium 1” to the manager of bank
No. L
* • • *
Remarks made at the recent trial of the so-
called Mount Morgan gold robbers, in conjunction with
the Charters Towers case of Plant v. Rolleston at present
proceeding, have struck certain magnates of the Mount
Morgan Gold Mining Co. with deadly terror. In the
Charters Towers case, just mentioned, it is argued on
behalf of plaintiff, that by ancient English law all gold or
gold-bearing quartz in a grant of freehold is vested not in
the grantee but in the Crown. The Mount Morgan Gold
Mining Co., in fearful anticipation of evil to come, is
consequently applying for three leases of 25 acres each on
the site of its present freehold, making a total of 149
acres under leasehold. This leasing (in case of an adverse
decision) will, of course, prevent jumping and legalise
future working, and, also, by a piece of legislative
legerdemain, sanctify the exploitation of the freehold in
the past.

Bayley’s, whose shares have steadied a great
deal, are now running 10-head of stamps, the stone
treated by which “ comes almost exclusively from a heap
of seconds accumulated at grass when only very rich
stuff was being treated by the Panklast.” “ Almost
exclusively ” is good. “The output the first week in-
creased to 600 oz.” (and last week it was 7500 z.). How
much, if any, rich stuff is being added to keep up or
raise the average ? Ten head of stamps should crush
120 tons or so of stone; if so, then Bayley’s seconds
give 6oz. per ton. Wanted—the official interpretation of
“ almost exclusively."
• * * •
Broken Hill South has closed down and applied
for labour suspension. When the much - advertised
sulphide concentrating plant was started several months
ago, it was announced that the salvation of the mine had
come. Afterwards it appeared that, although the plant
was doing everything that had been claimed for it, there
was one drawback —no market could be found for the
product; and, finally, after a few months’ run, it was
closed down while the directors commenced a hunt round
Europe for a market. Meanwhile, it was promised that
the suspension was temporary, and that exploratory work
would be continued. Now the closing of the mine finishes
the game. Shares once sold at over £B.

The latest from the Murchison is that there are
four doctors on the field, and all are down with typhoid.
A large number of patients are not receiving proper
attendance, and heavy mortality is feared.

Coolgardie Govt, water-supply officer dis-
criminates dexterously. Bore-water by itself is undrink-
able. Half-bore, half-fresh, is shandygaff, price 6d. per
gal. But the man who condenses bore-water and sells it
at 4d. has a prior right to supply.

« • * *
Martin Neil, a veteran Russell (N.Q.) pros-
pector, tells a circumstantial George-Washington about
the last big thunderstorm. “ I was making a sluice-race,
when a flash of lightning struck the pick, ran down my
arm, flipped my hat off, knocked me senseless, and when
I came to I found the heel of one boot cut off as clean as
a whistle.” This is the sort of thing that killed mis-
sionary Gribble and makes an indignant Providence take
all the gold out and fill the dirt with big stones.

OKTLY ADDRBSS.
The Slay of the hadji.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The “ Salaamat Jalan ” —the Bon Voyage—had
not on this occasion justified her appellation. The
nor’-easter had been at high jinks ; instead of
pushing the doddering old trader steadily over
her accustomed thousand miles of ocean between
the Aru Islands and Macassar, he had half-a-
dozen times essayed to push her under instead.
The rapid dowsing of her huge mat sails had saved
her then, but, now that they were hoisted to their
highest, the monsoon had played his rarest prank
and refused to blow at all. So here, upon the
eighth day out, the lofty peak of Bonthain, instead
of towering imminent upon the starboard beam,
showed but a shadowy speck above the cobalt sea-
line far ahead.
“ How far ?” asked Cardigan. He was a trader
from the Arus, and was in a hurry. But the
Javani skipper wasn’t. Taking the mountain’s
height as a basis, he deliberately applied a thou-
sand-year-old Chinese formula. Then he answered
in Malay, “Of your miles, about one hundred and
ten.”
The other turned away—stared up at the blind-
ing sky, away over the oily sea. “ Too long !” he
muttered. “ Two days’sail yet, with this infernal
old ramshackle —even if there was a breeze!”
He walked to the little palm-thatched deck-
house, went in, shut the sliding-door behind him,
and lifted from its place a section of the split-
bamboo floor. Between this and the deck was
a 15-inch space—scant lying-room enough for the
man that the trader now looked down upon, with
a puzzled horror on his hard, brown face. The
other, pallid and gasping, looked up with wildly-
staring eyes, and from his mouth came husky
ghosts of words.- The trader, reading the grey
lips, repeated in a whisper the almost soundless
question.'
“Howlong! No longer! Not another hour in
this he broke off, took a brandy-bottle from
a tambox slung to the roof-ridge, and put it to the
other’s mouth.
The man took a gulp that humanised again his
ghoulish eyes; then Cardigan helped him from his
lair and laid him on the sleeping-mat—the ghost
of a straw-haired youth of twenty-two, with a
fluff of coming beard about a mouth mean and cruel.
To an observer, that is; but “ Hard-case-Oar-
digan ” was no observer. He was the typical wild
whiteman of the Archipelago; more generosity
than honesty, and more courage than either!—and
he had, as such strong-hearted fellows will, fallen
in love with this fish-blooded creature that lay on
its back and feebly reviled him.
“My intentions was the best, Tom!” the big
fellow said in a rueful whisper. “Pulled through,
all right, we would, too, only this cursed calm
comes an’ blocks us ! Who the devil’d expect the
monsoon to go slack in July ?”
The other, with the bottle in his hand and some
of the brandy in his voice, snarled like a cornered
cat, “What I want to know——Cardigan
dropped swiftly to his knees, stopped the words
with one brown paw, and pointed with the other
to the bulkhead.
“For your life, you fool!” he hissed into the
young fellow’s ear, “ the skipper may come in
there any minute! Don’t let’s bo dead-’uns sooner
than there’s any help for !”
Then he got up and listened. The captain’s
voice came faintly between the slappings of the
prahu’s “overhang” upon the water, as she
wa liowed on the lazy swell. Out of ear-shot yet !
The trader stooped again and whispered rapidly.
“ S . tafce of th f Cfe j 8 .just this, young man :
iwo hours more n that hole d finish you ! That s
one certainty. Then, if you’re found, you’ll be
krissed, an’me’long with you. ’Nother certainty!
So, quiet as a wooden mouse is your only chance —
an’ a thin one at that!” More gently, then, as
the other’s terror made him let the bottle roll
away; “I’ll save you, Tom, if man can doit!
An’, any case, we won’t go under for nothin’!” He
tapped the revolver-case under his linen jacket.
“ But there’s 40 krisses against us, if there’s one;
an’ the old man’s a reg’lar tiger-cat 1”
And) that very momen t, the “ old man ’’-who,
by bbe way, was a young one—was heard in his
den on the^fcher sid / of th * bu i khead . He wanted
the white m he oalled t and oardigan went .
Jusal handed him a box of cl ’ ieroots> an( f mo tioned
him to sit. “ Serious!” the trader told himself,
and seriouB he found ib for whab the skipper of
the <* S alaamat Ja i a n ” asked him, without pre-
ambl6) waa> if he had heard of the killing of the
bo ly hadji who had been striving to make true
be ii evera G f the accursed heathen of the Arus ?

the so-called white man till the mahogany coun-

But its owner wasn t called a hard case for

Men said at the islands that in some Macassar
hapal-— such as this, let us say the murderer

Down Cardigans spine went a creeping as or

J^ Ppmted to the bulkhead. #
Cardigan s back the ant-procession ceased.
A twitching he had felt before came into pistol-
arm and trigger- finger, and, without moving
head, he noted that of the two he was the nearer
oor ; _
“ J heard thee say ‘Too long! ‘Too long for
what? I asked myself, and followed thee. Thou
didst not hear Jusal glanced at his slippered
but I heard . Also, I saw . He stooped
against the bulkhead, pointed to a tiny hole—
then started round and found the door blocked,
Kris and pistol leaped out together, and for ten
seconds the men were statues. No rush, Jusal
knew, was as quick as a bullet, and call out he
dared not, for he saw in this man’s deadly eye
that his first cry would also be his last.
44 Gouch i ” the white man softljr oalled.
V Round here with you, quick; an bring ’long
that ooil of line overhead I Like lightnm’, now I
an’ nobody’ll see you I ” ,
With a desperate gulp of what remained in.the
bottle Gouoh whipped down the coir-line, darted
from door to door, glanced at the kris, and backed
hastily into the corner.
“ Down with that pig-sticker, now, my little
man ! ” Cardigan said.
English this was, but the skipper understood.
Sulkily he threw down the weapon, and Cardigan
kicked it across the cabin.
“ Now, Tom Gouoh,” he said, “lash this little
spitfire up as tight as a boiler-plate, ’n’ be quick
about it, too! ”
Gouoh made an artistic job of it; then mounted
guard with the pistol while Cardigan took “ a
squint round outside.” Instantly the Javani got
to business. He had sized-up Gouoh and saw a
chance.
“White man,” he said swiftly and eagerly,
“ ye cannot escape! Me ye may kill, but there-
after will the crew kill ye both. Thee —the slayer
of the hadji—they will roast alive ! Understand
thou Malay ? ”
Gouoh was a Penang man, and understood so
well that the pistol nearly fell from his shaking
fingers. “What is it ?” he stammered. “What
seekest thou of me ? ”
“ This! ” and the little man wriggled fiercely in
his bonds. “ Me thou hast not injured, save only
at that black-bearded dog’s bidding. On him
would I be revenged ! Unbind me—and I will
preserve thy life. By the Stone of the Caaba I
swear it! ”
With terror-sharpened judgment Gouoh weighed
the chances —for himself. Of Cardigan’s thousand
kindnesses —his freely ventured life—he never
thought at all. It simply wasn’t in the creature !
In half a minute he decided, put the pistol down,
and was searching for a knife to cut the cords,
when far away across the sweltering ocean arose
the noise of a million wheels upon a mighty bridge.
“Angin besar!” shouted the little captain.
“ A squall! Quick, fool —quick! ”
But then, with night-like darkness and a roar
that smothered out all other sound, the wind was
upon them, and the deck —hove quickly up on
Gouch’s side—flung him heavily to leeward on the
top of Jusal. For nearly a minute the angle was
too great to let him scramble up to starboard,
where the door was. The prahu was fairly beam-
ended, and Jusal commended his soul to Allah.
But then she slowly righted. Gouoh crept out
and was instantly wind-nailed against the house-
side. Hither presently came Cardigan, crawling,
brought out the kris and the forgotten revolver,
and pressed the pistol into Gouoh’s hand. “Do
your best with it!” he shrieked in the other’s ear,
“ an’ I’ll make shift with this !”
But now the sea had got its head up. A dozen
snowy surges flogged them where they stood, then
bodily inboard came a huge “green sea” that
swept the bamboo structure overboard like paper.
Into a tangle of coir-cordage and bamboo spars
the three men rose again. The Javani, hand-and-
foot-bound, came up like a log, went down again
like a stone, me trader, active as a montcey, was
on board in two seconds, looked back, saw Gouoh
between the mainyard and the side, and plunged
in again. The ninety-foot bamboo spar, light
enough in itself, yet took from its enormous length
a powerful inward “ send,” and Cardigan, bracing
his back against the side, fended off the yard with
horizontal legs.
“Now’syour time,Tom I” he panted. “Scramble
up, quick !”
Gouoh did more than scramble—he sprang from
the other’s rigid legs ! The jerk broke Cardigan’s
outward “ thrust” —the spar came resistlessly in—
and in a moment the “hard case” was soft enough!

Through Salayer Strait at sunset stood the
“Salaamat Jalan,” jury-rigged—an easy matter
in craft that are not much more at any tiine. The
monsoon had settled down again to steady work,
and the white-haired old juragan— captain now—
was investigating.
Said he to Gouoh —“And where, then, wast
thou before the squall ? Till then had no man of
us seen thy face.”
“ Thy captain had. With him in his house did
I live. He kept me close, so that the other white
man might not know, because against him was I
to bear witness at Macassar !”
“He who was crushed ? What, then, was he ?”
“He was the slayer of the Hadji!”
ALEX. MONTCOMERY .
“I will only have two children, because
they look so nice on the carriage back-seat.” —
Balzac.
Crushing of the splendid Q. sugar-crop
will begin early this month, and the planters’
organ again emphasises the probable scarcity of
white labour. This is in order to drag the
wretched unemployed up to flood the market and
reduce wages. Whatever white labour seeks em-
ployment in the mongrel-ridden province should
be prepared to organise and stand out for decent
wages, which the industry can well afford to pay.

is quite an extraordinary play, only a little too
melancholy. I once played the leading part my-
self, so that all the ladies wept; only one did not
weep, not even a single tear, and that was the
point of the play, the whole catastrophe.”—
Heine.

Randwick, March 12, 1894.
[REPLY.]
TO THE 400 ELECTORS OF RAND-
WICK, who signed the Requisition to me
to become a Candidate for that Electorate.
Gentlemen, —
I have to thank you most sincerely for youi
Requisition, and for your strong expression of
confidence in me, but I regret to say that I can-
not at present become a candidate for Parlia-
mentary honors. As I take a strong interest in
public affairs, I hope in the near future to be in
a position to place my services at your dis-
posal.
Yours faithfully,
H. OXENHAM,
Normanhurst,
Randwick, May 15, 1894.
“ Some years ago, in Victoria, a ‘ bride
when asked at the altar whether she would have
the man for her husband, replied ‘Nc,’ ‘Well,
what have you come here for ? ’ demanded the
disgusted clergyman. ‘To tell him so,’said the
artless girl.”—Sydney D.T.
Dear Bulletin, —Be your extract from
an interview with Mr. Guy Booth by, it must
not be supposed that “the intrepid Australian
Explorer ” took in anybody but a green reporter
and his equally credulous sub-editor. TheR.G.S.
of London distinctly declined to notice that
gentleman’s alleged exploratory work. I would
undertake to say that any average reporter or so-
called interviewer in Sydney, Melbourne, or any
other of the capitals, could be filled to the brim
with skilfully-told lies about Australia. —D.
P.S.-—Mr. G.B. travelled from Queensland to
Victoria overland.
Minister Suttor, of N.S.W,, firmly believes
that every boy should be taught boxing and should
become an expert with his fists. Yet, according to
a Melb. paper, Mr. Suttor only engaged in one
fight in his life, and then he got the worst
of it, not through lack of pluck, but
because of his hopeless want of science. An-
other N.S.W. politician, however, has an even
worse record. He also only engaged in one scrap
in his life. It arose out of a quarrel in a bush
shanty, and the future politician found himself
rushed into the difficulty unawares. His sup-
porters assured him that, after the language used
by the other party, he must fight, and, despite his
frantic remonstrances, they assisted him off with
most of his clothes and planted him opposite his
half-naked antagonist in a paddock. Then there
was a hurried biting and kicking match and a
cloud of dust arose, out of which the future mem-
ber presently emerged with a scared expression of
countenance, and ran for his life. He took refuge
behind a heap of stones, and when his opponent
ran after him he let fly sundry rocks and
knocked him out. The crowd protested and called
bad names, but the politician threw gibbers
at all and sundry, and being a dead shot presently
routed the whole assemblage. Then he put on his
clothes and went home, and became a rich and
respected citizen.
Dear Bulletin, —Here is a story told me
by a man of culture and refinement and, I believe,
veracity : —“Some time ago I bought, in N.S.W.,
an estate on account of the fine orangery. When
I took it over the trees were in beautiful condi-
tion, but recently they were attacked by soma
disease which was rapidly killing them. Not
knowing exactly what to do, I wrote to the
Department of Agriculture to send up an expert
to examine and advise. Soon afterwards I was
sitting on my verandah, enjoying a smoke, when
up drove, in a sulky, a gentleman I had known in
another country. ‘ Hullo !’ I said, ‘ what brings
you into these parts ? ’ ‘Oh ! I’rn an Inspector of
Agriculture. I’ve come to examine those orange-
trees of yours.’ Wondering what the deuce he
knew about orange-trees and their diseases I in-
vited him inside, while the groom took away the
horse and sulky. We dined and talked over
affairs generally, but not a word about orange-
trees. lEe his billet, I said, ‘I suppose his Nibs
is sticking to you?’ ‘Not he. John Gregory is
my chief backer. You see, my wife has a good
connection that is professionally useful to him, so
the benefit is mutual.’ We continued talking and
sipping whisky till afternoon, when he thought he
would ‘ get.’ So bis trap was brought and I saw
him to the gate of the property, when he suddenly
exclaimed, ‘Ah ! by-the-by, I forgot to look at
those orange-trees of yours ; but I tell you what,
you send me a note of what you consider wrong
with the trees, and I’ll forward a report based
upon that to headquarters.’ ‘I can hardly do
that,’l remarked, ‘since I do not know what is
wrong with them.’ ‘ Well, I’ll fix it up, ’ and with
an au revoir he drove off.f [Sometime after this I
received a letter from ‘ headquarters ’ notifying
me that the expert had reported that the trees
were dying through the roots reaching brackish
water. In reply, I stated that the expert must
have been labouring under some misapprehension
as, firstly, the trees were on a hill 60ft. above any
water; secondly, the younger trees were dying
first, and I thought the oldest trees, having the
longest roots, should be the first affected ; and,
thirdly, that the nearest brackish water was a
quarter of a mile away. Then I got a letter
stating that the Department agreed with the
inspector’s opinion. Then I gave the N.S.W.
Agricultural Department best. ” —Fact.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth thei r
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

Beer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I want no spirits, no whisky, or gin,
With water or seltzer nor even alone }
To change one’s liquor is deadly sin,
A sin that it takes long days to atone :
When the coat of my stomach is worn through
and done,
And bones are brittle and flesh is sere,
1 Remember, friends, if you’re not all gone,
Get me a glass of colonial beer.
0 rich, warm, sparkling liquor, wherein
Fire, air and water are all in one—
With a trifle of mud that it be not too thin,
And a twang of the faintest, to give it a tone
I fear you have made me a sorry drone;
And absence of work gives now no fear,
I want no vittles and work for none :
Get me a glass of colonial beer.
1 care not aught for your English kin,
Nor eke for porter in bottles of stone;
Your fervour oozes itself from my skin,
And has worked its way deep into the bone.
I have tangled my feet while my light has shone,
Shall I tangle them still in that region drear,
Where drinks are seldom and life is lone ?
Get me a glass of colonial beer.
Envoi ;
When I get to heaven I’ll Jay me prone
On my face and pray for this thing most dear,
Pray like to the Lord on his throne
To get me a glass of colonial beer.
HREMES.
Problem : Whether a buckjumper can get
the saddle off without the girths being loosed.
“The Spirit of the World, beholding the
absurdity of men, let a sardonic smile for one
short moment wander o’er his lips. That smile
was Heine." —Matthew Arnold.
The swarm of lawyers increases in Aus-
tralia at a frightful rate. In every suburban
family there is a small boy with a big head whom
his sisters look up to as a future Chief Justice.
And every fresh lawyer is a handicap to justice.
“ We must account as ungenteel and mean
the gains of all hired workmen; we are likewise
to despise all who retail from merchants goods for
prompt sale, for they never can succeed unless
they lie most abominably. All mechanical
labourers are by their profession mean, for a work-
shop can contain nothing befitting a gentleman.”—
Cicero, 700 8.0.
Editor Bulletin, — Lie. the Chinaman “run-
ning amuck,” you are wrong. “ Amuck ” (ahmuk)
is not Malay, but Arabic, and means “mad
drunk.” It is in common use all over Southern
Asia, and is applied to persons drunk with bhang
—a preparation of hempseed. The old-fashioned
Indian Sepoy often used to make himself ahmuk
before going into action. Fact. Bhang was his
substitute for Dutch courage.—J.R.
Undeterred by tales of newspaper “ white
elephants,” Auckland (M.L.) Herald has nearly
completed palatial premises towering high above
other Queen-street structures. The Herald,
however, is founded on piety—which is a rock.
Three-fourths of its space is devoted to accounts
of bun-worries and prayer-meetings, and one of
the proprietors every Sunday rounds up a congre-
gation to his private residence and preaches at
them in the very best hardshell style.
Another “ champion mean man ” is de-
scribed by an eminent Sydney publican, at whose
bar a first-class lunch is provided for customers.
Every day, about one o’clock, a well-dressed
person enters unostentatiously, and skirmishes
round until he gets a chance to seize an empty
pewter-pot off the counter. Then he retires with
the pot to the lunch-table, and eats with cam
persistence for 15 minutes, taking long drinks of
nothing in between times. When he has finished
eating he takes one more satisfying drink out of
his blank pot, wipes his mouth, puts the vessel—
still full of the same old emptiness—on the
counter, and departs full of good food and con-
tentment.
Washington (U.S.) has just had a “spicy ”
trial, involving a well-known social magnate,
Colonel Breckinridge, and a 15-year-old girl named
Pollard. The Woman’s Purity League, of Boston,
adopted a series of resolutions concerning the case,
in which they say : “ This cowardly, silver-haired
monstrosity, old enough to be her grandfather,
deliberately decoys, entraps, and betrays a poor
school-girl.” In closing, the resolutions say :
“ We extend to Madeline Pollard our sympathies.
There are few women to-day who have not done
indiscreet things in their school-girl days.” Which
last sentence (remarks S.F. Argonaut) must
make the husbands of the Purity Leaguers quite
pensive. The court was crowded with sightseers,
mostly women, whose unseemly and morbid curio-
sity so disgusted Judge Bradley that he rebuked
them sternly : “The spectators here have been on
trial for their decency, and they have been proved
guilty of indecent conduct. These people who
come here day after day are like buzzards waiting
for a pile of carrion.”
Dear Bulletin, — Lie first introduction of
Kanakas. In the summer of ’G7-’t>B Messrs.
M‘Lean and Beit, of Westbrook Station, Darling
Downs, obtained from one of Bobby Towns’
island traders a couple of boys, Caraffo and
Johnny. The idea was to break them into shep-
herding, but they were found wanting all along
the line. The M‘Lean in question was Colonial
Treasurer at the time of his death through falling
from his horse while riding with a loose rein. By
the same token the old school of squatters were
mostly ignorant and careless with regard to
horses. Beit, M‘Lean’s partner, capsized a buggy
in which he and his first wife were proceeding
to the station, and she was killed. Said
Beit was a choleric, but just man. The
violent temper was hereditary, teste the his-
torical shindy at “Newlands,” Parramatta River,
when the father of Beit and Governor Sir Charles
Fitzroy exchanged shots over a game of whist.
His Ex. let fly a decanter at the head of his host,
who retaliated with a silver candlestick. Beit, pere,
was a nominally-Christianised German Hebrew,
who first struck Australia’s shores with a cargo of
dolls’ eyes and monkeys on sticks. His descend-
«»ts are now still powerful in the land. —Wurby
