The Parson’s Guest.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I.
Nature had evidently destined Mr. Mason for
the ministry. A small suburban Methodist church
was clearly written in the book of Fate as his
future. He was thin, frail-looking, scantily-
bearded, and the definition of a straight line
exactly described his mind. Naturally, hemarried
an anajmio girl who was equally suited to the cir-
cumstances. She had a great opinion of the
dignity of her position as a minister’s wife, and as
this belief did nobody any harm she was never
undeceived, and the two formed a little mutual-
ad miration-society into which a big-headed baby
was presently admitted.
In common with the rest of his cloth, Mr.
Mason looked upon the preparation of his weekly
sermons as a work entailing a severe mental
strain. When he was engaged in the arduous
task of stringing weary old platitudes together in
the room he called his study, his admiring wife
crept about with hushed footsteps, and enquired
anxiously after his health when he emerged pale
and wearied. It was whilst he was thus busy at
flattening-out the devil that there came a vigorous
peal at the bell, which made Mrs. Mason rush in
alarm to the door lest the noisy summons should
be repeated. A burly, boarded man stood there,
apparently possessing the Masons’ missing share
of vitality. He was well-dressed, but for all that
did not look quite like a “ gentleman.”
“I wish to see the Reverend Mr. Mason,” he
said in a loud and hearty voice.
“ Is your visit important ?” enquired Mrs
Mason, “ because Mr. Mason is just now preparing
his sermon, and I do not like to disturb him.”
“ I am leaving town to-day, so cannot come
back again,” returned the visitor. “I have a
message from his cousin.”
“ From his cousin ?”
“Yes, I have just come from Queensland and
am going on to Melbourne to-night.”
Mrs. Mason asked the stranger to enter and
went and called her husband.
“ You have a cousin called James Parker ?” said
the man when the parson came in.
“I have relations of that name, but lam not
personally acquainted with them.”
“That’s quite right. Jim said you did not
know each other. He heard you were here and
asked me to call and tell you that he would be
down himself shortly.”
The little clergyman murmured something about
the pleasure he would feel at meeting his relative.
His visitor hesitated and glanced so meaningly at
Mrs. Mason that that innocent-minded matron
blushed and felt quite uncomfortable.
“ It is rather a delicate business,” he com-
menced after a pause, “ but Jim was desirous I
should make matters plain before he came. The
truth is that he is desirous of stopping with you
during his visit. He is well-off,” he continued
hastily, seeing a change pass over the countenance
of both man and wife, “ and is willing to pay
handsomely for his accommodation; but the
trouble is this —Jim boozes a good deal.”
“ Boozes ? ”
“ Yes, drinks. Gets the horrors. I’ve nursed
him myself through two or three attacks. Jim
has always got some fad or other, and he thought
if he could stay with you a bit it might do him
good. Help to cure him of his weakness for
drink. If you think you can manage it I wish
you would let me know.”
The parson and his wife reflected. A cousin
with money who wanted to be converted ! “ I
think we can oblige him,’ said Mr. Mason at last;
“ in fact, it is our clear duty to do so.”
Jim’s friend seemed relieved. “Now, I’ll just
give you the run of the ropes,” he said in a more
easy and colloquial manner.
“ Is Mr, Parker married ? ” interrupted Mrs,
Mason.
“ Well, not exactly. He’s had his little
entanglements, but I don’t think he ever quite
got married.” Mrs. Mason got very red and
wished she had not spoken, and their visitor pro-
ceeded.
“ Jim is sure to break out once or twice before
he gives it up, and I’ll tell you just how to work
him. When Jim starts he always loses his legs,”
“Loses his legs?” repeated Mr. Mason as
though he thought the limbs in question were
detachable.
“ Yes ; there’s where you have the pull on him.
Put him to bed, and he’ll stop there so long as
you keep him supplied with liquor—if not, he’ll
crawl out on his hands and knees after it. When
the fit is going off he’ll begin to talk. First of all
he’ll begin heaving things at you.”
“But,” expostulated Mason, “I thought you
said he would be quiet.”
“ I don’t mean hard things, boots or brushes, or
anything; but he got religious once, and he’ll
think you’re a sinner, and want to heave texts and
sermons and lectures at yeu, especially
drink, then he’ll backslide.”
Mrs. Mason started.
“ Commence driving bullocks—he was bull
punching for four years. You never heard a man
driving bullocks ? Well, Jim could do it properly,
so you’ll have a treat. Then he’ll start swearing at
Kanakas. He was plantation-boss once. Kanakas
take a lot of swearing-at, and Jim was an artist at
it. Next he’ll make love. That’s always the end
of it. He goes the same round every time ’’—check-
ing them off on his fingers—“religion, bullock-
driving, nigger-driving and love-making. But
he’s a good-hearted fellow, and you’ll like him.”
The stranger rose. “I’ll wire Jim that you’re
agreeable, and you may expect him at any time.”
“I did not quite catch your name? ” said the
clergyman, interrogatively; but the man from
Queensland only bowed and departed, without
satisfying their curiosity.
11.
Mr. and Mrs. Mason held many a consultation
over their expected guest, and, once over the first
shock, began to look at the matter as a special
favour of Providence. He set to work to pre-
pare earnest discourses on the sin of intemperance,
and she got ready the spare-room, and hung appro-
priate texts around the walls.
In due course the unknown cousin arrived. He
was younger than his friend whom, however, he
somewhat resembled both in appearance and voice.
He was to all outward seeming clothed and in his
right mind, and poor little Mrs. Mason gradually
calmed down her excited nerves as she noticed
nothing unusual in his manner. His financial
arrangements with Mr. Mason were eminently
satisfactory, and the worthy couple felt that they
had struck a streak of good luck. Acting on the
hint conveyed , by Jim’s friend, the Masons,
although a most abstemious couple, had got in a
small supply of wine and a bottle or so of whisky.
But Parker resolutely declined it and even looked
stern disapproval when the clergyman himself
indulged in a very small glass, by way of
hospitality. One evening, much to the amaze-
ment of husband and wife, he completely took the
wind out of the parson’s sails by reading him a
lecture on the evils of moderate drinking.
“Don’t, my dear fellow,” he concluded impres-
sively ; “look at the bad example you set others.”
The good couple sat in affrighted silence. “ It’s
coming,” was their common thought, “ He must
have been drinking outside.” They glanced
anxiously at their cousin’s legs, which, however,
showed no weakness at present.
When Mr. Parker went to his room that night
he found a bottle of whisky left ostentatiously on
his dressing-table. Next morning the empty
bottle was found lying in the garden, and Mr.
Parker left the house after breakfast in a very bad
temper.
Late that night the clergyman, who had retired
to rest, heard him let himself in with his latch-
key. He rose and looked out to make sure that
everything was right, and by the light of the
candle left in the hall, tor the visitor’s benefit, saw
a singular sight. His cousin was evidently in a
cheerful humour, so cheerful that he could not for
an instant cease laughing. He was very unsteady,
but managed to get to his room, chuckling all the
time ; and the uneasy little parson heard unholy
merriment proceeding from his chamber at inter-
vals all night.
In the morning, Mason cautiously peeped into
the room. The whisky bottle had been emptied,
and his cousin was asleep, breathing heavily. His
face was flushed, he was only half-undressed, and
looked such a different individual from the sober,
serious, proper inmate he had been accustomed to,
that he scarcely knew him. He left some more
liquor in the room, and crept noiselessly out.
That was an awful day. Parker drove bullocks
all the morning in a stentorian voice, which re-
sounded through the small weatherboard house
like blasphemous thunder. Mrs. Mason and the
maid had to go about with wool in their ears.
Visitors who called, serious people, were greeted
with profane objurgations directed against
“Smiler,” “ Rattler ’’ and “Boxer,” also against
Brandy, Strawberry and Sherry. In the after-
noon he cursed Kanakas in several heathen
languages, but unfortunately mostly in English.
He was still in full blast when a dark-com-
plexioned lady of masculine figure came to the
door and enquired for Mr. Parker. Mrs, Mason
told the enquirer that Mr. Parker was too unwell
to see anybody. At that moment a volley of
abuse came floating down the stairs. The lady
seemed to recognise the voice, for she smiled
“ It’s all right,” she said ; “ you see, he gave me
this address, and it seemed so queer his stopning
here that I called to make sure he wasn’t coming
the double over me. He owes me a bit of money”
However, it’s all right. I’ll call again.” And she
“Good gracious!” thought poor Mrs. Mason
as an idea struck her. “ That must be one of
entanglements.”
That evening an elderly old maid came to see
Mrs. Mason. Fortunately, the patient was quiet
and the two ladies were enjoying a little scandal
when the servant opened the front-door, there was
a step in the passage, and in came Mr. Parker
Poor Mrs. Mason’s nerve gave way completely
“ Henry ! Henry 1” she screamed, “ He’s come to
make love to us.”
At these awful words the old maid went into
shrieking hysterics, and the wretched clergyman
rushed in distractedly from his study.
There stood Mr. Parker sober and collected
“I presume this is some trick of that wretched
brother of mine?” he said severely.
Mason looked at him and the truth dawned on
him as a burst of insane merriment came from
overhead. “Is that your brother upstairs?’’he
asked.
“ I expect so; did you think it was me ?”
“ I could not help it. You see, his face was all
flushed and queer-looking, and he is very much
like you.”
“ Now, what has he been up to?”
Mason recounted the particulars of the first
visit.
“Of course. That’s his idea of a joke. He
simply described himself instead of me. I cer-
tainly asked him to make arrangements with you
for my stopping here, and he wrote that he had
done so but that I must keep an eye upon you as
you were addicted to the vice of secret drinking.”
Mr. Mason groaned. “Now I am a rigid teeto-
taller, and was much annoyed to find you put a
bottle of whisky in my room the other night
which I promptly emptied out of the window, f
saw my brother in town yesterday morning and
could see that he had been drinking, and lectured
him severely. He did nothing but laugh, and
I suppose managed to steal the latch-key out of
the pocket of my overcoat. I had to go up the
line on business yesterday and was detained all
night.
Although the bad language overheard in the par-
son’s house created some talk, the thing soon blew
over, and the couple had no cause to regret know-
ing their new relatives. But the old maid has
never forgiven Mrs. Mason to this day.
